Mishneh Torah, Shofar, Sukkah and Lulav
משנה תורה, הלכות שופר וסוכה ולולב
Mishneh Torah, trans. by Eliyahu Touger. Jerusalem, Moznaim Pub. c1986-c2007
https://www.nli.org.il/he/books/NNL_ALEPH001020101/NLI

Mishneh Torah, Shofar, Sukkah and Lulav



Chapter 1

It is a positive commandment from the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer Hamitzvot</i> (Positive Commandment 170) and <i>Sefer Hachinuch</i> (Mitzvah 405) count this mitzvah as one of the 613 mitzvot of the Torah.</i> to hear the sounding of the shofar<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The mitzvah is not the blowing of the shofar, as might be inferred from the verse, but rather listening to the blowing. The blessing recited before the fulfillment of this mitzvah, praising God for commanding us "to listen to the sounding of the shofar (Chapter 3, Halachah 10)," emphasizes this principle.<br>Accordingly, the Rambam writes (Chapter 1, Halachah 8) that a person who blows a shofar without hearing it does not fulfill the mitzvah. Conversely, he writes (Responsum 78) that if the mitzvah were the blowing of the shofar, a person who heard it being blown, but did not blow it himself, would not fulfill his obligation.<br>It must be noted that the published text of the siddur of Rav Amram Gaon states that the mitzvah is "to sound the shofar." Also, concerning the blowing of the shofar in the <i>yovel</i>, the Rambam himself writes that: "it is a positive mitzvah to blow the shofar on the tenth of Tishre... (<i>Hilchot Shemitah V'Yovel</i> 10:10)."</i> on Rosh HaShanah, as [Numbers 29:1] states: "It shall be a day of sounding [the ram's horn] for you."<br>The<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Though the ram's horn is not explicitly mentioned in the verse, our Sages derived the requirement as the Rambam explains.</i> shofar, which is sounded both on Rosh HaShanah and for the <i>yovel</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">to announce the freeing of the slaves and the return of property, as explained in Leviticus 25:9-13.</i> is a bent<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Rams' horns are always bent. This, too, has homiletic significance, referring to the bending over of our proud hearts. (See <i>Rosh HaShanah</i> 26b.)</i> ram's horn.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rosh HaShanah</i> 16a states that a ram's horn is used to recall the <i>akedah</i> (binding) of Isaac.</i> All shofarot other than that of a ram are unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's opinion is based on the statement of Rav Levi (<i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 26b), who declares: "The mitzvah of Rosh Hashanah is to be performed with bent [shofarot]," implying the use of a sheep's or ram's horn for that is their natural shape.<br>The Ra'avad, Rabbenu Asher, and many other Rishonim maintain that Rav Levi desired to designate the type of shofar which is most preferable to use, but did not intend to disqualify the horns of other animals. Their view is accepted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 586:1), which states that it is desirable to use the horn of a ram. However, if that is not possible, the horn of any animal may be used. The only exceptions are the horns of a cow and some wild animals whose horns are single, solid entities (Ramban).</i><br>Even though the sounding of the shofar on Rosh HaShanah<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The above verse merely mentions יום תרועה - "a day of sounding" - without stating what must be sounded.</i> is not explicitly mentioned in the Torah [it was derived by our Sages in the following manner]. Concerning the <i>yovel</i>, [Leviticus 25:9] states: "You shall make a proclamation, sounding the shofar... you shall proclaim with the shofar." The oral tradition<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 33b</i> explains that just as the "sounding" required by the Torah in the <i>yovel</i> requires a shofar, so, too, the "sounding" on Rosh HaShanah requires a shofar.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">An analogy (גזרה שוה) is drawn between the two verses, to teach that the same type of "sounding" is required on both occasions. Thus, since the Torah specifies states that the "sounding" of the <i>yovel</i> is carried out with a shofar, that same instrument is used on Rosh HaShanah.</i>
In the Temple,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">As will be explained in the commentary to Chapter 2, Halachah 8, and Chapter 7, Halachah 13, in certain contexts the Rambam interprets the expression במקדש - literally, "in the holy place" - as referring to the entire city of Jerusalem. (See also the Rambam's commentary to the mishnah, <i>Rosh HaShanah</i> 4:1.) However, in the present context, it refers to the Temple alone. Thus, <i>Rosh HaShanah</i> 27a states that the shofar was sounded in this manner only "on the Temple Mount and at the eastern gate."</i> on Rosh HaShanah, they would blow [the shofar in the following manner]: There was one shofar and two trumpets, [one on either] side.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The verse from Psalms mentions "the voice of the shofar," using the singular. In contrast, it refers to "trumpets."</i> The sounding of the shofar was extended,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">for greater emphasis</i> while that of the trumpets was shortened, because the mitzvah of the day is performed with the shofar.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rosh HaShanah</i> 26b contrasts the sounding of the shofar and trumpets on Rosh HaShanah with their being blown on a fast day declared because of unfavorable conditions. On the latter occasion, the sounding of the trumpets was emphasized in keeping with the instructions of Numbers 10:9.</i><br>Why were the trumpets sounded together with it? Because [Psalms 98:6] states: "You shall sound trumpets and the voice of the shofar before God, the King."<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 27a states that the expression "before God" implies: in the Temple.</i> However, in other places<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">even within Jerusalem</i> on Rosh Hashanah, only the shofar is blown.
At the outset, we should not blow a shofar of idol worship.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">For it is not appropriate to use such a shofar for a mitzvah.</i> However, if one sounded it, one has fulfilled his obligation.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> explains that this refers to a shofar belonging to a gentile that was used in the worship of idols, or a shofar which was itself worshiped as an idol. However, as will be explained, if the shofar of idol worship belonged to a Jew or if it was made from the horn of an animal that was sacrificed to an idol, even after the fact, its use is not acceptable.</i> [In contrast,] should one sound a shofar belonging to an apostate city,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">עיר הנדחת. Deuteronomy 13:13-19 describes the laws governing a city in which the majority of the inhabitants have turned to idol worship. All the idolaters must be slain and all the homes and property burned. No benefit may be derived from them. (See also <i>Hilchot Avodat Kochavim</i>, Chapter 4.)</i> one has not fulfilled one's obligation.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 28a explains that the difference between a shofar of idol worship and one of an apostate city is that all the property within the latter must be destroyed. Since ultimately this shofar must be burnt, even before it is actually destroyed it is no longer considered to be an existent entity. Thus, we may not use it on Rosh HaShanah, because a shofar used for the mitzvah must be of a specific size - slightly larger than one's hand, as mentioned in Halachah 5 - and a nonexistent entity has no size at all.<br>A shofar of idol worship must also be destroyed. However, should the gentile negate its connection to idol worship before it is acquired by a Jew, it need not be destroyed, as stated in <i>Hilchot Avodat Kochavim</i> 8:8. Since there is a possibility that it will not have to be destroyed, it is considered to be an existent entity and may be used for the mitzvah.<br>This principle applies only to aspects of idol worship belonging to a gentile. It is impossible to negate the connection between an idol worshiped by a Jew and its forbidden nature. (See <i>Hilchot Avodat Kochavim</i> 8:9.) Hence, a Jew's shofar of idol worship may not be used. Similarly, if a Jew has acquired a shofar of idol worship that belonged to a gentile before the latter negated its connection to idol worship, the Jew is no longer capable of negating this connection. Therefore, such a shofar may not be used on Rosh HaShanah.<br>Also, once an animal has been sacrificed to an idol, there is no possibility of negating its connection to idol worship. Hence, its horn may not be used as a shofar.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 586:3-4) quotes all the above laws as halachah. The Ramah relates that certain authorities recommend not using even a gentile's shofar of idol worship unless we know that he negated its connection with idol worship before Rosh HaShanah begins.</i><br>Concerning a stolen shofar:<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">one who blows it fulfills his obligation - Though in general, a mitzvah performed with a stolen article is not acceptable (see Chapter 8, Halachah 1; <i>Hilchot Chametz U'Matzah</i> 6:7), this case is an exception...</i> one who blows it fulfills his obligation, because the mitzvah is only to listen to the sound,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Hence, a person who listens to the shofar being blown fulfills his obligation</i> even though the listener does not touch [the shofar]<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Based on this statement, the commentaries propose that, according to the Rambam, a person who blows a shofar without putting his lips to it fulfills his obligation. The Ramban (see <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> 1:6) does not accept this view.</i> or lift it up.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Accordingly, after the fact, by hearing such a shofar, one has fulfilled one's obligation, because</i> The laws of theft do not apply to sound alone.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">for sound is not a physical entity that can be possessed. Rabbenu Manoach and the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> cite other examples from <i>Nedarim</i> 13b-15a, where the Sages differentiate between sound and material entities.<br>The Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Sukkah</i> 3:1) derives this law as follows:Everyone agrees that a stolen lulav is unacceptable. What is the difference between a shofar and a lulav?<br>Rabbi Yosse responded: "Concerning a lulav, [Leviticus 23:40] states: 'You shall take for yourself,' implying that only 'your own' is acceptable. In contrast, [Numbers 29:1] states: 'It shall be a day of blowing for you,' implying that regardless [of the nature of the shofar used, the blowing shall be 'for you']".<br>Rabbi Eliezer explained: "There, [concerning the lulav,] one must perform the mitzvah with the lulav itself. Here, [concerning the shofar,] one performs the mitzvah with its sound."Nevertheless, because a sin is associated with this mitzvah, Ashkenazic custom is that no blessing should be recited before blowing such a shofar (<i>Magen Avraham</i> 586:4).</i><br>Similarly, a shofar from an<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">animal consecrated as an...</i> <i>olah</i> offering should not be sounded,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Once an animal is consecrated as an <i>olah</i> offering, no part of its body may be used any other purpose. Therefore, we should not use such a shofar from such an animal for any purpose whatsoever.<br>This halachah applies before the blood from the sacrifice has been offered on the altar. Afterwards, the skin and horns of the animal become the property of the priests and may be used for mundane purposes (Rashi, <i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 28a).</i> but<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Hilchot Me'ilah</i> 5:16, the Rambam writes:Concerning the sound, sight, and smell of consecrated objects: We should not derive benefit from them. However, all the implications of the prohibition against using them for mundane purposes do not apply.</i> if one sounds it, he fulfills his obligation, because the laws of<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">The prohibitions forbidding use of consecrated articles for mundane purposes...</i> מעילה do not apply with regard to sound alone. If you ask: "Behold, he has derived benefit from hearing [the shofar's] sound?"<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Though the laws of מעילה do not apply to sound, there is still a Rabbinic prohibition against benefiting from the sound of consecrated articles. Therefore, one might think that we would be unable to fulfill the mitzvah with such a shofar (<i>Lechem Mishneh</i>). Accordingly, the Rambam explains:</i> - mitzvot were not given for our benefit.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi (<i>Rosh Hashanah</i>, ibid.) states: "the mitzvot were not given to the Jewish people for their enjoyment, but rather as a yoke."<br>This concept has relevance in the ethical, as well as the halachic sphere. In the tenth chapter of <i>Hilchot Teshuvah</i>, the Rambam writes:One who serves [God] out of love occupies himself with the Torah and the mitzvot...for no ulterior motive, not because of fear that evil will occur, nor in order to acquire benefit...<br>The great Sages would command the more understanding and brilliant among their students in private: "Do not be like servants who serve their master for the sake of receiving a gift. Rather, since he is the Master, it is fitting to serve Him"; i.e., serve [Him] out of love.The above is not intended to imply that a person should not feel happy and fulfilled in the service of God. Quite the contrary; indeed, the Rambam concludes these halachot (Chapter 8, Halachah 15) with a description of the importance of happiness in the service of God. However, the intent is that the happiness should be a byproduct and not the goal of the service. We should be totally committed to fulfilling God's will, and the expression of that commitment should generate satisfaction and joy.<br><b>Based on this concept -</b> that mitzvot were not given for our benefit;<br><b>a person who vows not to derive benefit from a shofar may use it to blow the teki'ot required to fulfill the mitzvah. -</b> Doing so is not considered a violation of his vow.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 586:5) advises that the person who took the vow should not blow the shofar himself [because many consider that to be a pleasurable experience (<i>Taz</i>)]. Rather, he should hear the <i>teki'ot</i> from a colleague.<br>Also, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (ibid.) notes that if a person specifically states that he vows not to listen to a colleague's <i>teki'ot</i>, he may not hear that person blow the shofar on Rosh HaShanah, because a vow (<i>neder</i>) can also negate a mitzvah. (See <i>Hilchot Nedarim</i> 3:6-8.)</i><br>Based on this concept, a person who vows not to derive benefit from a shofar may use it to blow the <i>teki'ot</i> required to fulfill the mitzvah.
Regarding a shofar to be used on Rosh HaShanah: It is forbidden to violate the festival laws to obtain it. This applies<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">not only when obtaining the shofar requires the violation of a <i>melachah</i> (forbidden labor) prohibited by the Torah, but</i> even when the forbidden practice is in the category of <i>sh'vut</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Hilchot Shabbat</i> 21:1, the Rambam defines <i>sh'vut</i> as a prohibition instituted by the Sages because a particular activity resembles one forbidden as a <i>melachah</i> by Torah law, or because performing it may cause one to perform a <i>melachah</i>.<br>Though these prohibitions are of Rabbinic origin, the Sages reinforced the power of their decrees and equated them with Torah law. Hence, just as one may not violate a <i>melachah</i> forbidden by the Torah to obtain a shofar, so, too, it is forbidden to violate a <i>sh'vut</i> forbidden by Rabbinic law (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>). [Nevertheless, note the clarification of this principle in Chapter 2, Halachah 6.]</i><br>How is the above exemplified? If there is a shofar in a treetop or across a river<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">and that is the only shofar available - one may not climb the tree - lest one accidentally cut off a branch (ibid., 6)</i> - and that is the only shofar available - one may not climb the tree or swim across the water to bring it.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">lest one prepare a swimming aid (ibid., 23:5). The Mishnah (<i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 32b) mentions other examples of forbidden activities.</i> Needless to say, we may not cut the shofar [from the animal's head]<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">In his commentary on the mishnah, the Rambam explains that this refers to cutting the horn off with a household knife. Since a craftsman's knife is not being used, cutting the shofar off is not forbidden by Torah law. Nevertheless, the Rambam uses the expression "needless to say," because such an activity bears a closer resemblance to one forbidden by Torah law than those mentioned previously.</i> or perform a forbidden labor<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">cutting off the horn with a craftsman's knife</i> [to prepare a shofar so that we may blow it].<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">[to prepare a shofar so that we may blow it].</i><br> [The rationale for the above is:] Blowing the shofar fulfills a positive commandment,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">as stated in Halachah 1.</i> while [the observance of] the festivals fulfills both a positive and a negative commandment.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Hilchot Sh'vitat Yom Tov</i> 1:2, the Rambam writes:Whoever rests from a <i>melachah</i> categorized as "work" on one of [these days] fulfills a positive commandment, because the Torah describes them as "days of rest"... if one performs a <i>melachah</i> that is not intended to prepare food,... one negates the performance of a positive commandment and transgresses a negative commandment, as [Leviticus 23:8] states: "You shall not perform any servile work."</i> The observance of a positive commandment does not negate the observance of both a positive and negative commandment.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Though the performance of a positive commandment overrides a negative commandment (<i>Yevamot</i> 3b), that applies only when the negative commandment is not reinforced by a positive commandment, as in the case at hand.</i><br>It is permitted to rinse a shofar with water, wine, or vinegar in order to improve its tone.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">It is forbidden to prepare a utensil for use on a festival. (See <i>Hilchot Sh'vitat Yom Tov</i> 4:8.) However, this activity is not placed in that category (Rabbenu Manoach).</i> However, as an expression of deference, one should never<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., even before Rosh HaShanah</i> use urine [for that purpose], lest one view the mitzvot in a deprecating manner.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam concludes <i>Hilchot Shechitah</i> (14:16):...lest one view the mitzvot in a deprecating manner, because the deference is not to be granted to the mitzvot in and of themselves, but to the One who commanded us to fulfill them, blessed be He.</i>
The minimum size of a shofar is [a measure] sufficient that one may hold the shofar in one's hands [with the ends] visibly [protruding]<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The protrusions are necessary lest one say that a person is producing the sounds by blowing into his hands without a shofar.</i> on either side.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Niddah</i> 26a defines this measure as "an expanded handbreadth." The <i>Beit Yosef</i> notes that, in this context, a handbreadth is defined as four thumbbreadths. Thus, the difference between the width of a thumb and the other fingers accounts for the "expansion." In modern measure, a handbreadth is considered between 8 (<i>Shiurei Torah</i>) and 9.6 (<i>Chazon Ish</i>) centimeters.</i><br>Should a shofar be cracked lengthwise,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., from its mouth to its end;</i> it is unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This is a quote from <i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 27b. The Rabbis have noted that, in contrast to a crack along the shofar's width, in this context no minimum figure is mentioned with regard to the portion of the shofar remaining uncracked. Two contrasting interpretations are offered to explain the difference.<br>Some maintain that as long as the entire length (or the majority of the length) of the shofar is not cracked, the shofar is not disqualified. Others maintain that even the slightest crack along the length of the shofar disqualifies it, because the pressure of the blowing will cause the crack to grow until, ultimately, the entire shofar will be cracked (Rabbenu Asher; <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 586:8).<br>Concerning <i>halachah l'ma'aseh</i>, both the <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> (586:8) and the <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> (586:43) write that when no other shofar is available, one may rely on the first opinion. Nevertheless, even the latter opinion does not disqualify a shofar that is cracked lengthwise if it is tied firmly so that the crack will not expand, or if the shofar is heated and the crack closed. Some opinions also allow such a shofar to be used if the crack is plugged closed with other substances.</i> Should it be cracked along its width<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">with the crack extending along the majority of the shofar's circumference (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> ibid., 9),</i> - if a measure equivalent to the minimum size of a shofar remains,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Most authorities require a handbreadth to remain from the crack to the shofar's mouth (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>). However, the <i>Ba'al Ha'itur</i> maintains that a shofar is acceptable even if the minimum measure remains only from the crack to the end of the shofar.</i> it is kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">This applies even if the sound of the shofar is changed because of the crack (<i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, ibid.).</i> It is considered as if it were cut off at the place of the crack.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Hence, the crack does not disqualify it.</i><br>[Regarding a shofar with] a hole: If it was plugged<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">In his commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 3:5), the Rambam writes that the hole must be plugged. This point is not accepted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (ibid., 7), which emphasizes that if the hole is not plugged, the shofar is kosher even though its sound has changed. Nevertheless, the Ramah states that if another shofar is available, a shofar with a hole should not be used.</i> with another substance,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., any substance other than a ram's horn;</i> it is unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">because the sound produced does not come from the shofar alone, but rather from the shofar and the other substance.</i> If it was plugged with its own kind, it is kosher [under the following conditions]:<br>the majority of the shofar remained whole;<br>the plugging of the holes did not alter its sound.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">These three conditions are dependent on the Rambam's interpretation of <i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 27b. Rabbenu Asher interprets the passage differently. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (ibid.) favors the Rambam's interpretation, but states that under difficult circumstances, when no other shofar is available, one may rely on Rabbenu Asher's interpretation.</i><br>If one [merely] perforated the insides of the horn,<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">the bonelike tissue inside the horn</i> [but did not remove them,] it is kosher, because a substance of the same kind is not considered an intervening entity.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The presence of a foreign substance inside the shofar would cause it to be disqualified, as stated in the following halachah. However, since this tissue is considered to be part of the horn itself, the shofar is acceptable.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (ibid., 586:15) quotes this law, but also adds that if one removed this tissue from the horn and then hollowed it out, the tissue would be unacceptable for use as a shofar.</i> Should one stick together fragments of <i>shofarot</i> until one has constructed a shofar, it is unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot, Rosh Hashanah</i> 27a explains that this construction is not called a shofar. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (ibid., 10) states that this law applies even if the fragment of the shofar closest to one's mouth is of sufficient size to be considered a kosher shofar itself.</i>
If one made any addition to a shofar<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">whether from the mouthpiece or from the wider end (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 586:11).</i> - whether of its kind<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">using a fragment of a ram's horn</i> or from another substance<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">it is unacceptable. - On the surface, this law appears to be an extension of the principle mentioned in the last clause of the previous halachah. If so, one might question why the Rambam mentions them in two separate halachot.</i> - it is unacceptable.<br>Should one coat it with gold<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">or any other foreign substance</i> from the inside or at the mouthpiece, it is unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">for the sound must come from the shofar itself. Though <i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 26b mentions that the mouth of the shofar used in the Temple was coated with gold, the place where the person blowing would put his mouth was not covered.</i> Should one coat it<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">with gold or any other substance</i> on the outside: If its sound is changed from what it was originally, it is not acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">for then, the sound we hear is a product of both the shofar and the coating, and not the shofar alone.<br>Based on this law, the Ramban advises against making designs in the shofar and coating them with paint or metal, for this may alter the shofar's sound and prevent the <i>teki'ot</i> from being acceptable.</i> If its sound did not change, it is kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">All these laws are quoted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 586:16).</i><br>Should one place one shofar within another: If one hears the sound of the inner shofar,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., with the further end of the inner shofar protruding beyond that of the outer shofar</i> one has fulfilled one's obligation.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">for the outer shofar had no effect on the sound we hear. The <i>Shulchan Aruch (Orach Chayim</i> 586:20) emphasizes that one may fulfill one's obligation under such circumstances only if the sound of the inner shofar remains totally unchanged. The <i>teki'ot</i> are unacceptable if its sound is altered.</i> If one hears the outer shofar,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., its edge protrudes beyond that of the inner shofar</i> one has not fulfilled one's obligation.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">for then, one is hearing the sound of two <i>shofarot</i>. The Torah commanded us to hear one shofar and not two (<i>Tosafot, Rosh Hashanah</i> 27b).</i><br>Should one widen the narrow portion of the shofar and narrow its wider end, the shofar is unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 27b explains the derivation of this law as follows: The terminology which Leviticus 25:9 uses when commanding us to blow the shofar, שופר והעברת, has an additional implication. That expression is also related to the word עבר, meaning "to pass." The manner in which we use the shofar must parallel the manner in which the ram passes by with it on its head.<br>Based on the same principle, our Sages (ibid.) explain that a shofar is unacceptable if it was heated to the point that the horn became soft, and then turned inside out.</i>
If a shofar was long and one shortened it,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">It makes no difference whether the portion is cut away from the shofar's mouthpiece or from its wider portion (<i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 586:63).</i> it is kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot, Rosh Hashanah</i> 27b explains that the necessity of mentioning this law arises from the last clause of the previous halachah. Since we find that the Torah requires us to use the shofar in the same manner as which it was carried by the ram, a special teaching is necessary to inform us that a shofar is acceptable even if it was shortened.<br>The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> explains that such a shofar is acceptable even it was shortened because of a disqualifying factor which it possessed on the portion which was cut off.</i> If one scraped away the horn<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">either from the inside - widening the hollow of the shofar</i> - either from the inside or from the outside<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">scraping away its outer shell</i> - even if one did so to the extent that all that remained was the thin external shell,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">even if the sound of the shofar changes (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 586:13; <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 586:65).</i> it is kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">since no change is made in its fundamental shape.</i><br>Regardless of whether [the shofar's] sound is heavy, thin, or raspy,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbenu Manoach and the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> translate צרור as "dry," explaining that blowing a shofar causes it to dry out and produce a raspy tone. Hence, it was customary to rinse it with water or wine, as mentioned in Halachah 4.</i> it is kosher, because all the sounds produced by the shofar are kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">In his commentary on this clause, Rabbenu Manoach injects a spiritual concept emphasizing how the musical quality of the shofar's tones are not significant, but rather the stirring and rousing nature of the shofar's call which motivates the people to Teshuvah.</i>
When a person sounds a shofar within a pit or within a cave,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Hai Gaon writes that these laws were not merely questions of abstract theory. Rather, they carried practical relevance in the Talmudic period, when the Jews frequently had to perform mitzvot clandestinely, to avoid being observed by the Roman authorities.</i> those standing within the pit or cave fulfill their obligation.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">for they hear the shofar's sound alone. Needless to say, both they and the person blowing the shofar must fulfill the conditions outlined in Chapter 2 regarding a person's fulfillment of the mitzvah when hearing the shofar blown by a colleague.</i> Concerning those standing outside: If<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">all</i> they hear<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">is</i> the sound of the shofar, they fulfill their obligation.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">However,</i> If they hear the sound of an echo,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">even if they hear the sound of the shofar together with it,</i> they do not fulfill their obligation.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">because another sound is mixed together with the desired sound.</i><br>Similar principles apply regarding one who blows into a giant barrel. If he hears<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> explains that the Rambam does not mention people standing within a barrel, because that is a very unlikely eventuality.</i> the sound of a shofar,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">alone,</i> he fulfills his obligation. If he hears an echo,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">together with the shofar,</i> he does not fulfill his obligation.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Taz</i> 587:1 explains that this concept is also relevant for synagogues with poor acoustics. If the people hear echoes together with the shofar's sound, they do not fulfill their obligation.<br>At present, there is a more common application of this principle. A person who hears the shofar through a microphone does not fulfill the mitzvah. In addition to the difficulties involved with the use of the microphone on a festival, there is a more essential problem. The listeners are not hearing the sound of the shofar, but rather a second sound, produced by a different mechanism. The microphone converts the sound waves of the shofar to electronic signals; these are then amplified and converted to a different set of sound waves. Hence, by hearing such a sound, we cannot fulfill the mitzvah obligating us to hear a shofar's call.</i>

Chapter 2

Everyone is obligated to hear<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Though the Rambam's statements are based on <i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 29a, he makes a slight emendation, adding the expression "to hear," in order to emphasize that the mitzvah is hearing, not blowing, the shofar.</i> the sounding of the shofar: priests, levites, Israelites, converts,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i> 12:17 states:All the gentiles - when they convert and accept all the mitzvot of the Torah - ...are considered as Jews in every regard.</i> and freed slaves.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i> 14:19 states: "Freed slaves are the same as converts."</i> However, women,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Women are freed from the obligation to perform mitzvot that are linked to a specific time. Nevertheless, based on <i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 33a, the <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 589:6 states that we are allowed to blow the shofar for them.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> continues that the women should not recite a blessing before hearing the shofar. Those statements are based on the Rambam, <i>Hilchot Tzitzit</i> 3:9 and Chapter 6, Halachah 13 below. The rationale is that the blessing recited before a commandment praises God "for commanding us to..." There is no commandment, either from the Torah or the Sages, requiring women to hear the blowing of the shofar. Hence, they should not recite this blessing.<br>The Ashkenazic concept differs. The Ramah (based on <i>Tosafot, Rosh Hashanah</i>, ibid.) maintains that women may recite a blessing. Since our Sages explained that the women are granted reward for fulfilling these mitzvot, it is obvious that the commandment is relevant to them and they may say "who commanded us."</i> slaves,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., gentile slaves (עבדים כנעניים), who are required to fulfill only the mitzvot for which women are obligated. (See <i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i> 12:11, 14:9.) A Jew sold as a slave (עבד עברי) is required to fulfill all the mitzvot.</i> and minors are free of the obligation.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Until a person reaches majority, he is not obligated to perform any of the mitzvot. Nevertheless, when a child reaches an age when he can appreciate the mitzvah, his father is obligated to train him to hear shofar as part of his education. (See <i>Hilchot Nachalot</i> 11:9.)</i><br>A person who is half slave and half free,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Hilchot Avadim</i> 7:4, the Rambam explains that a slave can reach this status in one of three ways:a) his owner took money with the intent of freeing half of the slave;<br>b) his master freed half the slave and sold or gave the other half to a colleague;<br>c) a slave belonged to two masters; one freed him and one did not.Such a slave is considered as having two different natures included in the same person. Thus, the aspect of him which is a free man is obligated to hear the shofar, and the aspect which is a slave is not. See also Halachah 3.</i> a <i>tumtum</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">an expression meaning "closed one" in Hebrew. It refers to a person whose genitalia are covered by a layer of skin. Hence, this person's gender cannot be determined without this skin's removal. Thus, there is a doubt whether this person is male or female. However, should this layer of skin be removed and the person's gender be discovered, there is no difference between him and an ordinary person of the same gender. (See <i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 2:25.)</i> and an <i>androgynous</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">a term borrowed from the Greek, in which <i>andro</i> means "man," and <i>gynous</i> "woman." It refers to person who has both male and female sexual organs. The Sages were unsure whether to consider such a person a male or a female. (See <i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 2:24 and also the commentary on the following halachah.)</i> are obligated [to hear shofar].<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">In the latter two cases, the obligation results from the doubt which exists whether these individuals are male (and hence obligated to hear shofar) or not.</i>
Whoever is not [himself] obligated regarding this matter<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., those mentioned in the previous halachah, a שוטה (a mentally unstable individual) and a חרש (a deaf person). In contrast, a blind person is obligated to hear the shofar.</i> cannot facilitate the performance of the mitzvah<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">by blowing the shofar</i> for one who is obligated.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">However, a male who has already fulfilled his obligation to hear the shofar can blow the shofar for a colleague (Rabbenu Manoach).</i> Thus, if a woman or a minor blows the shofar, one who hears does not fulfill his obligation.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Nevertheless, they are allowed to blow the shofar for themselves. (See Halachah 7 and <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 589:6.)</i><br>An <i>androgynous</i> can facilitate the performance of the mitzvah for one of his kind,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., another <i>androgynous</i></i> but not for one who is not of his kind.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">a normal adult male or a <i>tumtum</i>.<br>The Ra'avad objects to the Rambam's statement, noting that there is a difference of opinion among the Sages (<i>Yevamot</i> 83a) whether an <i>androgynous</i> is considered as half male and half female, or as a unique entity in his own right. The latter opinion is compatible with the Rambam's decision. However, according to the former opinion, it appears that an <i>androgynous</i> would resemble a person who is half slave and half free. As the following halachah states, such a person cannot blow the shofar even for himself, let alone for others.<br>The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> defends the Rambam's decision, explaining that the Rambam does not consider the difference of opinion in <i>Yevamot</i> as unresolved. Rather, he, as does Rav Yitzchak Alfasi (the Rif), maintains that the halachah follows the latter opinion.<br>Furthermore, the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> explains that even according to the former opinion, it is possible to differentiate between an <i>androgynous</i> and one who is half slave and half free, the former condition being brought about by God; the latter, by man.</i> A <i>tumtum</i> cannot facilitate the performance of the mitzvah [for anyone], whether of his kind or not of his kind, for<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">in his case, the doubt is not about the general category, but an individual question about the particular <i>tumtum's</i> nature</i> if [the layer of skin covering] the <i>tumtum's</i> [genitalia] is cut open, it is possible that it will be discovered that the <i>tumtum</i> is a male,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">and the <i>teki'ot</i> he blows are acceptable.</i> but it is possible that it will be discovered that the <i>tumtum</i> is a female.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">and hence, unable to blow the shofar for a male.</i>
A person who is half slave and half free cannot even facilitate the performance of the mitzvah<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">by blowing the shofar</i> for himself,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">let alone for others,</i> because the aspect of himself which is a slave cannot facilitate the performance of the mitzvah for the aspect of himself which is free.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Every activity performed by such an individual is considered as a combined effort carried out by both aspects of his being. The aspect of his being which is a free man is obligated to hear shofar blown by one who is obligated to perform the mitzvah. Hence, the <i>teki'ot</i> he blows himself include the influence of his slave side, and are not sufficient to facilitate his performance of the mitzvah (<i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 29a).</i><br>How should he fulfill his obligation? He should hear a free man blow the shofar.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">One might ask: Since his hearing the shofar also involves his slave side, how is that hearing sufficient to allow him to fulfill his obligation?<br>The <i>Tzaphnat Paneach</i> explains that there is a difference between hearing the shofar and blowing it. Hearing the shofar is a complete act in and of itself. Hence, since the free side of the person has heard the shofar, he is considered to have performed the mitzvah. In contrast, the blowing of the shofar is merely the preparation for the fulfillment of the mitzvah. Hence, the free side of the person cannot fulfill his obligation by hearing <i>tekiot</i> which were produced by a person whose entire being was not obligated to perform the mitzvah.<br>Based on this explanation, we can understand how such a person fulfills the mitzvah of lulav, for there as well, the act of taking the lulav constitutes rather than prepares for, the fulfilment of the mitzvah.</i>
A person who occupies himself with blowing the shofar in order to learn<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">without the intention of fulfilling the mitzvah of hearing the shofar</i> does not fulfill his obligation. Similarly, one who hears the shofar<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">even though he desires to fulfill the mitzvah</i> from a person who blows it casually<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">without the intention of facilitating his colleague's performance of the mitzvah</i> does not fulfill his obligation.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">for he has heard an unacceptable shofar blast (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 589:5).<br>These laws relate to a question of a much larger scope: Must a person who performs a mitzvah have the intention of doing so or not? <i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 32b, which is the source for these decisions, maintains that such intention is necessary. However, other Talmudic sources imply that there is no need for such intention. Whenever a person performs the deed of a mitzvah, he fulfills his obligation, regardless of his intention.<br>On the surface, the Rambam's own decisions regarding this question appear paradoxical. <i>Hilchot Chametz U'matzah</i> 6:3 states:A person who eats matzah without the intention [of fulfilling the mitzvah] - e.g., if gentiles or thieves force him to eat - fulfills his obligation.There, it appears that the Rambam does not require a person to have the intention to fulfill a mitzvah, for in this instance the only reason the person ate the matzah was the coercion of the gentiles.<br>The commentaries attempt to resolve the issue. The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> states that to fulfill his obligation, the person being forced to eat the matzah must know that today is Pesach, that he is obligated to eat matzah, and that it is matzah which he is eating. Rabbenu Nissim, the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, and Rabbenu Manoach follow a different line of reasoning. They differentiate between eating matzah and hearing a shofar. In the former case, a person's body benefits from the activity regardless of his intention. To support this thesis, they draw a parallel to the following passage.<br>Generally, the Torah frees a person from liability if he commits a transgression while being only מתעסק (performing a deed without any thought). However, a person who eats forbidden foods or engages in forbidden sexual relations in this manner is liable, because he derived pleasure from his activities (<i>Keritot</i> 19b).<br>Similarly, since the person derived physical satisfaction from eating the matzah, even though he was forced to do so, the action is attributed to him. In contrast, since his body did not benefit from hearing the shofar, he does not fulfill his obligation until he hears the shofar blown in the proper manner.<br>As regards <i>halachah l'ma'aseh</i>, in <i>Orach Chayim</i> 589:8, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> quotes the Rambam's decision concerning blowing the shofar, stating that a מתעסק does not fulfill the mitzvah. Similar decisions are rendered concerning <i>Kri'at Shema</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 60) and lulav and etrog (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 651).<br>Concerning matzah, the <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 475:4 quotes the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i>'s opinion, and the <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> adds the explanation mentioned in the name of the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>. (Even so, the <i>Pri Chadash</i> and other commentaries do not accept this decision.)</i><br>If the person hearing had the intention of fulfilling his obligation, but the person blowing did not have the intention of facilitating the latter's performance of the mitzvah,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 28b quotes Rabbi Zeira as instructing Shamaya: "Have me in mind and blow for me."</i> or the person blowing had the intention of facilitating his colleague's performance of the mitzvah, but the person hearing did not have the intention of fulfilling his obligation, [the person hearing] did not fulfill his obligation.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Magen Avraham</i> (189:4) states that if a person comes to the synagogue on Rosh Hashanah with the intention to fulfill his obligation to hear the shofar, that intention is sufficient. Even if he has no specific intention while the shofar is being blown, he fulfills his obligation.</i> Rather, both the person hearing and the one allowing him to hear<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., the person blowing the shofar</i> must have the [proper] intention.
If a person blew the shofar with the intention of enabling all those hearing his blowing to perform the mitzvah,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The blower need not have each individual in mind. However, as the previous halachah states, he must have the intention that his blowing enable people to fulfill their obligation to hear shofar. If he blows without that intention, their listening is of no consequence.</i> and a listener heard while having the intention to fulfill his obligation<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">However, as stated in the previous halachah, if the listener lacks that intention, he does not fulfill his obligation.</i> - even though the person blowing did not have a specific intention that this individual would hear his blowing, nor did he know about him<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">the listener has fulfilled his obligation, because the blower had in mind all those who heard him. - <i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 29a derives this principle by contrasting the law mentioned in the previous halachah with the law mentioned in this halachah's latter clause.</i> - the listener has fulfilled his obligation, because the blower had in mind all those who heard him.<br>Accordingly, if a person was traveling on a journey<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Magen Avraham</i> (589:5) explains that a person who continues traveling must be sure that he has the intention of fulfilling his obligation. However, if he stops to hear the shofar, that itself is sufficient proof that he desires to fulfill the mitzvah.</i> or was sitting in his home and heard the <i>teki'ot</i> from the person leading the congregation, he has fulfilled his obligation if he had that intention, since the leader of the congregation had the intention of enabling the many to fulfill their obligation.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">In his commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 3:5), the Rambam writes: "the [function of] leader of the congregation was instituted in that capacity only in order to enable the many to fulfill their obligation."</i>
If the festival of Rosh Hashanah falls on the Sabbath,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">According to the fixed calendar we use, Rosh Hashanah never falls on Sunday, Wednesday, or Friday (<i>Hilchot Kiddush Hachodesh</i> 7:1). Thus, the second day of Rosh Hashanah will never fall on the Sabbath. However, it is not at all infrequent for the first day of Rosh Hashanah to fall on the Sabbath.</i> the shofar is not sounded in every place.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">However, it is sounded in certain places, as explained in Halachot 8 and 9. There is an allusion to this in the Torah itself. Though Numbers 10:10 describes Rosh Hashanah as a day of "the sounding of the shofar," Leviticus 23:24 refers to it as a day of "the remembrance of the sounding [of the shofar" - i.e., this refers to Rosh Hashanah which falls on the Sabbath, when the shofar is not actually sounded (Rashi, <i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 32a).</i> [This law was enacted] even though blowing [the shofar] was forbidden only as <i>sh'vut</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See the commentary on Chapter 1, Halachah 4 for a definition of the term <i>sh'vut</i>. <i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 29b equates the blowing of the shofar with removing bread from the oven. It appears from <i>Shabbat</i> 117b that the removal of bread from the oven was forbidden only because it is "a weekday act," not in the spirit of the Sabbath. (See <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 588:4.)</i><br>It would be appropriate for [the shofar] to be sounded, for a positive commandment of the Torah should supersede <i>sh'vut</i> instituted by the Sages.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">A question can be raised, based on the Rambam's statements in Chapter 1, Halachah 4:Regarding a shofar to be used on Rosh Hashanah: It is forbidden to violate the festival's laws to obtain it. This applies even when the forbidden practice is in the category of <i>sh'vut</i>.The <i>Lechem Mishneh</i> offers a number of resolutions to this difficulty. Among them:<br>a) Had the proper attention been paid to the matter before Rosh Hashanah, the <i>sh'vut</i> mentioned in Chapter 1, Halachah 4 with regard to obtaining the shofar would not exist. In contrast, the <i>sh'vut</i> involved in blowing the shofar cannot be avoided.<br>b) The <i>sh'vut</i> mentioned in Chapter 1, Halachah 4 must be violated before the mitzvah is performed. Hence, there is no way the prohibition can be relaxed in the mere expectation that a mitzvah will be performed. In contrast, in this instance, the performance of the mitzvah and the <i>sh'vut</i> occur concurrently.<br>However, the order of the halachot here suggests a different explanation. As the Rambam states in the following halachah, certain leniencies are taken regarding the <i>sh'vut</i> of sounding the shofar on Rosh Hashanah (since, as mentioned above, it is forbidden only because it is a "weekday act"). In contrast, the activities mentioned in Chapter 1, Halachah 4 are of a more severe nature, and hence no leniency may be taken in their regard.</i> If so, why is the shofar not sounded?<br>Because of a decree [of the Sages] lest a person take it in his hands and carry it to a colleague so that the latter can blow for him,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam alters the expression used by <i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 29b, which states: "perhaps he will take it to a skilled person so that the latter will teach him." Perhaps, the emendation was made because it is more likely that a person would desire that his colleague blow for him than begin to teach him on Rosh Hashanah itself.</i> and [in the process,] carry it four cubits in the public domain or transfer it from one domain to another,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Shabbat</i> 12:8 equates the two and explains the nature of this <i>melachah</i>. It must be noted that the phrase "transfer it from one domain..." is the Rambam's addition and does not appear in <i>Rosh Hashanah</i>, ibid. (Indeed, <i>Tosafot, Megillah</i> 4b explains that there is no suspicion that the latter prohibition will be violated.)</i> and thus violate a prohibition punishable by being stoned to death.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">as is the commission of all Shabbat <i>melachot</i> (<i>Hilchot Shabbat</i> 1:1-2.)</i> [This is necessary because] all are obligated in the mitzvah of blowing the shofar, but not all are skilled in it.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">A similar expression is also found in <i>Hilchot Megillah</i> 1:13, explaining why the Megillah is never read on the Sabbath. It must be noted that while the Temple was standing, a similar decree was not enacted on the first day of Sukkot which fell on the Sabbath, even though it is possible that a person would carry his lulav in a forbidden manner. Note our commentary on Chapter 7, Halachot 13 and 14, which explains the difference between the two festivals.<br>The fact that a Rabbinic decree can cause a mitzvah to be nullified - particularly as significant a mitzvah as the blowing of the shofar - has been the subject of much discussion by the Rabbis. In his commentary on the tractate of <i>Sukkah</i>, Rabbenu Nissim states that this decree was instituted only because in most Jewish communities, the people were not aware of the date the Rabbis had established for Rosh Hashanah. Hence, since the people could not be sure that they were in fact performing a mitzvah, the Rabbinic decree had the power to negate its performance. Even in the places where they were conscious of the proper date, they would not blow the shofar in such a year, so that great divisions in Torah practice would not be created among the Jewish people.<br>In <i>Likkutei Torah</i>, Rav Shneur Zalman of Liadi explains the matter from a mystical perspective. He writes that the Sabbath itself generates spiritual influences which resemble those produced by the blowing of the shofar. Thus, there is no necessity to blow the shofar, and the Sages were therefore willing to allow such a decree to negate its observance.</i>
Children who have not reached an age at which they can be educated:<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbenu Manoach explains that this refers to children of six or seven.</i> We need not prevent them from blowing [the shofar] on a Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">even though blowing the shofar on the Sabbath is prohibited for an adult</i> which is not the festival of Rosh Hashanah, so that they will learn [to blow].<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">This halachah is not directly related to the laws of Rosh Hashanah, but rather to the laws of the Sabbath. If a child has not reached the age where he can be educated concerning the mitzvot, his parents are not required to prevent him from violating any of the mitzvot (<i>Yevamot</i> 114a).<br>The law stated here represents a reversal of the Rambam's understanding of <i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 33a from that in his commentary on the Mishnah. Indeed, his change in interpretation caused the Ra'avad to object to this halachah. It is interesting to note that the Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 4:9) and the primary interpretation mentioned in the <i>Halachot</i> of Rav Yitzchak Alfasi (the Rif) - sources which the Rambam relies upon heavily - follow the Rambam's initial approach.</i><br>An adult is permitted to be involved in the instruction [of children<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">He may tell them to blow the shofar and teach them how to blow (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).</i> in the blowing of shofar] on the festival.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">even though the blowing of the shofar is prohibited on the Sabbath.</i> [This applies] concerning both children who have reached an age at which they can be educated and those who have not reached that age, for blowing [the shofar] is prohibited only as <i>sh'vut</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Since the prohibition against blowing the shofar is not severe, a number of leniencies are taken in this regard. The shofar is sounded for women (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 589:6), and similarly, additional blasts are sounded to "upset Satan" (Ramah, <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i>, 596:1). However, it is forbidden for an adult to sound the shofar on Rosh Hashanah unnecessarily. Nevertheless, even a child who has reached the age when he is educated concerning the mitzvot need not follow this stringency (Ramah, ibid.).</i>
When [the Sages] decreed not to sound [the shofar] on the Sabbath, they applied that decree only to places which lacked a court.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The Sages forbade sounding the shofar on the Sabbath lest someone carry it in a forbidden manner, as explained in the previous halachah. However, that decree was not applied to places where the Sanhedrin, Israel's supreme court, held its sessions. The influence of the court would be felt throughout the entire city and its surroundings. Hence, the people would be more scrupulous in their observance of the mitzvot. (See the Rambam's commentary on the Mishnah <i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 4:1 and the following halachah.)</i> However, while the Temple was standing and the Supreme Court was seated in Jerusalem,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">After the Temple was constructed, the Sanhedrin held its sessions in the Chamber of Hewn Stone in the Temple. Forty years before the destruction of the Second Temple, the Sanhedrin was forced to move from that place to other locations in Jerusalem. (See <i>Hilchot Sanhedrin</i> 14:12-13.)</i> everyone<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., not only was the shofar sounded at the seat of the Sanhedrin and on the Temple mount, but throughout the city; even private individuals were allowed to blow the shofar (<i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 30a).</i> would sound the shofar in Jerusalem throughout the entire period the court held its sessions there.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad objects to the Rambam's statements. He disagrees with the Rambam on two points:<br>a) while the Temple was standing, the Rambam maintains that the shofar was sounded throughout Jerusalem, while the Ra'avad maintains that it was sounded in the Temple alone.<br>b) after the Temple's destruction, the Rambam maintains that the shofar was not sounded in Jerusalem, while the Ra'avad argues that it was.<br>The difference between the two depends on the Rambam's interpretation of the Mishnah (<i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 4:1):When the festival of Rosh Hashanah fell on the Sabbath, they would sound the shofar in the <i>Mikdash</i>, but not in the country at large.In his commentary on that Mishnah, the Rambam writes: "We have already explained to you several times (see <i>Ma'aser Sheni</i> 2:4, <i>Shekalim</i> 1:3, <i>Sukkah</i> 3:10) that the term <i>mikdash</i> refers to the entire city of Jerusalem." Thus, he maintains that if Rosh Hashanah fell on the Sabbath while the Temple was standing, the shofar would be sounded throughout the city. After the destruction of the Temple, this practice was discontinued.</i><br>[This did not apply] to the people of Jerusalem alone.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The influence of the Sanhedrin could be felt even on the outskirts of the city.</i> Rather, every city that was within the outer limits<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the תחום שבת - 2000 cubits from the outermost house from each point on the city's circumference. (See <i>Hilchot Shabbat</i>, Chapters 27 and 28.)</i> of Jerusalem<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">There were a number of smaller villages surrounding Jerusalem which met all the requirements specified below.</i> and [whose inhabitants] could:<br>see Jerusalem<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., excluding those within a wadi - below the mountains, who could not see Jerusalem.</i> - i.e., excluding those within a wadi;<br>hear the shofar blown in Jerusalem<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., excluding those on the mountaintops - Since they were situated on the mountaintops, they could see the city even from afar. However, the sound would not carry that far.</i> - i.e., excluding those on the mountaintops; and<br>travel to Jerusalem<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., excluding those separated by a wadi - which they would be unable to cross</i> - i.e., excluding those separated by a wadi from the city<br>the<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The exclusions mentioned by the Rambam are quoted from <i>Rosh Hashanah</i>, ibid., based on the text of the Mishnah. The Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 4:1) interprets the Mishnah slightly differently.</i> people of these cities would blow the shofar on the Sabbath as in Jerusalem. However, in the other cities of Israel,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">even where the courts held session</i> they would not sound [the shofar on the Sabbath].
At present, while the Temple is destroyed, wherever a court whose judges received <i>semichah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Semichah</i> is the term used to refer to the permission granted to a judge to act in that capacity. Only a judge who himself possesses <i>semichah</i> can convey this distinction to a colleague. Moses gave Joshua and the seventy elders <i>semichah</i>, and they began a chain that stretched over many generations, ending several hundred years after the destruction of the Temple. (See <i>Hilchot Sanhedrin</i> 4:1-2.)</i> in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>semichah</i> could be conveyed only in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> (ibid.:6).</i> permanently holds sessions,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., if, on Rosh Hashanah, the court held special sessions in a city where it was not normally located, the shofar would not be sounded. (See the Rambam's commentary on the Mishnah, <i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 4:1.)</i> the shofar is sounded on the Sabbath.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah (<i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 29b) states:When the Temple was destroyed, Rabbi Yochanan ben Zakkai ordained that the shofar be sounded in every place where a court holds sessions.The Talmud elaborates:[After the destruction of the Temple,] Rosh Hashanah once fell on the Sabbath, and all the cities gathered together [in Yavneh]. Rabbi Yochanan ben Zakkai asked the family of Beteira: "Shall we blow?"<br>"Let us consider the matter," they replied to him.<br>"Let us blow, and then consider the matter after having blown," he answered them.<br>"Let us consider the matter," they replied again.<br>He told them, "The sounding of a horn has already been heard in Yavneh. Once a deed has been performed, no further discussion is entertained."</i> Furthermore, the shofar is sounded on the Sabbath only in a court that has sanctified the new moon.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Originally, the months were sanctified by the courts after hearing the testimony of witnesses who saw the new moon. (See <i>Hilchot Kiddush Hachodesh</i>, Chapters 1 and 2.)</i> However, the shofar will not be sounded in other courts, even though their judges have received <i>semichah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's statements represent a middle road in a difference of opinion between the Rabbinical giants upon whom our understanding of the Talmud is based. Rashi (<i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 29a) states that the shofar was sounded on the Sabbath only in the presence of the Supreme Sanhedrin. Thus, this law applied only in Yavneh and in the few other cities where the Sanhedrin subsequently held its session.<br>Rav Yitzchak Alfasi (the Rif) quotes this law in his <i>Halachot</i> even though he quotes only those laws which are applicable in his age. From this it appears that he maintains that the shofar may be sounded in the presence of any court of stature on the Sabbath, even if its judges do not possess <i>semichah</i>. Indeed, the Ramban and Rabbenu Nissim write that Rav Yitzchak Alfasi would have the shofar sounded in his own court when Rosh Hashanah fell on the Sabbath. Similarly, the text <i>Haezer</i> records that the author saw a Sage from Damascus who stated that he saw the shofar blown on Rosh Hashanah which fell on a Sabbath.<br>(The Rambam's Commentary on the Mishnah, ibid., does not mention the need for the judges to possess <i>semichah</i>. Thus, it is possible that he also originally subscribed to this view.)<br>In the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>, the Rambam's position lies in between these views. On one hand, unlike Rav Yitzchak Alfasi, he requires that the judges possess <i>semichah</i> and be of sufficient stature to have sanctified the new moon (thus, excluding any contemporary courts). Nevertheless, unlike Rashi, he allows the shofar to be sounded in the presence of any court that has those qualifications, and not the Supreme Sanhedrin alone.</i> Also, the shofar is sounded only in the presence of a high court.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation of בית דין גדול is based on the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, who explains that in this context the term refers to an established court of judges with <i>semichah</i>, and not the Supreme Sanhedrin, as is sometimes the case. Indeed, authoritative manuscripts of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> from Yemen omit the word גדול from the above expression.</i><br>It may be sounded during the entire time they are in session.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">but only while they are in session. If they have risen, the shofar can no longer be sounded. However...</i> Even after they<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">the judges</i> have begun preparing to rise<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">as long as they have not risen - the shofar may be sounded before them - Though the Sages (<i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 30a) raised a question on this matter and left it unanswered, the Rambam allows the shofar to be sounded under such circumstances. Since the question concerns a Rabbinic decree, the more lenient approach is taken (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).</i> - as long as they have not risen - the shofar may be sounded before them. However, outside the court, the shofar may not be sounded.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">In this aspect, there was a difference between the sounding of the shofar on the Sabbath between Jerusalem and Yavneh. In Jerusalem, the influence of the Temple and the court was felt so strongly that even outside the presence of the court, the shofar could be sounded. In contrast, in Yavneh and other cities where courts were located, the shofar was sounded only in the presence of a court (<i>Rosh Hashanah</i>, ibid.).</i><br>Why is the shofar allowed to be sounded in the court? Because the court is scrupulous [in the observance of the mitzvot] and, in its presence, those who blow the shofar will not carry the shofar in the public domain, for the court will warn the people and inform them.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>S'dei Chemed</i> derives a general principle from this law. <i>Hilchot Shabbat</i> 21:27 states that all Sabbath and festival prohibitions that were categorized as <i>sh'vut</i> were suspended in the Temple, because the priests were scrupulous in their observance of the mitzvot and there was no danger that any infringement of Torah law would occur. Similarly, the above text continues, all decrees of this nature may be suspended in the presence of a high court, because the court will ensure that the Torah's laws will be kept.<br>This logic runs contrary to a statement of Rabbenu Nissim, who writes that although Rabbi Yochanan ben Zakkai instituted the blowing of the shofar on the Sabbath in the presence of a court, he was not that lenient regarding the taking of the lulav and etrog. Thus, we see that the leniency mentioned here applies to the shofar alone and not to other cases.</i>
In the present age, when we celebrate Rosh Hashanah in the exile for two days,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">There is a slight imprecision with the Rambam's statements. Rosh Hashanah was celebrated for two days throughout almost the totality of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> even while the Temple was standing. It was impossible to notify the people in most of the land (for the messengers were not allowed to travel more than two thousand cubits beyond Jerusalem) whether or not the court had substantiated the testimony of the witnesses on the day when Rosh Hashanah was expected to fall. (See <i>Beitzah</i> 4b-5a; <i>Hilchot Kiddush Hachodesh</i> 5:7-8.)</i> the shofar is sounded on the second day just as it is sounded on the first.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the actual sounding of the shofar. According to Sephardic custom, there is a difference concerning the recitation of the blessing <i>shehecheyanu</i> (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 499:3).</i><br>If the first day<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned above, according to the fixed calendar which we employ at present, it is impossible for the second day of Rosh Hashanah to fall on the Sabbath.</i> falls on the Sabbath, those who were not in the presence of a court fit to blow the shofar on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> notes that there is some difficulty correlating this statement with that of the previous halachah, which implies that, at present, no courts are of sufficient stature to blow the shofar on the Sabbath. Among the possible resolutions to this difficulty are:<br>a) as the Rambam writes in <i>Hilchot Kiddush Hachodesh</i> 5:3, until the age of Abbaye and Ravvah, there were courts of judges who sanctified the new month based on the testimony of witnesses. Thus, the Rambam could be referring to such a court.<br>b) In <i>Hilchot Sanhedrin</i> 4:11, the Rambam discusses the possibility of the renewal of the practice of <i>semichah</i>. He could be referring to such an eventuality in the present instance.</i> may blow the shofar on the second day alone.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">This is our practice at present.</i>

Chapter 3

How many shofar blasts is a person required to hear on Rosh Hashanah?<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">according to Torah law?</i> Nine.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">However, as explained throughout the chapter, the Sages have required us to hear many more.</i><br>[This figure is derived<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 33b.</i> as follows]: The Torah mentions the word תרועה [sounding the shofar] three times<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Teru'ah</i> is also mentioned in relation to sounding the trumpets (Numbers, Chapter 10), but that is not relevant in this immediate context.</i> in association with Rosh Hashanah<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">twice: Leviticus 23:24 and Numbers 29:1.</i> and the <i>Yovel</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">once: Leviticus 25:9.</i> Every תרועה must be preceded and followed by a [single] long blast.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Leviticus 25:9 states: והעברת שופר, and Numbers 29:1 states: תעבירו שופר. In both cases, the verb means "cause to pass." From that expression, <i>Rosh Hashanah</i>, ibid., derives that a long note should be sounded before the תרועה.<br>Based on the above, the text <i>Galia Masechta</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 3) explains that the essential element of the mitzvah is the <i>teru'ah</i>, since that is the sound specifically required by the Torah. The <i>teki'ot</i> are required only as a supplementary factor.</i> According to the oral tradition, we learned that<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">whether on Rosh Hashanah or on Yom Kippur of the Yovel - <i>Rosh Hashanah</i>, ibid. notes that the verses which describe the sounding of the shofar on both occasions share a common word. Hence, an analogy (שוה גזירה) is established between them. Therefore...</i> - whether on Rosh Hashanah or on Yom Kippur of the <i>Yovel</i> - all the soundings of the shofar of the seventh month are a single entity.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Thus, on each occasion, the same three series of blasts must be sounded.</i><br>Thus, nine shofar blasts must be sounded on both of them: <i>teki'ah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The single, long blast preceding the <i>teru'ah</i></i> <i>teru'ah,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">a blast which is interrupted into shorter sounds, as described in Halachot 2-4.</i><i> teki'ah;<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The single, long blast following the <i>teru'ah</i></i> teki'ah, teru'ah,</i> <i>teki'ah; teki'ah, teru'ah, teki'ah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">two other identical series of blasts.</i>
Over the passage of the years and throughout the many exiles, doubt has been raised concerning the <i>teru'ah</i> which the Torah mentions, to the extent that we do not know what it<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 34a explains that our custom of blowing the shofar was instituted by Rabbi Abahu in Caesaria approximately one hundred years after the destruction of the second Temple. In this halachah, the Rambam explains the reason for Rabbi Abahu's action.<br>The Rambam's statements are accepted by Rabbenu Asher, Rabbenu Nissim, and most other authorities. Nevertheless, it is significant that Rav Hai Gaon, one of the major authorities who preceded the Rambam, offers a different explanation for Rabbi Abahu's actions.<br>Rav Hai Gaon maintains that it is impossible that a doubt existed among the Jewish people concerning such an important manner as sounding the shofar. Since the shofar was sounded each year, surely the tradition could have been maintained. Hence, he explains that all the different manners of sounding the shofar were acceptable and that Rabbi Abahu's contribution was the establishment of a uniform custom that included all the different practices common among the Jewish people. By mentioning the "many years" and "exiles," the Rambam addresses himself to the question raised by Rav Hai Gaon.</i> is:<br>Does it resemble the wailing with which the women cry<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">thus, it would be short, staccato sounds like sobs</i> when they moan,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The word <i>teru'ah</i> is translated into Aramaic as <i>yevavah</i> (moan), as in Judges 5:28: "Sisra's mother gazed out the window and moaned through the lattice."</i> or<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">does a <i>teru'ah</i> resemble...</i> the sighs<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., longer sounds</i> which a person who is distressed about a major matter will release repeatedly?<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Since before a person cries, he sighs (<i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 33b).</i> Perhaps a combination of the two<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">sighing and the crying which will follow it - is called teru'ah, because a distressed person will sigh and then cry. Therefore, we fulfill all [these possibilities]. - as explained in detail in the following halachah.<br>The commentaries have emphasized the homiletic aspects of these halachot. On Rosh Hashanah, a Jew must concentrate on the coronation of God as King of the world. He may feel internal distress when he meditates on how his daily behavior does not reflect the proper relation between a subject and his king. Ultimately, this may motivate him to sigh and even to cry.</i> - sighing and the crying which will follow it - is called <i>teru'ah</i>, because a distressed person will sigh and then cry? Therefore, we fulfill all [these possibilities].
The crying<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">mentioned in the previous halachah</i> refers to what we call <i>teru'ah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">short, staccato sounds like sobs.
