Since a <i>teru'ah</i> is considered to be a single blast, the entire series of sounds must be completed without the person who blows pausing to catch his breath.</i> The repeated sighs<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">mentioned in the previous halachah</i> refer to what we call three<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> writes that one may add to the number of <i>shevarim</i> as long as one does not take a breath between them. However, the <i>Magen Avraham</i> (590:2) writes that it is Ashkenazic custom not to sound more than three <i>shevarim</i>.</i> <i>shevarim</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Like sighs, these sounds are neither short like <i>teru'ot</i>, nor prolonged like <i>teki'ot</i>, but rather of intermediate length, as described in the following halachah.</i><br>Thus, the order of blowing the shofar<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">to fulfill the mitzvah as commanded by the Torah.</i> is as follows: First, one recites the blessing<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">as explained in Halachah 10.</i> and sounds a <i>teki'ah</i>; afterwards, three <i>shevarim</i>; and afterwards, a <i>teru'ah</i>; and afterwards, a <i>teki'ah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">in accordance with the opinion that maintains that a <i>teru'ah</i> resembles both sighing and crying.</i> He repeats this pattern [until he completes] three series.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">in order to have heard the three series of blasts required by Halachah 1.<br>Although the pattern of shofar blowing described by the Rambam has become universally accepted among the Jewish people, it is unclear when this practice was first instituted. One of the fundamental texts of the period directly following the conclusion of the Talmud, the <i>Sh'eltot</i> of Rav Achai Gaon (note 171), mentions a different practice.<br>Three series of blasts from each pattern are not blown directly after each other. Rather, after sounding three series of <i>teki'ah, shevarim, teru'ah, teki'ah</i>, three series of <i>teki'ah, shevarim, teki'ah</i>, and then three series of <i>teki'ah, teru'ah, teki'ah</i> is sounded.</i><br>[Then,] he sounds a <i>teki'ah</i>; afterwards, three <i>shevarim</i>; and afterwards, a <i>teki'ah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">in accordance with the opinion that maintains that a <i>teru'ah</i> resembles sighing. It must be noted that from the passage on <i>Rosh HaShanah</i> 34a it seems that Rabbi Abahu instituted the series of three <i>teru'ot</i> before the series of three <i>shevarim</i>.</i> He repeats this pattern [until he completes] three series.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">to fulfill the obligation explained in Halachah 1.</i><br>[Then,] he sounds a <i>teki'ah</i>; afterwards, a <i>teru'ah</i>; and afterwards, a <i>teki'ah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">in accordance with the opinion that maintains that a <i>teru'ah</i> resembles crying.</i> He repeats this pattern [until he completes] three series.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">as explained above.</i><br>Thus, there are a total of thirty shofar blasts,<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">18 <i>teki'ot</i>, six <i>shevarim</i>, and six <i>teru'ot</i>.<br>From the Rambam's expression, it appears that he considers the <i>shevarim</i> and <i>teru'ah</i> sounded in the first series as two separate blasts. From this, the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> derives a halachic insight, maintaining that according to the Rambam, one may take a breath between the two. (The <i>Mayim Chayim</i> supports this conclusion, noting that, regarding this series, the Rambam states that one should blow "three <i>shevarim</i>, and afterwards, a <i>teru'ah</i>.") Nevertheless, the Ramban and other authorities do not permit this leniency.<br>Regarding <i>halachah l'ma'aseh</i>, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 590:4) suggests adhering to the more stringent view regarding the <i>teki'ot</i> required by Torah law. However, the Ramah maintains that this is unnecessary. Both the <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 590:9, 10 and the <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 590:20 suggest adhering to whichever practice is customarily followed in one's local community. However, if there is no accepted custom, the <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> suggests accepting the more stringent practice.</i> in order to remove any doubt.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">as mentioned in the previous halachah.<br>Though all three sounds would be included in the series of <i>teki'ah, shevarim, teru'ah, teki'ah</i>, it would be insufficient for such a series alone to be sounded. According to the opinions that maintain that a <i>teru'ah</i> is either sobbing or crying alone, there would be another sound interrupting between either the <i>teki'ah</i> which precedes or that which follows the <i>teru'ah</i> (<i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 34a).</i>
The required length of a <i>teru'ah</i> is that of two <i>teki'ot</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">These statements are the source of much controversy among the Rabbis. The Mishnah (<i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 33b) states: "The required length of the <i>teki'ah</i> is three <i>teru'ot</i>; a <i>teru'ah</i> is three sobs."<br>The Talmud questions:Behold, the <i>baraita</i> has taught: "the required length of a <i>teki'ah</i> is that of a <i>teru'ah</i>!"<br>Abbaye replied: "The author of the Mishnah considered the length of all the <i>teki'ot</i> in comparison to the length of all the <i>teru'ot</i>. The author of the <i>baraita</i> considered the length of each blast. There is no difference of opinion between them."In his commentary on the Mishnah, the Rambam interprets the above to mean: "The required length of all six <i>teki'ot</i> is equivalent to the required length of all three <i>teru'ot</i>," thus establishing each <i>teru'ah</i> as twice the length of a <i>teki'ah</i>. Hence, according to the Rambam, the length of the <i>teki'ot</i> are dependent on the length of the <i>teru'ot</i>.<br>Rabbenu Nissim objects to the Rambam's interpretation, explaining that the word והעברת, the verb mentioned in the commandment to blow the shofar, implies a long blast, and hence the measure mentioned by the Rambam would not be appropriate.<br>Similarly, the Ra'avad does not accept the Rambam's interpretation, and explains that the Mishnah establishes an independent measure for the length of a <i>teki'ah</i> - three <i>teru'ot</i> - with each <i>teru'ah</i> being equal to three short sobs. Thus, the measure of a <i>teki'ah</i> is equivalent to nine short sounds. The Ramban and the Rashba also subscribe to this view.<br>Rashi and most Ashkenazic authorities accept the simple meaning of the Mishnah and explain that a <i>teki'ah</i> is equal to the length of a <i>teru'ah</i>. However, as explained in the previous halachot, there are three different interpretations of the definition of a <i>teru'ah</i>. Thus, the required length for the <i>teki'ah</i> varies accordingly.<br>Regarding <i>halachah l'ma'aseh</i>, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 590:3) mentions the views of Rashi and the Ra'avad, but not that of the Rambam.</i> The required length of the three <i>shevarim</i> is that of a <i>teru'ah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., the length of the three <i>shevarim</i> should resemble a series of <i>teru'ot</i>.</i><br>When a person sounds a <i>teki'ah</i> and a <i>teru'ah</i>, and afterwards sounds a long <i>teki'ah</i>, extending it twice the length of the original one,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">with the intention that it be considered to be the final blast of the first series and the initial blast of the following series</i> we do not say that it may be considered to be two <i>teki'ot</i><sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">although it is of sufficient length</i> - thus allowing one [to complete the series merely by] sounding a <i>teru'ah</i> and another <i>teki'ah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">This represents a rephrasing of the Mishnah, <i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 4:9 and <i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 28a.</i> Rather, even if one extended a <i>teki'ah</i> the entire day, it is considered to be only a single <i>teki'ah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">This decision runs contrary to the statements of the Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 3:3), which does not accept a <i>teki'ah</i> blown with such an intention at all.</i> and one must sound another <i>teki'ah, teru'ah,</i> and <i>teki'ah</i> [until he completes] three series.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">This law is quoted as halachah by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 590:6). However, the Ashkenazic authorities maintain that deference should be paid to the opinion mentioned in the Jerusalem Talmud.</i>
If a person hears one shofar blast at one hour and a second one an hour later<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">even if he waits the entire day - <i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 34a-b quotes Rabbi Yochanan: "If a person heard the nine shofar blasts even over a span of nine hours, he fulfills his obligation."</i> - even if he waits the entire day - the two may be considered to be a single unit<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">It is undesirable to delay the shofar blasts or to interrupt between them by talking or performing any other activity. Nevertheless, if an interruption was made between blasts, as long as one concentrates one's attention on each shofar blast, they are considered to be a single halachic unit.</i> and he may fulfill his obligation.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 588:2) quotes this law. However, the <i>Magen Avraham</i> (588:2) compares this situation to the laws of <i>Kri'at Shema</i> (see Ramah, <i>Orach Chayim</i> 65:1) and explains that if one is prevented from hearing the shofar by forces beyond one's control, it is considered to be an interruption and one must begin the last series of blasts anew.</i><br>The above applies provided each series is heard in the proper order;<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">a <i>teki'ah</i>, the series of<i>shevarim</i>, <i>teru'ot</i> or the combination of the two, and then a <i>teki'ah</i>, as stated in Halachah 1.</i> i.e., one may not hear a <i>teru'ah</i> and afterwards two <i>teki'ot</i>, or two <i>teki'ot</i>, and afterwards a <i>teru'ah</i>, and the like.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> explains that with this phrase, the Rambam implies that even though a delay does not constitute an interruption, any shofar blast which is not in the proper order invalidates the entire series. Most other authorities (see <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 590:12) explain that this applies only if one sounds the shofar with an improper blast, intending to fulfill one's obligation. Otherwise, no significance at all is attached to the shofar blast and it is not considered an interruption.</i>
If a person heard nine shofar blasts from nine men simultaneously,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">At the same time, six blew <i>teki'ot</i> and three, <i>teru'ot</i>, so that the person could have heard the required number of shofar blasts</i> he has not fulfilled his obligation for a single blast.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbenu Manoach gives two reasons for this decision:<br>a) because of the cacophony of sounds, the listener is unable to properly distinguish any one;<br>b) As stated in the previous halachah, one must hear the shofar blasts in the proper order, a <i>teki'ah</i> preceding and following a <i>teru'ah</i>; that requirement is not fulfilled in this instance.<br>Rashi, in his commentary on <i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 34b, disagrees with the Rambam's decision, explaining that <i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 27a states that even though two people blow the shofar simultaneously, since the mitzvah is dear to the hearer, he will concentrate sufficiently to differentiate between the sounds. Furthermore, the <i>Tosefta</i> 2:12 states specifically that one does fulfill one's obligation in this manner.<br><i>Tosafot</i> disagrees with Rashi, mentioning the second reason stated by Rabbenu Manoach. It appears that the Rambam goes further and discounts Rashi's opinion entirely. According to <i>Tosafot</i>, the listener will have heard at least the first <i>teki'ah</i>, while the Rambam explicitly states "he has not fulfilled his obligation for a single blast." (See <i>Lechem Mishneh</i>.)<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 588:3 quotes the Rambam's decision as halachah. The Ramah quotes the law from <i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 27a, allowing a person to fulfill his obligation even though he heard two people blowing the shofar at the same time, provided he heard the blasts in the proper order.</i> [If he heard] a <i>teki'ah</i> from one, a <i>teru'ah</i> from another, and another <i>teki'ah</i> from a third, in sequence, he has fulfilled his obligation.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The fact that different people sounded the shofar does not prevent the fulfillment of the mitzvah.</i><br>The above applies even if one heard [the shofar blasts] with interruptions, even if [the blowing was extended] over the entire day.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">There is a certain redundancy with this statement, because the same principle was mentioned in the previous halachah. Nonetheless, the Rambam included it to emphasize that the passage of time is not considered an interruption even when the shofar is being blown by different people (Rabbenu Manoach).</i> A person does not fulfill his obligation until he hears all nine shofar blasts, for they are all [only] one mitzvah.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">as explained in Halachah 1.</i> Thus, they are dependent one on the other.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Based on this principle, a person who does not know how to blow either the <i>shevarim</i> or the <i>teru'ot</i> should not sound the <i>teki'ot</i>, for he will accomplish nothing by doing so (<i>Tosafot, Rosh Hashanah</i> 33b). Nevertheless, if a person knows how to sound both <i>teki'ot</i> and <i>shevarim</i>, he should sound three series of such blasts even if he does not know how to sound a <i>teru'ah</i>. Since it is possible that the mitzvah may be fulfilled by these series (as explained in Halachot 2 and 3), at the very least they should be sounded (Rabbenu Nissim, <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 593:2).</i>
The congregation<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">but not an individual (<i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 34b)</i> is obligated to hear the shofar blasts<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., the nine blasts mentioned in Halachah 1</i> together with the order of blessings.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 16b explains that the Sages instituted the blowing of the shofar in the midst of the prayer service in order to confuse Satan, lest he lodge accusations against the Jews in the midst of their prayers. The Sages explain (ibid., 32b) that it would have been appropriate to institute the shofar blowing in the morning service. However, the Romans desired to stamp out the performance of the mitzvot and sent guards to make sure that the mitzvah was not fulfilled. After midday, the guards left, and then, in the musaf service, the shofar would be sounded.<br>Originally, the Sages' decree involved determining the place of the service when the mitzvah required by the Torah would be fulfilled and this halachah must be interpreted in that context. However, as explained in Halachot 11 and 12, it became universally accepted Jewish custom to blow the shofar before the musaf prayers to fulfill the Torah's commandment, and thus "confuse the Satan" even before the beginning of our prayers, and then to sound the shofar again in the midst of those prayers to fulfill the Rabbinic decree. See <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 592:7.</i><br>How is this expressed? The <i>chazan</i> recites <i>Avot</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">literally, "patriarchs"; the first blessing of the <i>Amidah</i>, which praises God as "the God of Abraham, the God of Isaac, and the God of Jacob."</i> <i>Gevurot</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">literally, "mighty acts"; the second blessing of the <i>Amidah</i>, which begins: "You are mighty," and recounts different expressions of God's power.</i> the sanctification of God's name,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The third blessing of the <i>Amidah</i>, which includes the <i>Kedushah</i> prayer.</i> <i>Malchuyot</i><sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Three additional blessings are recited in the musaf service of Rosh Hashanah: <i>Malchuyot</i> (acceptance of God's sovereignty), <i>Zichronot</i> (acknowledgement of God's remembrance of the Jewish people), and <i>Shofarot</i> (describing the significance of the sounding of the shofar).<br><i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 16a states:The Holy One, blessed be He, declares: "On Rosh Hashanah, recite before me <i>Malchuyot, Zichronot</i>, and <i>Shofarot</i>.<br><i>Malchuyot</i> - so that you will crown Me as King over you.<br><i>Zichronot</i> - so that I will remember you for good.<br>By what means? Through the shofar."These blessings are described in the following halachot.<br>The prayers mentioning the sacred nature of the holiday and the sacrifices which were offered in the Temple are also included in the <i>Malchuyot</i> blessing.</i> [ - at this point,] the shofar is sounded three times;<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., one series of <i>teki'ah, teru'ah, teki'ah</i> required by the Torah. Though this point is disputed in the Mishnah, <i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 32a relates that this opinion (quoted in the name of Rabbi Akiva) was the accepted practice in Yavneh.</i> <i>Zichronot</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">the shofar is sounded three times - the second series of <i>teki'ah, teru'ah, teki'ah</i> required by the Torah.</i> - the shofar is sounded three times; <i>Shofarot</i><sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">the shofar is sounded three times - the final series of <i>teki'ah, teru'ah, teki'ah</i> required by the Torah.</i> - the shofar is sounded three times - [and concludes the <i>Amidah</i> with] the <i>Avodah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">literally, "the service," i.e., the blessing <i>R'tzey</i>, which asks God to accept our prayers and restore the Temple service.</i> an acknowledgement [of God's wonders],<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">the blessing <i>Modim</i>.</i> and the priestly blessing.
These three intermediate blessings recited on Rosh Hashanah and Yom Kippur of the <i>Yovel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah (<i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 26b) states: "Rosh Hashanah and the <i>Yovel</i> are identical regarding the sounding of the shofar and the blessings."</i> - <i>Malchuyot, Zichronot</i>, and <i>Shofarot</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The blessings must also be recited in this order (<i>Magen Avraham</i> 593:4).</i> - are each dependent on each other.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., a person who cannot recite all three blessings should not recite any. Generally, a person who does not know all the blessings of the <i>Amidah</i> should recite those that he knows. However, on the musaf service of Rosh Hashanah, a person should begin the <i>Amidah</i> only if he knows the entire service (<i>Magen Avraham</i> 593:2). (See also the <i>Bi'ur Halachah</i>.)</i><br>In each of these blessings, one is required to recite ten verses<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 32a explains that the number ten alludes to the ten expressions of praise used by King David in Psalm 150; or, alternatively, to the ten statements of creation; or, alternatively, to the ten commandments. The Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 4:7) explains that the ten verses of <i>Malchuyot</i> refer to David's ten expressions of praise; the ten <i>Zichronot</i>, to the ten calls to repentance proclaimed by Isaiah; and the ten <i>Shofarot</i> to the ten sacrificial animals offered in the Temple on Rosh Hashanah.<br>One may recite additional verses if one desires (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 591:4). Indeed, the text of the blessings generally recited for <i>Malchuyot</i> and <i>Shofarot</i> includes eleven quotes.</i> reflecting the content of the blessing<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">as explained in the commentary on the previous halachah</i> - three verses from the Torah,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Torah is on a higher level of holiness, its verses are mentioned first.</i> three from the Book of Psalms, and three from [the words of] the prophets.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The commentaries question why the Book of Psalms is given prominence over the words of the prophets, when generally the prophets' statements are considered on a higher level of holiness. Among the resolutions offered to this difficulty is that chronologically, the Psalms were written before the prophecies quoted (<i>Tosafot, Rosh Hashanah</i> 32a).<br>Examples of the verses recited are mentioned in the following halachah and commentary.</i> One concludes with a verse from the Torah.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">because of the dearness of the Torah. <i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 32b explains that this was the custom of the experienced Sages of Jerusalem.</i> Should a person conclude with a verse from the prophets, he fulfills his obligation.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">However, one should preferably conclude with a verse from the Torah. This is our present practice.<br>From the statements of the Rambam and the relevant passages from the Mishnah and Talmud, it would appear that originally there was no standard text containing universally accepted verses for these blessings. Rather, certain verses were commonly accepted and would be recited by almost all those who prayed. Others were left to the preference of each individual.<br>(One must remember that machzorim were uncommon in this period, and most of the prayers were recited by heart. Thus, the verses chosen by an individual might often have been those which he had been able to commit to memory.)</i><br>Should a person recite only one verse from the Torah, one from the sacred writings, and one from the prophets,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Thus mentioning three verses in each blessing, one from each division of the <i>T'nach</i></i> he fulfills his obligation.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">after the fact. However, at the outset it is desirable to recite all ten verses.</i> Even if he states: "In Your Torah, God, our Lord, it is written..." and recites one verse from the Torah and concludes, nothing further is necessary.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The commentaries have questioned the Rambam's statements, which appear self-contradictory. In the previous clause, he stated that one must recite three verses - one from the Torah, one from the prophets, and one from the writings - to fulfill one's obligation, while this clause implies that the recitation of one is sufficient.<br>The source for the difficulty is the following quotes: The Mishnah, <i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 32a, states:One should not recite fewer than ten verses for <i>Malchuyot</i>, ten for <i>Zichronot</i>, and ten for <i>Shofarot</i>. Rabbi Yochanan ben Nuri says: "If one recites three - three of each - one fulfills his obligation."<i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 35a quotes Rav Chanan'el as saying in the name of Rav:Even if he states: "In Your Torah, it is written..." [and recites one verse], nothing further is necessary.From the Talmud, it appears that both these statements are accepted as halachah. The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> explains that the Rambam interprets Rabbi Yochanan ben Nuri's requirement of three verses as the total for all three blessings. Thus, for each blessing, one must state "In Your Torah, it is written..." and recite a single appropriate verse. [This surely differs from the Rambam's statements in his commentary on the Mishnah.]<br>The Ramban, Rabbenu Nissim, and others explain that Rav Chanan'el agrees with Rabbi Yochanan ben Nuri and thus, in addition to "In Your Torah, it is written...," he also requires one to say: "In Your holy writings, it is recorded...," and: "by your prophets, it was proclaimed...," mentioning an appropriate verse in each instance. (This opinion is quoted by <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 586:8 and the <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 591:11.)<br>Alternatively, one may explain the Rambam's position as follows: Ideally, one should recite ten verses for each blessing; after the fact, three are sufficient, as stated by Rabbi Yochanan ben Nuri. When does this apply? Once one has recited at least one verse from the Torah. However, if one has not recited any verses, it is sufficient to say "In Your Torah, it is written..." and recite a single verse from the Torah, as Rav Chanan'el said in the name of Rav.<br>The flexibility regarding the number of quotes in each blessing was only granted in the previous generations. Since at present we have adopted a standard set of blessings, a person should not delete verses at will (<i>Magen Avraham</i> 591:6; <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 591:8).</i>
One should not recite [verses for] <i>Malchuyot, Zichronot</i>, and <i>Shofarot</i> which describe [Divine] retribution;<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Since Rosh Hashanah is a day of judgement, we are careful not to mention any factors that might lead to negative repercussions. For this reason, no confessional prayers are recited on Rosh Hashanah, and extreme care is taken regarding all aspects of our prayers and speech. (See Maharsha, <i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 32b.)</i> for example, for <i>Zichronot</i>, [Psalms 78:39]: "And He remembered that they were mere flesh...";<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">It is uncertain why the Rambam mentions a verse for <i>Zichronot</i> before one of <i>Malchuyot</i>.</i> for <i>Malchuyot</i>, [Ezekiel 20:33]: "With outpoured anger, I will reign over you...";<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 32a mentions that this prophecy concludes with the redemption of our people; since it is associated with anger and wrath, it is not fit to be mentioned on Rosh Hashanah.</i> for <i>Shofarot</i>, [Hoshea 5:8]: "Blow the shofar in Givah..."<br>Similarly, <i>Zichronot</i> which are merely associated with an individual [should not be mentioned] even if they are of a positive nature - for example, [Psalms 106:4]: "Remember me, O God, amidst the favor of Your people," [Nehemiah 5:19]: "Remember me, my God, for good."<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Even though on Rosh Hashanah each man is judged individually, and his own personal future is determined (see <i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 16b; <i>Hilchot Teshuvah</i> 3:3), it is desirable to look beyond these individual matters and concentrate our prayers on the welfare of the Jewish people as a whole.</i><br>Verses using the verb פקד - for example, [Exodus 3:16]: "I have surely taken note..."<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 32a also mentions another verse - Genesis 21:1: "and God granted providence to Sarah" - as an example of this principle. The Talmud objects to this verse because it is of an individual nature. Though it continues to resolve that difficulty, explaining that God's granting providence to Sarah effected the future of the entire Jewish people, the Rambam nevertheless avoided the problem by choosing the verse which is clearly of general import.</i> - may not be included as <i>Zichronot</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The meaning of פקד is often "remember." Indeed, the Targum of the cited verse in Genesis is דכיר, the Aramaic for "remembered." Furthermore, the Talmud mentions that Rabbi Yosse allowed verses using the verb פקד, and although Rabbi Yehudah objected, in differences of opinion between the two, Rabbi Yosse's position is generally favored. Nevertheless, since there are many verses that use the verb זכר, there is no difficulty in establishing a practice that will be accepted by all opinions (<i>Beit Yosef</i> 591).</i> One may mention verses that refer to retribution to be visited upon the gentile nations - for example, [Psalms 99:1]: "God is King, the nations will tremble..."; [Psalms 137:7]: "Remember, O God, against the Edomites, the day of Jerusalem"; or [Zechariah 9:14]: "God, the Lord, will sound the shofar and proceed in a southerly storm-wind."<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Despite this decision, only the latter verse is included in the text of the blessings we recite on Rosh Hashanah.</i><br>All the following verses: [Deuteronomy 6:4]: "Hear Israel, God is our Lord, God is one"; [Deuteronomy 4:35]: "To you, it has been revealed that you may know..."; and [Deuteronomy 4:39]: "And you shall know today and draw it close to your heart..." express the concept of God's sovereignty.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">These verses all express the oneness of God and creation; thus they emphasize how "He is unique and one, with none that compare to Him. His Kingship permeating all dominion." (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 591:11).</i> Even though [these verses] do not explicitly mention His kingship, they are equivalent to [Exodus 16:18]: "God will rule forever and ever"; and [Deuteronomy 33:5]: "When He became King in Jeshurun."<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shemot Rabbah</i> 48:4 states that this verse refers to Moses. Though the Rambam accepts the principle that Moses served the people as a king (see <i>Hilchot Beit HaBechirah</i> 6:11), he does not necessarily view this verse as the source for that concept.<br>The verses which have been accepted in the standard text for these blessings are as follows:<br><I>MALCHUYOT</I><br>TorahAnd you shall know today and draw it close to your heart that God is the Lord in the heavens above and the earth below. There is nothing else (Deuteronomy 4:39).<br>God will rule forever and ever (Exodus 15:18).<br>He does not look at iniquity in Jacob or see wrongdoing in Israel; God, his Lord, is with him and the desire of the King is in him (Numbers 23:21).<br>And there was a King in Jeshurun and the heads of the people assembled, together with all the tribes of Israel (Deuteronomy 33:5).<i>Psalms</i>For sovereignty is God's and He rules over the nations (22:29).<br>God is King. He has clothed Himself with grandeur; the Lord has girded Himself with strength. He has established the world firmly so that it will not falter (93:1).<br>Lift up your heads, O gates; be upraised, eternal doors, so the glorious King may enter. Who is the glorious King? God, strong and mighty, God, mighty in battle. Lift up your heads, O gates; be upraised, eternal doors, so the glorious King may enter. Who is the glorious King? The God of hosts. He is the glorious King for eternity (24:7-10).<i>Prophets</i>Thus said God, King and Redeemer of Israel, the God of hosts, "I am the first and I am the last. Aside from Me, there is no God" (Isaiah 44:6).<br>Deliverers will ascend Mount Zion to judge the mountain of Esau and the kingship will be God's (Ovadiah 1:21).<br>God will be King over the entire earth. On that day, God will be One, and His name, One (Zechariah 14:9).<i>Concluding Verse</i>Hear Israel, God is our Lord, God is One (Deuteronomy 6:4).<I>ZICHRONOT</I><br><i>Torah</i>And God remembered Noah and all the beasts and all the livestock that were with him in the ark. And God caused a wind to pass over the earth, and the waters were calmed (Genesis 8:1).<br>God heard their outcry. And God remembered His covenant with Abraham, with Isaac, and with Jacob (Exodus 2:24).<br>I will remember My covenant with Jacob, also my covenant with Isaac, and also My covenant with Abraham, and I will remember the land (Leviticus 26:42).<i>Psalms</i>He has made a remembrance of His wonders, gracious and merciful is God (111:4).<br>He provides sustenance to those who fear Him. He will remember His covenant forever (111:5).<br>For them, He remembered His covenant and He relented in accordance with His abundant kindness (106:45).<i>Prophets</i>Go and call out in the ears of Jerusalem, saying: "Thus said God, 'For you, I remember the devotion of your youth, the love of your bridal days, as you went after Me in the desert, in an uncultivated land' (Jeremiah 2:2).<br>I will remember My covenant with you [made] in the days of your youth, and I will fulfill it for you as an everlasting covenant (Ezekiel 16:60).<br>"Is Ephraim not My beloved son? Is he not a precious child? For whenever I speak of him, I recall him even more. The inner parts of my being stir for him. I will surely have compassion on him," says God (Jeremiah 31:19).<i>Concluding Verse</i>For them, I will remember the covenant with their ancestors, whom I took out from the land of Egypt before the eyes of the nations to be their Lord. I am God (Leviticus 26:45).<I>SHOFAROT</I><br>TorahAnd it was on the third day, at dawn. There was thunder and lightning and a heavy cloud on the mountain. The sound of the shofar was very strong and all the people in the camp trembled (Exodus 19:16).<br>And the sound of the shofar proceeded, becoming stronger. Moses spoke and God answered Him out loud (Exodus 19:19).<br>And all the people saw the voices and the flames, the sound of the shofar and the mountain smoking. The people saw and recoiled and stood off from afar (Exodus 20:15).<i>Psalms</i>The Lord ascends through <i>teru'ah</i>, God through the sound of the shofar (47:6).<br>Sound off with trumpets and the call of the shofar before the King, God (98:6).<br>Blow the shofar on the new moon, on the appointed time of our sacred day. For it is a statute for Israel; an ordinance of the Lord od Israel (81:4-5)<br>Praise God. Praise the Almighty in His holiness. Praise Him in the firmament of His strength. Praise Him for His mighty acts. Praise Him according to His abundant greatness. Praise Him with the call of the shofar. Praise Him with harp and lyre. Praise Him with timbrel and dance. Praise Him with stringed instruments and flute. Praise Him with loud cymbals. Praise Him with resounding cymbals. Let every being with a soul praise God. Halleluyah (150).<i>Prophets</i>All those who inhabit the world, who dwell on earth: When the banner is raised on the mountain, you will see. When the shofar is sounded, you will hear (Isaiah 18:3).<br>And it shall be on that day, that a great shofar will be sounded, and those who were lost in the land of Asshur and those who were banished in the land of Egypt shall come and bow down to God on the holy mountain in Jerusalem (Isaiah 27:13).<br>And God will appear over them and His arrow shall go forth like lightning. God, the Lord, will sound the shofar and proceed in a southerly storm-wind (Zechariah 9:14-15).<i>Concluding Verse</i>And on the days of your rejoicing; on your holidays, and on your Rosh Chodesh days, you shall sound the trumpets over your burnt-offerings and over the sacrifices of your peace offerings, and they shall be a remembrance for you before your Lord. I am God, your Lord (Numbers 10:10).</i>
The following is the commonly accepted custom for blowing the shofar on Rosh Hashanah<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in Halachah 7 and the commentary, the Sages had originally ordained that a person hear the shofar blasts required by the Torah in the midst of the musaf service. However, as evident from <i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 16a-b, even in Talmudic times the custom mentioned by the Rambam was already the general practice.</i> in the communal services:<br>After<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The laws pertaining to an individual are mentioned at the conclusion of Halachah 12.</i> the Torah is read<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Tefillah</i> 13:10 states:The common custom is to read on the first day: "And God showed providence to Sarah..., and on the second day: "And God presented a trial to Abraham..."As stated in that halachah, the haftarah is recited after the Torah reading is concluded.</i> and returned to its place,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Though at present, Ashkenazic custom is to blow the shofar in the presence of the Torah scrolls, the Rambam's son, Rav Avraham, supports his father's decision in the <i>Ma'aseh Rokeach</i>, explaining that it is not proper to make any interruptions between the shofar blowings which precede musaf and those within the prayer service.</i> the congregation is seated.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">This practice is mentioned in <i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 16a and the <i>Sh'eltot</i> of Rav Achai Gaon. However, today it is Ashkenazic custom to stand when hearing these shofar blasts. Nevertheless, based on this original practice, these are still referred to as <i>teki'ot meiyoshav</i> - "the <i>teki'ot</i> sounded while seated."</i> One person stands<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbenu Manoach questions the need for the person blowing the shofar to stand. He offers two possible explanations:<br>a) as a mark of deference to the community;<br>b) in respect for the blessing recited, as implied by Nehemiah 8:4.<br>The <i>Hagahot Maimoniot</i> quotes the Jerusalem Talmud (the location of the source in the Jerusalem Talmud is a matter of question) as drawing an analogy (גזרה שוה) between the blowing of the shofar and the counting of the Omer. Hence, just as the Omer must be counted while standing, the shofar should be sounded in a similar position.</i> and recites the blessing:<br>Blessed<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">as required before the fulfillment of any positive commandment (<i>Hilchot Berachot</i> 11:1,2,8).</i> are You, God, Lord of the universe, who has sanctified us with His commandments and commanded us to hear the sounding of the shofar.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in Chapter 1, Halachah 1, the fundamental aspect of the mitzvah is hearing, not blowing, the shofar. Nevertheless, the <i>Sh'eltot</i> suggests concluding the blessing "to blow the shofar" and the <i>Sefer Mitzvot HaGadol</i> advises the text "concerning the blowing of the shofar."</i><br>The entire congregation responds "Amen." He then recites the blessing <i>shehecheyanu</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">as is necessary before the fulfillment of any mitzvah performed infrequently (<i>Hilchot Berachot</i> 11:9).</i> and the entire congregation responds "Amen" to him. Thirty shofar blasts are sounded in the proper order.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">as explained in Halachah 3.</i><br>Kaddish is recited.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">"The chazan always recites Kaddish before and after every prayer service (Rambam, <i>Seder Tefillot kol Shanah</i>)."</i> The congregation stands<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">and remain standing during the repetition of the the <i>Amidah</i> as well. Therefore, the shofar blasts sounded during these prayers are referred to as <i>teki'ot m'umad</i> (the shofar blasts sounded while standing).</i> and recites the musaf service.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">in a hushed tone.<br>The <i>Tur</i> relates that there were varying customs regarding the recitation of the musaf service in a hushed tone. In certain communities, it had been customary for the congregation to recite the musaf prayers as on other festivals without reciting <i>Malchuyot, Zichronot</i>, and <i>Shofarot</i>. Only the chazan would recite the latter. However, even in the <i>Tur's</i> time, the prevailing custom was that even the congregation recited these three blessings.<br>The <i>Sh'lah</i> records the custom of sounding the shofar for <i>Malchuyot, Zichronot,</i> and <i>Shofarot</i> while the congregation recites the <i>Amidah</i>. However, this custom is not accepted in all communities.</i> After the chazan completes the fourth blessing<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., Malchuyot - a teki'ah, three shevarim, a [series of] teru'ot, and a teki'ah are sounded once. - The rationale for this practice and the different customs associated with these shofar blasts are discussed in Halachah 12 and its commentary.<br>In the text of prayers with which the Rambam concludes <i>Sefer Ha'ahavah</i>, he also mentions the recitation of the prayer <i>Hayom Harat Olam</i>.</i> - i.e., <i>Malchuyot</i> - a <i>teki'ah</i>, three <i>shevarim</i>, a [series of] <i>teru'ot</i>, and a <i>teki'ah</i> are sounded once.<br>He then recites the fifth blessing<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., Zichronot. After he completes the blessing, a teki'ah, three shevarim, and a teki'ah are sounded. He then recites the sixth blessing - i.e., Shofarot. After he completes the blessing, a teki'ah, a [series of] teru'ot, and a teki'ah are sounded once, and he concludes the prayers.</i> - i.e., <i>Zichronot</i>. After he completes the blessing, a <i>teki'ah</i>, three <i>shevarim</i>, and a <i>teki'ah</i> are sounded. He then recites the sixth blessing - i.e., <i>Shofarot</i>. After he completes the blessing, a <i>teki'ah</i>, a [series of] <i>teru'ot</i>, and a <i>teki'ah</i> are sounded once, and he concludes the prayers.
The person who sounds the shofar while they are sitting<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">the thirty shofar blasts mentioned in Halachah 3.</i> also sounds the shofar according to the order of blessings<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., <i>Malchuyot, Zichronot</i>, and <i>Shofarot</i></i> while they stand.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Hai Gaon also mentions this practice. The <i>Kolbo</i> explains that it is appropriate, since "when a person begins a mitzvah, we tell him to complete it" (Jerusalem Talmud, <i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 1:8). Nevertheless, the <i>Magen Avraham</i> 592:7 writes that in many communities, it has become customary to divide the honor of shofar blowing between two individuals.</i> He<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., the person blowing the shofar. Rabbi Yitzchak Alfasi and Rabbenu Asher write that not only he, but the entire congregation should refrain from speaking. The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> does not accept this view, yet it is quoted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 592:3.</i> should not speak<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Permission is granted to speak about matters required for the prayers or for blowing the shofar (Ramah).</i> between the shofar blasts while the congregation is seated and those sounded while they stand.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">for since they are both part of one mitzvah, it is improper to interrupt between them.</i> If he did talk between them, even though it is a transgression, he does not repeat the blessing.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Since, essentially, the mitzvah is fulfilled by hearing the <i>teki'ot</i> before musaf, and the others are sounded only "to confuse Satan" (Rabbenu Manoach).</i>
It is logical that each series of shofar blasts should be sounded three times for every blessing,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Because of the doubt mentioned in Halachah 3, it would be proper for us to blow three series of blasts - i.e., <i>teki'ah, shevarim, teru'ah, teki'ah; teki'ah, shevarim, teki'ah</i>; and <i>teki'ah, teru'ah, teki'ah</i> - at the conclusion of each blessing.</i> as [the shofar] was sounded while [the congregation] was seated.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">the shofar blasts sounded before musaf.</i> However, since they<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">the congregation</i> satisfied every possible doubt<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">regarding the proper manner of blowing the shofar, as explained in Halachah 3.</i> [by hearing] the shofar while seated, there is no need for the congregation to repeat them<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">these shofar blasts</i> in their entirety during the order of blessings. Rather, it is sufficient for them to hear one series for each blessing,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">as explained in Halachah 10</i> and they will thus have heard the shofar during the order of blessings.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">and fulfilled the requirement of the Sages mentioned in Halachah 7.<br>In practice, a number of different customs are followed regarding this matter. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 592:1) suggests following the pattern described by the Rambam, but sounding each series three times, rather than once. The Ramah writes that Ashkenazic custom is to sound one series - <i>teki'ah, shevarim, teru'ah, teki'ah</i> - after each blessing. However, the <i>Sh'lah</i> suggests blowing three series of blasts - i.e., <i>teki'ah, shevarim, teru'ah, teki'ah; teki'ah, shevarim, teki'ah</i>; and <i>teki'ah, teru'ah, teki'ah</i> - at the conclusion of each blessing.</i><br>All of the above applies only to a congregation.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">as specifically stated in Halachot 7 and 10.</i> However, there is no [set] custom regarding an individual. He fulfills his obligation whether or not he hears [the shofar blasts] during the order of blessings,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Some authorities object to the permission granted by the Rambam for an individual to interrupt his recitation of the the <i>Amidah</i> in order to hear the sounding of the shofar. However, the Rambam's opinion is based on his interpretation of <i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 34b. (See <i>Ma'aseh Rokeach</i>.)</i> whether seated or standing.
The blessings<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Malchuyot, Zichronot,</i> and <i>Shofarot</i>.</i> are not dependent on the <i>teki'ot</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore, if it is impossible for a person to hear the shofar blown, he should still attempt to recite or hear the blessings.</i> nor are the <i>teki'ot</i> dependent on the blessings.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Thus, if it is impossible for a person to hear the blessings, he should still attempt to hear the shofar being blown.</i><br>When there are two cities<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">in one it is known that there will definitely be someone to recite the nine blessings - recited in the musaf service on Rosh Hashanah</i> - in one it is known that there will definitely be someone to recite the nine blessings, but there is no one to blow the shofar<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">in the other, a doubt exists whether or not there will be someone to blow the shofar, one should go the latter, since the sounding of the shofar is a requirement of the Torah - as mentioned in Chapter 1, Halachah 1</i> - in the other, a doubt exists whether or not there will be someone to blow the shofar, one should go the latter, since the sounding of the shofar is a requirement of the Torah, while the blessings<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">as is the prayer service in its entirety</i> are of Rabbinic origin.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., surely if one could be sure of fulfilling the Torah commandment, one should do so at the expense of the fulfillment of the Rabbinic commandment. However, even if, as in the case described, there is a doubt whether one can fulfill the Torah commandment, one should take that risk, rather than forego the opportunity entirely, even if doing so will cause one to negate the fulfillment of the Rabbinic commandment.</i>

Chapter 4

These are the required measurements of a sukkah: Its height<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the height of its inner space, without including the height of the <i>s'chach</i> (<i>Eruvin</i> 3b)</i> should not be less than ten handbreadths<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">anything less is not considered to be a dwelling fit for human habitation (See <i>Sukkah</i> 4a.) <i>Sukkah</i> 4b-5a derives the concept as follows: The ark and the <i>kaporet</i> covering it were ten handbreadths high. This constituted a line of demarcation between the place where the <i>Shechinah</i> was manifest and the area below it. Thus, we see that a height of ten handbreadths is sufficient for an independent area.<br>Rabbenu Manoach establishes a closer relationship between the above concept and a sukkah, noting that Exodus 25:20 describes how the wings of the cherubs "shall shield the <i>kaporet</i>," using the verb סככים, which has the same root as the word <i>s'chach</i>. The beginning of the height of the cherub's "shield," ten cubits, is the minimum of the height for our <i>s'chach</i>.</i> nor more than twenty cubits.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Any structure more than twenty cubits high can only be built as a permanent dwelling. Hence, it is unfit to serve as a sukkah, which must be of a temporary nature. (See <i>Sukkah</i> 2a and the Rambam's commentary on the Mishnah.)[ It must be noted that any structure less than 20 cubits high can serve as a sukkah even if its walls are of a permanent nature.]<br>A cubit is 48 centimeters according to <i>Shiurei Torah</i> and 57.6 centimeters according to the <i>Chazon Ish</i>.</i> Its area should not be less than seven handbreadths by seven handbreadths.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">the minimum size necessary to contain a person's head, the majority of his body [6 handbreadths by six handbreadths], and a small table [a handbreadth by a handbreadth] (Jerusalem Talmud, <i>Sukkah</i> 2:8). A handbreadth is 8 centimeters according to <i>Shiurei Torah</i>, and 9.6 centimeters according to the <i>Chazon Ish</i>.<br>The Rambam explains the requirement of seven handbreadths by seven hand breadths as follows: The first three hand breadths are not of consequence because of the principle of <i>l'vud</i> and four additional handbreadths are required since the smallest area of halachic consequence is four cubits by four cubits (Commentary, to the Mishnah, <i>Sukkah</i> 1:1).<br>If the sukkah is not seven handbreadths in either length or width, it is invalid, even if its area equals 49 square handbreadths. If it is round in shape, it must be sufficiently large to encompass a square seven by seven (See Halachah 7). This size is required because if either of the dimensions were less, it would not be considered a dwelling fit for human habitation (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 634:1-2; <i>Magen Avraham; Taz</i>).</i> [There is no maximum limit to] its area,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The commentaries point to <i>Sukkah</i> 27b: "All Israel is fit to sit in one sukkah," as the source for this statement. A sukkah large enough to contain "all Israel" must possess a sizable area.</i> and one may increase it [to include] a number of <i>millim</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">A <i>mil</i> is approximately a kilometer in contemporary measure.</i><br>A sukkah which<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">does not meet the above requirements and</i> is less than ten handbreadths high, smaller than seven handbreadths by seven handbreadths [in area], or taller than twenty cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 633:2-5) mentions a number of techniques by which a sukkah of this size can be made kosher, by decreasing the height of its inner space.</i> - even [if the increase or decrease] is of the slightest amount<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">is invalid.</i> - is invalid.
A sukkah which does not possess three walls is invalid.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Though <i>Sukkah</i> 6b mentions Rabbi Shimeon's opinion, which requires four walls, all authorities accept the more lenient view. The Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Sukkah</i> 1:1) explains that their difference of opinion is based on the exegesis of Isaiah 4:6:There will be a sukkah that will serve as a shadow from the heat during the day, a place of refuge, and a cover from storm and from rain.The Sages maintain that the verse refers to three different activities, and hence require three walls. Rabbi Shimeon counts "a cover from storm and from rain" as two different activities, and hence requires four walls.</i> However, if it has two complete walls<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., walls of at least seven handbreadths long, so that the minimum requirements for the sukkah's area mentioned in the previous halachah can be met</i> perpendicular to each other in the shape of [the Greek letter]<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbenu Manoach notes that a <i>gamma</i> has the same shape as the Hebrew letter <i>dalet</i> (see accompanying drawing) and asks why the Sages did not use that letter to refer to the intended shape. He explains that the very letters of the Hebrew alphabet are endowed with holiness. Hence, the Sages did not want to use them as an example to refer to a mundane matter.</i> <i>gamma</i>, it is sufficient to construct a third wall that is [only] slightly more than a handbreadth wide and place it within three handbreadths<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 16b teaches that whenever there is a gap of three handbreadths or less between two entities, the principle of <i>l'vud</i> applies. The gap is considered to be closed and the two parts connected. Thus, the third wall is considered to be more than four handbreadths long, hence spanning more than half of the length required for the third wall. Therefore, it is acceptable (Rabbenu Nissim).</i> of one of the two walls.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">See the accompanying diagram.<br>The Rabbis have posed an abstract question: Is the minimum requirement for a sukkah three walls (including one which is incomplete), or must a sukkah have four walls, however, the Torah was lenient enough to consider a sukkah of this nature as comparable to one of four walls.<br>The <i>Marcheshet</i> brings support for the latter view, quoting <i>Sukkah</i> 7b, which states that since the third wall only a handbreadth in size is considered to be a wall with regard to the laws of sukkah, it is also considered to be a wall with regard to the laws regarding a private domain on the Sabbath. In the latter instance, four walls are necessary.</i> Also, one must construct the likeness of an entrance,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">to complete this third wall. This is necessary...</i> since it<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">the sukkah</i> does not possess three complete walls.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">However, if the three walls are complete - i.e., at least seven handbreadths in length - as in the accompanying diagram, no "likeness of an entrance" is required.<br>The <i>Bayit Chadash (Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 430) explains that the requirement of a "likeness of an entrance" is a Rabbinic ordinance, and, according to Torah law, a sukkah is acceptable as long as the third wall is a handbreadth as required.</i><br>We have already explained in <i>Hilchot Shabbat</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">16:19</i> that wherever the term "a likeness of an entrance" is used, it may be a rod on one side, another rod on the opposite side, and a third above,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 630:2) states that if the two rods reach the <i>s'chach</i>, a third rod is unnecessary. (See <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 630:12.)</i> even though it does not touch them.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">as depicted on page 67.</i>
If the two walls<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">each being seven handbreadths or more long</i> were parallel to each other and there was an open space between them, one should construct a wall slightly more than four handbreadths wide<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">as depicted in the accompanying diagram.<br>Since the two walls are not connected, the third wall which "connects" them must be longer (<i>Sukkah</i> 7a).</i> and place it within three handbreadths<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">so that it will be considered <i>l'vud</i>.</i> of one of the two walls; then, [the sukkah] is kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The third wall is considered to be seven cubits long itself - the four cubits of actual length and the three cubits between it and the wall that are added to it, because of the principle of <i>l'vud</i> (Rambam, Commentary on the Mishnah, <i>Sukkah</i> 1:1).</i> However, it is necessary to construct "the likeness of an entrance."<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">between the wall of four handbreadths and the wall further removed from it.<br>This opinion is not universally accepted; and some authorities do not require a "likeness of an entrance" for such a sukkah. Nevertheless, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 630:3) quotes the Rambam's opinion as halachah.<br>The Ramah mentions that the "likeness of an entrance" is required only when the third wall needs the principle of <i>l'vud</i> for it to be considered seven handbreadths long. However, if the wall is actually seven handbreadths or more long, nothing more is necessary.</i><br>If the rods of the <i>s'chach</i> of the sukkah extend beyond the sukkah and one wall extends with them,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">As portrayed in the accompanying diagram, the sukkah has two walls, each at least seven handbreadths long, joined to each other at a right angle. The third wall is also joined to the other at a right angle; however, its length exceeds that of the wall opposite it, and thus, the fourth side of the sukkah, which remains open, slants at an angle.</i> they are considered to be [part of] the sukkah.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">sitting under the extension is considered to be the same as sitting under the portion enclosed by three walls. (See <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 631:7.)</i>
Walls<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">at least seven handbreadths high</i> which are connected to the roof of the sukkah, but do not reach the earth:<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">See accompanying diagram.</i> If they are more than three handbreadths above the earth, they are invalid;<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 16a records a difference of opinion between the Sages whether a "hanging partition" - i.e., a partition that is not connected to the earth - is kosher. The halachah does not accept such a partition, because animals can crawl under it. (See <i>Shabbat</i> 97a.) Nevertheless...</i> if the distance is less than that,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">three handbreadths</i> they are kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Because of the principle of <i>l'vud</i>, it is considered as if they actually reach the ground.</i><br>[The following rules apply] should the walls be connected to the earth, without reaching the <i>s'chach</i>:<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">See the accompanying diagram.</i> If they are ten handbreadths high, they are kosher even<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">they are considered to be a viable partition. Therefore...</i> though are removed several cubits from the roof,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">We say גוד אסיק מחיצתא - "Pull up and raise the partition;" i.e., it is considered as if the partition has been extended upward and reaches the <i>s'chach</i>. See <i>Sukkah</i> 4b for an additional discussion of this concept.<br>Though this concept is accepted, we do not say מחיצתא גוד אחית - "Pull down and extend the partition." Hence, in the first clause of this halachah, the partition is not acceptable until it reaches three cubits of the ground.<br>[It is possible to distinguish between the two cases as follows. A partition on the ground that must be extended upward serves as a functional divider. In contrast, a partition hanging downward cannot adequately fulfill its purpose unless it reaches within three handbreadths of the ground. (See also the <i>Or Sameach's</i> resolution of a difficulty arising from <i>Eruvin</i> 79a.)]</i> provided they<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">the walls</i> are positioned below the end of the roof.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., the <i>s'chach</i>, so that the <i>s'chach</i> covers them.<br>Furthermore, the walls may even be slightly removed from the <i>s'chach</i>. (See <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 630:9.)</i> If the roof<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">the <i>s'chach</i></i> was separated from the wall by more than three handbreadths, it is invalid;<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">for then the distance is too great for the <i>s'chach</i> to be considered the roof of these walls. In such an instance, not only is sitting under the open portion of no halachic import, the entire sukkah is invalid. Nevertheless, a sukkah in which the distance is...</i> less than this amount is kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">because of the principle of <i>l'vud</i>. This concept also applies when the walls are as high as the <i>s'chach</i>. Even though the walls extend beyond the <i>s'chach</i> the sukkah is kosher. Nevertheless, one should not sit under the open portion.</i><br>If one suspended a partition which is slightly more than four handbreadths high at a distance of less than three handbreadths from the earth<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">and thus, it is viewed as connected to the earth because of the principle of <i>l'vud</i>.</i> and a distance of less than three handbreadths from the roof,<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">and thus, it is viewed as connected to the <i>s'chach</i> because of the principle of <i>l'vud</i>.</i> it is kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 16b explains that even though we must rely on the principle of <i>l'vud</i> twice, the sukkah is still kosher.</i>
When a person constructs his sukkah among the trees,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">This halachah does not deal with the problem of the branches and leaves of the trees interfering with the <i>s'chach</i>. That issue is dealt with in Chapter 5, Halachah 12. Rabbenu Manoach and others have also raised questions whether the <i>s'chach</i> should be supported by the trees. (See the commentary on the following halachah.)</i> using the trees as walls, it is kosher if:<br>a)<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">the following two conditions are met</i> they are strong enough<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">by nature</i> - or he tied them and reinforced them so that they would be strong enough<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">that they would not be shaken by the wind at all times; - even if the wind is not strong enough to uproot them, it should not cause them to sway back and forth</i> - that they would not be shaken by the wind at all times; and<br>b) he filled [the space] between the branches<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">this translation of the word אמיר is taken from Isaiah 17:6.</i> with hay and straw,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">weaving them together so that the wall would be a solid continuum (<i>Sukkah</i> 24b)</i> tying them<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">the hay and straw fillers</i> so that they will not be shaken by the wind.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">so that they will not be shaken by the wind.</i><br>[This is necessary,] for any partition that cannot stand before a normal<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">However, a sukkah's inability to stand before a hurricane wind does not invalidate it.</i> land wind<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">in contrast to sea winds, which are more powerful (<i>Sukkah</i> 23a)</i> is not considered to be a partition.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">This law also has implications with regard to the Sabbath laws. (See <i>Hilchot Shabbat</i> 16:15, 24.)</i>
If a person constructs his sukkah on top of a wagon<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">although the wagon moves and is not fixed in one place (Rashi, <i>Sukkah</i> 22b)</i> or on the deck of a ship,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 23a relates:A person who constructs his sukkah on the deck of a ship: Rabban Gamliel deems it invalid; Rabbi Akiva deems it kosher.<br>Once Rabban Gamliel and Rabbi Akiva were traveling on a ship. Rabbi Akiva arose and constructed a sukkah on the ship's deck. The next morning, the wind came and blew it over. Rabban Gamliel asked Rabbi Akiva: "Akiva, where is your Sukkah?"As apparent from the narrative, such a sukkah need not be strong enough to withstand sea winds. However, even according to Rabbi Akiva, it must be strong enough to withstand normal land winds, as explained in the previous halachah.</i> it is kosher, and one may ascend to it on the festival.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., on the first day of Sukkot in Eretz Yisrael and on the first and second days in the diaspora.</i> If one constructs it on the treetops or on a camel's back, it is kosher,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 23a explains that Rabbi Yehudah objected to the use of such a sukkah, explaining that since it was not fit to be used on all seven days of the holiday (because of the prohibition against using it on the first day), it should not be used at all.<br>Our halachah follows Rabbi Meir's opinion. He accepts Rabbi Yehudah's motivating principle, but explains that in the case at hand, there is no inherent difficulty with using such a sukkah throughout the holiday. The only reason it is not used on the first day is an external factor - a Rabbinic decree - which should not affect the halachic status of the sukkah itself.</i> but one may not ascend to it on the festival,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Surely, this prohibition also applies on the Sabbath.<br>From the Rambam's words, it appears that the restriction applies when the floor of the sukkah is actually in the tree. In contrast, Rashi (<i>Shabbat</i> 154b), <i>Tosafot, Sukkah</i> 22b and the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> explain that even if the sukkah is on the ground and only the <i>s'chach</i> is supported by the tree, it is forbidden to use such a sukkah on the festival, lest one place utensils on the <i>s'chach</i>, and thus make use of the tree.<br>The <i>Magen Avraham</i> (628:6) quotes this opinion, but states that at present it is no longer customary to place articles on the <i>s'chach</i>. Therefore, it is permitted to use such a sukkah. His opinion is quoted by <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 628:7, and the <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 628:17, with one qualification. At the outset, it is desirable not to use an article as support for <i>s'chach</i> unless it is, itself, fit to be used as <i>s'chach</i>. Hence, since the trees themselves are not fit to be used as <i>s'chach</i>, they should not be used as its supports.</i> because climbing on a tree or animal is forbidden on a festival.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Beitzah</i> 5:2 relates:All the [prohibitions] which we are obligated [to observe as] <i>sh'vut</i>...on the Sabbath, we are obligated [to observe] on a festival. These are [the activities prohibited] as <i>sh'vut</i>: We do not climb a tree; we do not ride an animal...In his commentary on that Mishnah, the Rambam writes:"We do not climb a tree" - a decree lest we uproot [it];<br>"we do not ride an animal" - a decree lest we break off a branch to lead it.</i><br>If some of the walls were the result of human activity and some were trees, we consider [its structure]. We may ascend to any [sukkah] where, if the trees were taken away, it would be able to stand with the walls that were built by man alone.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam quotes this general principle from the Mishnah, <i>Sukkah</i> 23a. The Mishnah adds examples to express the concept more clearly:Two [walls] that were the result of human activity and one [wall] from the tree, or two [walls] from the tree and one [wall] that is a result of human activity.</i>
A sukkah that does not possess a roof is invalid.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">because a dwelling even of a temporary nature must have a roof.</i> To what does this refer? A sukkah whose walls are joined to each other like a hut;<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi (<i>Sukkah</i> 19b) explains that this is a reference to a hunter's hut. See Diagram A;</i> alternatively, when the side of the sukkah is placed against the wall.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See Diagram B.</i> However, if it<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">the sukkah</i> has a roof, even only a handbreadth in width,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">between the two walls, as depicted in Diagram C. As long as it has a roof at least a handbreadth wide, the remainder of the roof may be slanted.</i> or if one lifted the side of the sukkah close to the wall a handbreadth above the ground,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">so that handbreadth is considered to be a wall, as depicted in the diagram below.</i> it is kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">for the fact that the roof is slanted does not disqualify the sukkah.<br>The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, Rabbenu Manoach, and others explain that though leniency is taken and such a sukkah is allowed, it must still possess all the dimensions required of a kosher sukkah mentioned in Halachah 1 of this chapter. Accordingly, at least six handbreadths of the slanted roof must itself be kosher for use as <i>s'chach</i>, and it must be more than 16 handbreadths long, so that it will be of the required height. When the Sukkah meets these qualifications, one is permitted to eat and sleep within it. (See also Ramah, <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 631:10.)</i><br>A round sukkah<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">if its circumference is large enough to contain a square seven handbreadths by seven handbreadths, it is kosher even though it has no corners. - <i>Sukkah</i> 7b records an opinion which disqualifies such a sukkah because it is not fit for use as a permanent dwelling. Nevertheless, the halachah does not follow this view.<br>On this basis, we can understand the placement of this law. On the surface, it would be more appropriate to state this law as part of Halachah 1, which describes the dimensions of a sukkah. However, the Rambam structured the order of his halachot according to their motivating principles. Thus, the first clause of the halacah describes the construction of a sukkah whose shape causes it to be deemed unacceptable even as a temporary dwelling. In contrast, this clause describes a sukkah whose shape is abnormal, but acceptable for temporary purposes.</i> - if its circumference is large enough to contain a square seven handbreadths by seven handbreadths, it is kosher even though it has no corners.
Should one place <i>s'chach</i> over an <i>exedrah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">A structure frequently employed in Roman architecture, and which was quite common in Jewish homes as well. There were a number of possible forms of this structure. Our halachah (in contrast to <i>Hilchot Shabbat</i> 17:35) deals with the following structure: A roof is placed between two walls, and within this roof a hollow place is left to allow sunlight to enter. Pillars are placed at each of the corners of the hole. The question is whether such a structure can serve as a sukkah if one placed <i>s'chach</i> over the hole. See diagram A.</i> which has projections [extending from its pillars],<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">At times these pillars were ornamented with artistic projections. See the diagrams below.</i> it is kosher,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Because of the projections, the opening of the ceiling is considered to be a third wall extending over the entire width of the <i>exedrah</i> and reaching the ground (<i>Sukkah</i> 18b) Thus, greater leniency is granted in this instance than in Halachah 3 of this chapter, where a partition four handbreadths in width is required.<br>Needless to say, as evident from Halachah 5:14, the walls of the <i>exedrah</i> cannot be more than four cubits removed from the <i>s'chach</i>.</i> regardless of whether the projections can be seen from the inside<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">of the Sukkah</i> - although they cannot be seen from the outside<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">See diagram B.</i> - or whether they can be seen from the outside<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">of the<br>Sukkah</i> - although they cannot be seen from the inside.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">See diagram C.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 630:8) quotes the Rambam's statements as halachah. However, the Ramah advises against constructing a sukkah in this manner.</i>
If it<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">the sukkah constructed in the <i>exedrah</i></i> does not have projections [extending from its pillars]<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">as explained in the previous halachah</i> it is not valid, because it resembles a sukkah constructed in an alley,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">which is described in Halachah 3.</i> because it has [walls on] only the two sides of the <i>exedrah</i>. The middle of the <i>exedrah</i> does not have a wall and there are no projections opposite it.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Hence, the principle that the opening of the roof is considered to be a third wall, reaching to the ground, is not applied in this instance. Rabbenu Manoach notes that different principles apply in this context from those in the laws of <i>eruvin</i>, but explains that there is a basic difference between the two contexts. Here, the two walls were not constructed for the sake of the sukkah. In contrast, in the laws of <i>eruvin</i>, the walls were constructed for the sake of creating an enclosure.</i>
Should a person place <i>s'chach</i> over an alleyway which possesses a <i>lechi</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Shabbat</i> 17:2 states:How is one permitted [to carry articles] in a closed alleyway?<br>One constructs a <i>lechi</i> (vertical pole) [at the entrance] to the fourth side or one lays a beam (<i>korah</i>) across [the span of the fourth side].Rabbenu Manoach explains that although the alleyway is closed on three sides, were it not for the special provisions mentioned in this halachah, it would not be acceptable, because in this instance the <i>s'chach</i> is placed more than four cubits away from the end of the alleyway. Hence, generally, as explained in Chapter 5, Halachah 14, such a sukkah would not be acceptable.</i> or a well which possesses <i>pasim</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Shabbat</i> 17:27 states:When a total of eight <i>pasim</i> [partitions] are constructed around a well, two connected to each other at each of its corners, they are considered to be walls. Thus, even though on each side the open portion exceeds the closed, since the four corners are closed, it is permitted to fill up water from the well and to water an animal.<br>What is the height of each of these <i>pasim</i>? Ten handbreadths; their length must be at least six handbreadths and between each <i>pas</i>, there should be...no more than thirteen and one third cubits.Thus, there are no complete walls to this structure, and without the special provision granted by this halachah, it would not be acceptable.<br><i>Sukkah</i> 7b explains that each of these situations possesses an advantage over the other: The alley possesses an advantage in that it has two complete walls. In contrast, the well possesses an advantage in that it has partitions of some sort on each of its four sides. Hence, it is necessary to state both these laws, and neither could be derived from the other.</i> it is considered a kosher sukkah only on the Sabbath of the festival.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Tzafenat Paneach</i> explains this as applying only to the days which precede the Sabbath. However, once the sukkah is acceptable on the Sabbath, it is also deemed kosher for the remaining days of the festival. (This view is not accepted by other authorities.)</i> Since this <i>lechi</i> and these <i>pasim</i> are considered to be partitions with regard to the Sabbath laws,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">On the Sabbath, one is allowed to carry only within an enclosed domain. Since the situations mentioned in this halachah are not actual enclosures and achieve that status only because of a Rabbinic ordinance, the application of these principles is confined to the Sabbath itself. Thus, on the Sabbath...</i> they are also considered to be partitions with regard to the laws of sukkah.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">and the sukkah is considered to be enclosed by three walls as required.
