However, throughout the remainder of the holiday, when the Rabbinic ordinances are not in effect, they are not considered to be enclosed structures. Hence, they are not acceptable as a sukkah.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 630:7) quotes these laws as halachah. (Also see the Ramah's notes.)</i>
Should a person implant four poles in the four corners of the roof and place <i>s'chach</i> upon them,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">See the accompanying diagram.</i> it is kosher. Since he placed the <i>s'chach</i> [above] the edge of the roof, we consider that the lower walls ascend to the edge of the <i>s'chach</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">because of the principle of גוד אסיק מחיצתא, it is considered as if the walls of the house have been extended upward, as explained in Halachah 5.<br>This halachah is based on the statements of <i>Sukkah</i> 4b. There are differences in the versions of the relevant passage possessed by the Rambam and Rav Sherirah Gaon, on the one hand, and those possessed by other Sages, on the other hand. Because of those textual differences, the Ra'avad, Rabbenu Manoach, and others have questioned the Rambam's decision. (See <i>Maggid Mishneh</i>.)<br>These differences caused the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 630:6) to reject the Rambam's decision. The <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 630:31 states that even according to the Rambam, the poles must be placed exactly at the edge of the roof. If they are placed further in, even when they are within three handbreadths of the edge, the sukkah is not acceptable.</i>
A sukkah which has many entrances and many windows in its walls is kosher even though the open portion<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Any open portion less than three handbreadths in length is considered to be closed, based on the principle of <i>l'vud</i> (<i>Hilchot Shabbat 16:17). The Magen Avraham</i> 630:1 explains that regarding the laws of sukkah, this principle applies only when one constructs four walls. However, if the sukkah has only three walls, the principle of <i>l'vud</i> cannot be applied, to consider spaces less than three handbreadths in length to be closed.</i> exceeds the closed portion, provided there is no opening larger than ten cubits.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 7a states:[The laws governing] a wall of a sukkah resemble [those governing] the wall [of an enclosure] on the Sabbath...There is an additional [stringency to the laws] of the Sabbath that does not apply to a sukkah. On the Sabbath, [a wall] is permitted only when the enclosed portion is greater than the open portion. This does not apply to a sukkah.The Rambam describes the laws governing a wall on the Sabbath as follows (<i>Hilchot Shabbat</i> 16:16):Every wall whose open portion exceeds its enclosed portion is not considered to be a wall. However, if the open portion is equal to the closed portion, it is permitted, provided that none of the open portions exceeds ten cubits.Based on the above, the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> and the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> explain that just as concerning the laws of the Sabbath, the closed portion of a wall must exceed its open portion, so, too, concerning two of the walls of the sukkah. The leniency allowing a wall of the sukkah to be counted as a wall even though the open portion exceeds the closed portion applies only concerning the third wall. Just as other leniencies (see Halachot 2 and 3) are granted concerning the third wall, this leniency is also allowed.<br>Others explain that the intent is that even when the open portion of all four walls exceeds the closed portion, the sukkah is kosher, while on the Sabbath such an enclosure is not acceptable. This interpretation of the Talmud's statements is advanced by Rabbenu Asher and is quoted as halachah by the <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 630:5.</i><br>If there is an opening larger than ten cubits, it is necessary that the closed portion exceed the open portion, even though [the opening] is constructed in the form of an entrance.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Shabbat</i> (ibid.) states:If the open portion is constructed in the form of an entrance, even if it is more than ten cubits long it does not negate the wall, provided the open portion does not exceed the closed portion.However, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 362:10, 630:5) follows the opinion of <i>Tosafot</i> and other authorities, who are willing to accept a wall as kosher even though it has a wide opening, provided it is constructed in the form of an entrance.<br>The <i>Sefer Hashlamah</i> presents a third view, accepting an open portion more than ten cubits long in the form of an entrance as part of the wall of a sukkah, but not concerning the laws of the Sabbath.<br>The Ramah concludes his discussion of this halachah by stating that since these laws are somewhat complicated, it has become customary to build whole walls without any open portions. If one has only a minimum amount of wood, it is preferable to build three complete walls, rather than to construct four walls leaving open spaces.</i>
A sukkah whose inner space<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">the space between the ground and the <i>s'chach</i>.</i> exceeds twenty cubits [is not acceptable].<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">as stated in Halachah 1. Indeed, the question may be raised: Why did the Rambam state these two halachot so far removed from each other?</i> Should one reduce it<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">the sukkah's inner space</i> [by placing] pillows and coverings [on the floor], it is not considered to be reduced.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">for these are merely temporary additions that will later be removed.</i> [This applies even if] one considered them a permanent<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> interprets "permanent" literally. However, the <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 633:11 explains that according to one opinion, the definition of "permanence" is for the duration of the Sukkot holiday. (See also Ramah, <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 358:2.)</i> part of the sukkah.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Most people would not consider leaving these articles in the sukkah permanently. Accordingly, a particular individual's desire to do so is not taken into consideration, and the space is not considered to be reduced (<i>Sukkah</i> 4a).</i> If one reduced the space using straw and considered it as a permanent part of the sukkah, [the space] is considered to be reduced.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">However, if one laid straw on the floor of the sukkah without having such an intention, the space is not considered to be reduced (ibid.). In his commentary on <i>Ohalot</i> 15:6, the Rambam writes: "In general, one will have in mind to remove straw."<br>The <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 633:13 emphasizes that one should not reduce the space of the sukkah on the first day of the festival because of the holiday prohibitions. (See also Rabbenu Manoach.)</i><br>Needless to say, the above applies if one used earth and considered it to be a permanent part of the sukkah. However, if one [merely brought in] earth with no specific intention, [its space] is not considered to be reduced.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 633:4) quotes these decisions as halachah and adds that one must verbally express the desire to make the earth or straw part of the sukkah. The <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> (ibid.) states that many later authorities considered an unspoken intention as sufficient.</i><br>If it<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">the inner space of a sukkah</i> was twenty cubits high,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">and, therefore, unacceptable</i> but branches [from the <i>s'chach</i> descend within the twenty cubits, [the following principle applies:] If its shade would be greater than its open portion<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">this is the minimum measure required by the Mishnah (<i>Sukkah</i> 1:1) for <i>s'chach</i> to be kosher.</i> because of these branches alone,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., were the upper portion of the <i>s'chach</i> to be removed, the branches which hang down would create sufficient shade</i> it is considered as having thick <i>s'chach</i><sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., the <i>s'chach</i> is considered to begin at the low branches and to have been piled high.</i> and is kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 663:2) quotes this law as halachah.</i>
When [a sukkah is more than twenty cubits high, but] one builds a bench next to the middle wall extending across its entire span<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">and there are fewer than twenty cubits between the bench and the <i>s'chach</i></i> - if the width of the bench is equal to the minimum width of a sukkah,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">seven handbreadths, as in Halachah 1 above. See Diagram A.</i> it is kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi (<i>Sukkah</i> 4a) maintains that not only the area above the bench, but the entire sukkah is kosher, as evident from the last clause of Halachah 3. Though Rabbenu Nissim and other authorities maintain that only the area above the bench may be used as a sukkah, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 633:5) accepts Rashi's view.</i><br>Should one build the bench next to the middle wall along [one] side, if there are four cubits between the bench and the [opposite] wall, it is unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">because the area around the bench is surrounded by only two walls.</i> If there are fewer than four cubits, it is kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">because of the principle explained in the latter clause. In this instance as well, there is a disagreement between the Rabbis whether the entire sukkah is kosher or only the area above the bench. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 633:6) follows the view that only the area above the bench is acceptable for use as a sukkah. See Diagram B.</i><br>Should one build the bench in the middle [of the sukkah], if there are more than four cubits from the edge of the bench to any of the sides [of the sukkah],<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">that wall is too far removed from the bench to be considered to be a wall around it. Hence,</i> it<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">the sukkah in its totality, even the area above the bench...</i> is not acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">for in this instance, the sukkah must have four walls.</i> If there are fewer than four cubits,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">between the bench and the walls of the sukkah...</i> it is kosher. It is considered as if the walls touch the bench,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., the area from the bench to the wall is considered to be an extension of the wall. Thus, this halachah is the converse of the principle of דופן עקומה (<i>Dofen Akumah</i> literally, a crooked wall) mentioned in Chapter 5, Halachah 14.<br>There and in his commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Sukkah</i> 1:10), the Rambam explains that the principle of <i>Dofen Akumah</i> allows us to consider materials on the roof of the sukkah which are unacceptable as <i>s'chach</i> to be extensions of the wall, thus creating an L-shaped - i.e., "crooked" - wall.<br>Here, the Rambam explains that the opposite is also true. As long as there are no more than four cubits between the bench and the wall, the ground of the sukkah can also be considered to be an extension of its wall. (See <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>.)</i> and<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Since...</i> the distance from the bench to the <i>s'chach</i> is less than twenty cubits.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">as required in Halachah 1, the area above the bench is considered as having four walls and kosher <i>s'chach</i>.</i><br>If one constructs a pillar<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">ten handbreadths or more high, because otherwise, the pillar could never be considered to be a significant domain (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).</i> [within a sukkah<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">removed four cubits or more from the walls. (Otherwise, it would be considered to be kosher because of the principles mentioned above.)</i> whose <i>s'chach</i> is more than twenty cubits high, the following rule applies]: Even though it is of the minimum size required of a sukkah,<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">seven handbreadths by seven handbreadths</i> it is unacceptable,<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 4b explains that Abbaye desires to consider such a sukkah to be kosher, based on the principle of גוד אסיק מחיצתא mentioned in Halachah 11 - i.e., the walls of the pillar would be considered as extending upward until the <i>s'chach</i>. Ravva answered him that, in this instance, that principle cannot be applied...<br><b>for -</b> in contrast to those of a house<br><b>its -</b> the pillar's<br><b>walls are not discernible. -</b> See the <i>Chiddushim</i> of Rav Chayim Soloveichik, Halachah 11.<br><b>Thus, it is as if there is kosher s'chach above the pillar without any walls.</b></i> because its walls are not discernible. Thus, it is as if there is kosher <i>s'chach</i> above the pillar without any walls.
[The following rule applies] when [the inner space of the sukkah] was less than ten [handbreadths high]<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">the minimum height required by Halachah 1.</i> and one dug [into the ground of the sukkah] to create<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">a pit at least seven handbreadths by seven handbreadths (Rabbenu Manoach, <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 633:10) with...</i> an [inner space] of ten [handbreadths]:<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">between it and the <i>s'chach</i>.</i> If there are three handbreadths from the edge of the pit until the wall [of the sukkah], it is not acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">One might assume that as long as there are less than four cubits between the edge of the pit and the sukkah, the sukkah would be kosher, because of the principle mentioned in the previous halachah; i.e., the ground of the sukkah would be considered to be an extension of the wall. Nevertheless, <i>Sukkah</i> 4a differentiates between the two cases. In the situation described in the previous halachah, the wall was of the proper size; the only question was its proximity to the sukkah. In this instance, there is no halachically acceptable wall to begin with.</i> If there is less than that [amount], it is kosher,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 633:29 quotes authorities who maintain that only the area within the pit is kosher, and one does not fulfill the mitzvah of eating or sleeping in the sukkah by performing these activities in the portion covered by the <i>s'chach</i> outside the pit.</i> because any [distance] less than three [handbreadths] is considered to be [insignificant, and the two entities are considered to be] adjacent [to each other], as explained in <i>Hilchot Shabbat</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">14:7, which explains the principle of <i>l'vud</i> mentioned above.</i>
The walls of the sukkah are kosher [although made] from all [substances].<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah (<i>Sukkah</i> 12b) mentions many substances which are not acceptable as <i>s'chach</i> (as mentioned in the following chapter), and concludes "all are fit to be used as walls."</i> All that is necessary is a barrier of any kind.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 630:1) explains that one should take care not to use substances that have an unpleasant odor or substances that will shrivel during the holiday, and thus cause the walls to be less than the required measure.</i> Even living beings [may serve that purpose.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 630:11) states that an animal used for this purpose must be tied so that it will not run away.</i> Thus,] a person can create a wall [of the sukkah] by using a colleague so that he can eat, drink, and sleep in the sukkah, for which his colleague is serving as a wall<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Tzafnat Paneach</i> raises the question whether the person serving as the wall can also fulfill the mitzvah of eating in such a sukkah. He quotes a number of passages from which one might infer that he may.</i> [even] on the holiday.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The latter term refers to the first day of Sukkot - and in the Diaspora, the second day - and the day of Shemini Atzeret, when the laws prohibiting work and the Rabbinic ordinances extending those restrictions must be observed.</i><br>The above applies when one employs the person as a wall without his conscious knowledge. However, it is forbidden to create [a wall by using a person]<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad and the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> explain that this refers only to the third wall of the sukkah. If a sukkah has three kosher walls, a person may employ a colleague to serve as the fourth wall on the holiday, even though the latter is conscious of what he is doing. The Ramah (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 630:12) states this as halachah.</i> when the latter is conscious of the fact on the holiday.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Shabbat</i> 16:23 states:It is permitted to create a barrier of men, one standing next to the other, on the Sabbath, as long as the people who are standing are not conscious of the fact that they are serving as a barrier.Although it is forbidden to create an enclosure on the Sabbath, since the person serving as the barrier is unaware of what he is doing, no transgression is involved. It is his intention, and not that of the person using the sukkah, which is significant (Ra'avad). In contrast, when the person serving as the wall is conscious of his acts, it is considered as if he has created a structure of substance (<i>Magen Avraham</i> 630:19).<br>The <i>Magen Avraham</i> (ibid.) also states that leniency is granted only when humans serve as the walls. It is absolutely forbidden to create a wall using an animal on the holiday.</i> Nevertheless, it is permitted during the other days of the festival.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">with the exception of the Sabbath; i.e., there is no essential difficulty with the use of such a sukkah, the only problem is the Rabbinic prohibition mentioned above.</i><br>Similarly, a person may create a fourth wall<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Since a sukkah is kosher when it possesses only three walls, the addition of the fourth wall is not halachically significant. We are allowed to add to a temporary structure on the Sabbath.</i> from utensils on the holiday. However, he should not create a third wall using utensils<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">It is permitted to create a utensil from human beings, because one does not normally create an enclosure in such a manner. In contrast, an enclosure is frequently made from utensils (Rabbenu Manoach).</i> on the holiday, because, [by doing so], he is making the sukkah fit for use, and it is forbidden to create [even] a temporary tent on the holiday.

Chapter 5

[There are requirements regarding] the <i>s'chach</i> of a sukkah, and not all substances are acceptable [to be used for this purpose].<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Some of the requirements mentioned by the Rambam were established by Torah law, while others are Rabbinic ordinances, as explained below.</i> For <i>s'chach</i>, we may use only a substance which grows from the ground,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., is an agricultural product and is neither found naturally - e.g., metals - nor produced from animals nor manufactured synthetically;</i> has been detached from the ground,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">and is not still connected to its source of nurture;</i> is not subject to contracting ritual impurity,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This excludes articles used as food and those that have been fashioned into utensils. These three requirements are alluded to by Deuteronomy 16:13: "Make the Sukkot holiday for yourselves for seven days, when you gather in from your grain and grapes..."<br><i>Sukkah</i> 12a explains that the verse implies that the <i>s'chach</i>, the essential element of the sukkah, must resemble "the leftovers from the grain and grape harvest."<br>(The expression "alluded to" was used rather than "derived from" on the basis of the Rambam's commentary on the Mishnah, <i>Sukkah</i> 1:4. That expression implies that the concept cannot be derived from the verse per se. Rather, it was transmitted as a halachah l'Moshe miSinai, and the Biblical verse is merely an allusion.)<br>The Rambam gives examples of entities which are not acceptable as <i>s'chach</i> on the basis of these principles in the following halachah.</i> does not have an unpleasant odor,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">As implied by the following halachah, this and the following clause are Rabbinic decrees. <i>S'chach</i> with an unpleasant odor will not create an inviting holiday environment. Indeed, substances with an unpleasant odor should not be used for the sukkah's walls either.</i> and does not have elements which fall off and wither constantly.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">for a person will not be pleased to have portions of the <i>s'chach</i> falling into his food. The <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 629:39 states that the prohibition applies to elements that fall off naturally. However, if they fall off only when subjected to wind, there is no difficulty in using them.</i>
When a person uses as <i>s'chach</i> a substance which does not grow from the ground, is still connected to the ground, or is subject to contracting ritual impurity, [the sukkah] is not acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">for these requirements stem from the Torah itself. The particular aspects of these requirements are described in this and the following halachot.</i><br>However, if he transgressed and used as <i>s'chach</i> a substance which has elements which fall off and wither,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 13a gives the shrub known as hollow as an example of such a substance.</i> or which possesses an unpleasant odor,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 12b gives the plant known as wormwood as an example of such a substance.</i> it is kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">after the fact, for these are only Rabbinic requirements. The <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 629:38 emphasizes that even if other <i>s'chach</i> is available, there is no need to remove the <i>s'chach</i> with the unpleasant odor. However, the <i>Pri Megadim</i> stresses that if the odor is so unpleasant that no one would bear it in his home, it is forbidden to use such a sukkah.</i> [Our Sages]<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 13a</i> said only that one should not use these as <i>s'chach</i> lest one leave one's sukkah and depart. One must take care that the branches and leaves should not descend within ten handbreadths of the ground,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">the minimum height for a sukkah prescribed in Chapter 3, Halachah 1.</i> so that one will not be uncomfortable<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 4a describes this as "a disgusting dwelling, unfit for human habitation."</i> when using the sukkah.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's phraseology leaves room for the interpretation that this is a suggestion, but not an absolute requirement. (See Rabbenu Manoach.) Nevertheless, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 633:9) states that such a sukkah is unacceptable for use.</i><br>If one used metals,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">even if they have not been made into utensils, but are still in a raw state in which they are unfit to contract ritual impurity;</i> bones, or hides<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">even if they have not been made into garments, but are still in a raw state in which they are unfit to contract ritual impurity;</i> as <i>s'chach</i>, it is unacceptable because these do not grow from the ground. If one suspended vines and the like over it until they made a sukkah, it is unacceptable, because they were not uprooted [from the ground].<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The laws governing the use of such plants as <i>s'chach</i> after they have been hung over the sukkah and then detached from the ground are discussed in Halachah 12.</i><br>Should one use wooden utensils,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">In particular, this refers to wooden utensils that have a receptacle or are wide and other articles are frequently placed upon them - e.g., a table. However, wooden utensils that do not fall into these categories are not subject to contracting ritual impurity, and, hence, may be used for <i>s'chach</i>. An example of such utensil is a ladder. The Rambam (<i>Hilchot Keilim</i> 4:1) declares that a ladder is not subject to contracting ritual impurity. Hence, according to his opinion, there is no difficulty with using it as <i>s'chach</i>.<br>(It must be emphasized that other authorities question the Rambam's decision on a ladder and maintain that a ladder may be subject to contracting ritual impurity. Hence, it is preferable not to use it as <i>s'chach</i>. See the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> and Ramah, <i>Orach Chayim</i> 629:7.)</i> mats that were made to lie on,<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">However, mats that were made for use as <i>s'chach</i> or for shade may be used as <i>s'chach</i>. Halachah 6 discusses the laws which apply when a mat was made without any specific intention.</i> and the like as <i>s'chach</i>, it is unacceptable, because they are subject to contracting ritual impurity. Similarly, using broken<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">e.g., utensils with holes of sufficient size to render them no longer subject to contracting ritual impurity. (See <i>Hilchot Keilim</i>, Chapter 6.)<br><b>and worn out -</b> e.g., garments that have worn out and are less than three fingerbreadths by three fingerbreadths in size and, hence, are no longer subject to contracting ritual impurity (<i>Sukkah</i> 16a; <i>Hilchot Keilim</i> 22:21).<br><b>utensils as s'chach is unacceptable. -</b> This is a Rabbinic decree on the basis of the following rationale...<br><b>Since these substances were subject to ritual impurity -</b> previously,<br><b>[the latter law was instituted] lest one use broken pieces -</b> from utensils<br><b>which have not yet attained a state of [unquestionable] purity -</b> i.e., are still subject to contracting ritual impurity.<br><b>as s'chach</b>.</i> and worn out utensils as <i>s'chach</i> is unacceptable. Since these substances were subject to ritual impurity, [the latter law was instituted] lest one use broken pieces which have not yet attained a state of [unquestionable] purity.
If one used foods<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">This refers only to foods for humans. Food which is eaten primarily by animals is not subject to contracting ritual impurity (<i>Hilchot Tum'at Ochlin</i> 1:1) and hence may be used as <i>s'chach</i> (<i>Taz</i> 629:12).</i> as <i>s'chach</i>, it is unacceptable, because they are subject to contracting ritual impurity.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">There is a slight imprecision in the Rambam's statements. Produce is not subject to contracting ritual impurity until it comes into contact with liquid. (See Leviticus 11:38; <i>Hilchot Tum'at Ochlin</i> 1:1-2.) Nevertheless, foods are not fit to be used as <i>s'chach</i> even though they have never come into contact with water (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 629:1; <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 629:28).</i> [When one uses] branches from a fig tree which contain figs, runners from a grape vine which contain grapes, branches of a date palm which contain dates and the like,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., the difficulty being that although the branches are proper <i>s'chach</i>, the fruit is unfit to be used for that purpose...</i> [the following rules apply:] We see - if the waste<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">the branches and leaves</i> is more than the food;<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">the fruit</i> then we may use them as <i>s'chach</i>. If not, we may not use them as <i>s'chach</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbenu Manoach explains that these decisions are based on the principle that according to Torah law, mixtures are categorized according to the majority (בטל ברב).<br>On the basis of <i>Sukkah</i> 13b-14a, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 629:10) explains that sometimes the stems of the fruit are considered part of the fruit, and on other occasions part of the branches. The <i>Magen Avraham</i> 629:12 explains that generally, a stem is not considered to be longer than three handbreadths. However, there are certain instances (e.g., grains) when a longer measure is considered. Hence, it is proper to be stringent in this regard. (See also <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 629:15-16.)<br>The apparent contradiction between this halachah and Halachah 13 is discussed in the commentary on that halachah.</i><br>If one uses as <i>s'chach</i> vegetables<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> explains that this refers to vegetables that are used as animal food, and hence were not excluded by the previous clause of this halachah. Rabbenu Manoach and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 629:12) explain that this refers to vegetables that are eaten by humans. <i>Sukkah</i> 13b appears to support the latter interpretation, stating:The vegetables with which a person can fulfill his obligation on Pesach... [can] invalidate a sukkah, [because they are considered] to be vacant space.</i> which, when they dry up, will wither, and none of their substance will remain,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., the vegetables will dry up and wither during the Sukkot festival, leaving the sukkah with more vacant space than shade (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> even though they are now fresh, their place is considered to be vacant, as though they did not exist.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">According to the <i>Maggid Mishneh's</i> interpretation, the explanation of the law is straightforward. It teaches us that rather than consider the vegetables to be non-kosher <i>s'chach</i>, we consider their space to be vacant. The latter interpretation requires a slightly more intricate explanation. The vegetables are not considered to be non-kosher <i>s'chach</i>, which would invalidate the sukkah if they take up four handbreadths, as explained in Halachah 14. Rather, their space is considered to be empty. Hence, a space of three handbreadths is sufficient to invalidate the sukkah, as explained in Halachah 20.</i>
If one used as <i>s'chach</i> branches of flax which were not crushed and combed, they are kosher, because they are still considered to be wood.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The use of the word עץ, rendered as branches or wood, with regard to flax, has its source in Joshua 2:6, which describes how Rachav "hid them in branches of flax."</i> After the flax has been crushed and combed,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Rishon Letzion</i> questions the law when the flax has been crushed but not combed, and concludes that as long as it has not been combed, it is acceptable. Nevertheless, <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 629:5 does not accept this conclusion.</i> it may not be used as <i>s'chach</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Though it is not considered to be a garment and, hence, is not subject to contracting ritual impurity (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>; see also <i>Shabbat</i> 27b), it may nevertheless not be used as <i>s'chach</i>...</i> since its form has changed and it is as though it is no longer a product of the earth.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad disagrees with this principle and states that even before it is woven into a garment, flax is useful to stuff pillows and covers. Hence, it is subject to contracting ritual impurity and is thus unfit to be used as <i>s'chach</i>.<br>The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> points out a contradiction to the Ra'avad's logic. The "male arrows" mentioned in the following halachah are - like flax - prepared to be used for a purpose. Nevertheless, they are considered acceptable for use as <i>s'chach</i>.<br>Rav Kapach mentions two possible extensions of the difference of opinion between the Rambam and the Ra'avad:<br>a) paper or carton - According to the Rambam, it may not be used because it no longer resembles a plant produced from the earth. According to the Ra'avad, it might be acceptable, since it is not subject to contracting ritual impurity.<br>b) cotton wool - It may be used for the purposes mentioned by the Ra'avad and thus, according to his opinion, would be subject to contracting ritual impurity. Nevertheless, its natural form is preserved. Hence, according to the Rambam's opinion, it would be acceptable. (It must be noted that the <i>Magen Avraham</i> 629:3 and <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 629:5 maintain that after cotton has been combed, it is considered as though its form has been altered.)</i><br>One may use ropes made from palm bast or hemp and the like as <i>s'chach</i>, since their original form is unchanged and ropes are not considered to be utensils.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">nor are they subject to contracting ritual impurity. The <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 629:5 quotes this law as halachah, but adds that ropes of flax and canvas may not be used for this purpose.</i>
[The following rules apply] when one uses arrows as <i>s'chach</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The question whether the arrows are acceptable as <i>s'chach</i> or not revolves around another issue - their susceptibility to contracting ritual impurity.</i> Those which are "male"<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., the end of their body is sharpened and comes to a point, which is intended to be put inside a metal arrowhead.</i> are kosher;<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">for the arrow itself is considered a simple wooden utensil which is not susceptible to contracting ritual impurity. (See <i>Hilchot Keilim</i> 1:10.)<br>The <i>Magen Avraham</i> 629:2 states that this decision applies only before these arrows were placed in the arrowhead. If they have been placed in the arrowhead, even if subsequently removed they are no longer acceptable as <i>s'chach</i>.</i> those which are "female"<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., the end of their body is hollowed out for the arrowhead to be inserted within</i> are not acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">for they are subject to contracting ritual impurity.</i> Even though [ultimately,] they will be filled with iron,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Keilim</i> 2:3 states: "A receptacle which is made to be filled is not considered a receptacle." Thus, one might assume that these arrows would also not be considered as having a receptacle. The <i>Pri Ha'aretz</i> explains that since the iron arrowheads are often removed from these bodies, it is considered as though...</i> they have a receptacle. Hence, they are susceptible to contracting ritual impurity, as are all utensils with receptacles.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">It must be noted that Rashi and other commentators follow the simple interpretation of <i>Sukkah</i> 12b (the source for this law) and disagree with the Rambam's decision in <i>Hilchot Keilim</i>. They maintain that if a utensil has a receptacle, even if it will be permanently filled, it is subject to contracting ritual impurity.</i>
A mat of reeds, of raw rubber, or of hemp<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">if it is small, we may assume that it was made to lie on. - The commentaries note an apparent contradiction in the Rambam's words. <i>Hilchot Keilim</i> 25:13 states that even a small mat of reeds or hemp is not subject to contracting ritual impurity, because it is uncomfortable to lie on.</i> - if it is small, we may assume that it was made to lie on. Therefore, it may not be used for <i>s'chach</i> unless it was [explicitly] made for this purpose.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Since the mat was made with that specific intention, we do not follow the general principles, but rather judge it individually.</i><br>If it is large,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's statements are based on <i>Sukkah</i> 20a. It must be noted that his decisions are dependent on the version of the text of the Talmud he accepted. The Ra'avad and others interpret the word גדולה as related to the word גדיל (tassel). Thus, they explain that the Talmud is referring to a small mat. However, because it is made of thick tassels, rather than woven, it is not comfortable to lie on, and hence would most likely be used for shade. According to this opinion, any woven mat, even if it is large, is unfit to be used as <i>s'chach</i>. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 629:6) quotes the Rambam's interpretation.</i> we may assume that it was made for shade; therefore, it may be used for <i>s'chach</i> unless it was [explicitly] made to lie on.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Orach Chayim</i>, ibid.) mentions that in places where it is customary to affix mats permanently as the roofs for homes, they may not be used for <i>s'chach</i>. The Rabbis instituted this decree lest people remain in their homes on Sukkot without differentiating between them and a sukkah.</i><br>If it has a border,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">a small lip (<i>Sukkah</i> 20b)</i> even a large mat may not be used as <i>s'chach</i>, because it is considered to be a receptacle.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot, Sukkah</i> 20b quotes <i>Ketubot</i> 50b, which explains that such mats were used to collect dates.</i> Even if the border were removed, it may not be used as <i>s'chach</i>, because it would be considered to be a broken utensil.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">which, as explained in Halachah 2, may not be used as <i>s'chach</i> even though they are not susceptible to contracting ritual impurity.</i>
Boards which are less than four handbreadths wide<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">In many places throughout the Talmud, four handbreadths is established as the minimum size of an area. Hence, a board of that size is considered significant and may not be used as <i>s'chach</i> (<i>Sukkah</i> 14a).</i> may be used for <i>s'chach</i> even though they have been planed.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">smooth, and thus are fit to be used in their present state (<i>Sukkah</i> 15a; Rabbenu Manoach).</i> If they are more than four handbreadths wide, they should not be used as <i>s'chach</i>, even though they have not been planed. This is a decree [instituted] lest one sit under a roof and regard it as a sukkah.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">A person might think: "What is the difference between the sukkah and my house - they are both covered with boards?" This is surely a false assumption. As explained in the following halachah, since the boards of a roof were not placed there for the purpose of shade, but rather to be part of the permanent structure of the house, they cannot be considered to be <i>s'chach</i> (<i>Sukkah</i> 14a; Rabbenu Manoach).</i><br>If one placed a board which was more than four handbreadths wide over [a sukkah, the sukkah] is kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> explains that this applies only when the board is placed at the side of the sukkah. Thus, it could be considered to be an extension of the wall (דופן עקומה), as explained in Halachah 14. However, if it is in the midst of the sukkah, it is not acceptable. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> explains that this law can apply even in the midst of a sukkah, provided the sukkah already possesses its minimum size. His decisions in the <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 632:1 follow this interpretation.</i> However, one should not sleep under the board.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">According to the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i>, because it is part of the wall; according to the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, because it appears to be a separate domain.</i> A person who did sleep<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">or fulfilled any other of the activities which must be performed in the sukkah</i> under the board has not fulfilled his obligation.<br>There were boards that were four handbreadths wide, but less than four handbreadths thick. A person turned them on their side so that they would not be four handbreadths wide to use them as <i>s'chach</i>. This is not acceptable, because a board is unacceptable for use as <i>s'chach</i> whether one uses its width or thickness.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 14b explains that after the Sages forbade the use of such boards, they are considered to be equivalent to iron poles.<br>The <i>Magen Avraham</i> 629:22 writes that at present it is customary not to use boards as <i>s'chach</i>, even if they are less than four handbreadths wide. However, if there is no other <i>s'chach</i> available, one may use boards for that purpose even if they are more than four handbreadths wide.</i>
A roof which is not covered by a ceiling<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., the plaster and the stones - but rather has only boards fixed in place, is not acceptable, since they were not placed there for the purpose of a sukkah - as required by the following halachah</i> - i.e., the plaster and the stones - but rather has only boards fixed in place, is not acceptable, since they were not placed there for the purpose of a sukkah, but to be part of the house.<br>Therefore, if one<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">performed two activities:</i> lifted up the boards<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">from their place. (Though the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>Orach Chayim</i> 631:9 does not mention this requirement, the <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 631:4 does.)</i> and removed the nails<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">holding the boards in position. (See the Rambam's commentary on the Mishnah, <i>Sukkah</i> 1:7.) Through these actions, one is considered to have nullified the previous placement of the boards, and it is considered as though they have been placed there...</i> with the intent [that they serve] as a sukkah,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore</i> it is kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">for the boards are essentially fit to be used as <i>s'chach</i>. The only difficulty was the intention with which they were originally placed. A change of mind without an act is not sufficient, since, as explained in the commentary on the following halachah, we are required to "make" a sukkah and not use one which has already been made.</i> [This applies provided] that each board is not four handbreadths wide.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">as explained in the previous halachah. Rabbenu Asher states that in such a situation, the sukkah is acceptable even though the boards are more than four handbreadths wide.<br>He explains that in the previous instance, the reason the Sages forbade using such boards was to differentiate between them and the boards of a house. In the present instance, the fact that the person took apart the roof of his house obviously implies that he realizes that it may not be used as a sukkah. Hence, there is no need for such a decree. This opinion is accepted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, ibid.</i><br>Similarly, it is kosher if one removed a board from between two others and replaced it with kosher <i>s'chach</i>, with the intention [that it serve] as a sukkah.
A sukkah that was made for any purpose whatsoever - even if it was not made for the purpose of [fulfilling] the mitzvah - if it was made according to law,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 8b quotes a <i>baraita</i> which contains the latter statement and questions: "What does 'according to law' mean?.That it was made for the purpose of shade."<br>It appears that the Rambam uses the same expression, but with different implications. Since he explicitly states that the sukkah must be constructed for the purpose of shade, one might infer that the expression "according to law" is intended to include other concepts. Thus, it can be a reference to the requirements for a sukkah's size and the nature of the materials used for the <i>s'chach</i>, as mentioned in this and the previous chapter.</i> it is kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah, <i>Sukkah</i> 1:1, states: An old sukkah: The School of Shammai deems it unacceptable, while the School of Hillel rules it kosher<br>The commentaries explain that the term "an old sukkah" refers to any sukkah that was constructed for purposes other than the fulfillment of the mitzvah. In its discussion of this law, the Jerusalem Talmud requires that one must make an addition or change to the <i>s'chach</i>. Though that decision is not quoted by the Rambam, Rabbenu Asher mentions it and it is accepted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 636:1). Nevertheless, the <i>Magen Avraham</i> 636:1 explains that this is a desirable and proper step, but the sukkah is acceptable even though no changes have been made.</i> However, it must be made for the purpose of shade.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Isaiah 4:6 states: "It shall be a sukkah for shade from the heat...," thus defining the purpose of such a structure. A sukkah constructed for the purposes of modesty is not acceptable (Rabbenu Manoach).</i> Examples of this are sukkot made for gentiles, sukkot made for animals, and the like.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">which are outside the entire framework of mitzvot. <i>Sukkah</i> 8b also mentions the sukkot made for shepherds and watchmen. Though the latter are obligated to keep the mitzvah of sukkah, they did not necessarily construct their sukkot with that intent in mind.</i><br>In contrast, a sukkah that came about on its own accord<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">e.g., branches fell over a frame, and of course, any time a roof is covered for other purposes - e.g., the construction of a home, as mentioned in the previous halachah...</i> is unacceptable, because it was not made for the purpose of shade. Similarly, when a person hollows out a place in a heap of produce and thus makes a sukkah, it is not considered to be a sukkah, <sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkot</i> 12a explains that there is an added factor involved in this example. Deuteronomy 16:13 states: "Make a [celebration of] the Sukkot holiday for seven days." This refers to the construction of the sukkah and teaches that we must "make" a sukkah and not use what was already made. Therefore, despite the fact that the person hollowed out the space for the purpose of shade, the sukkah is not acceptable, because the produce was not originally placed there for that purpose.<br>We find a similar principle concerning <i>tzitzit</i>. Deuteronomy 22:12 states: "Make yourself tassels on the four corners of your garments." On the basis of this command, <i>Menachot</i> 40b teaches that it is unacceptable to tie <i>tzitzit</i> to a three-cornered garment and then cut a fourth corner, since we are required to make <i>tzitzit</i>, and not use what is already made.</i> because the produce was not piled there for this purpose.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Ba'al Hamaor</i> writes that if, originally, a person were to pile produce with the intention that later he would hollow out a sukkah, the sukkah would be acceptable. However, this opinion is not accepted by other authorities.</i> Accordingly, were one to create a space one handbreadth [high]<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">We find the measure of one handbreadth considered to define a structure with regard to the laws of ritual impurity. Accordingly, it is given significance in this context as well. (See <i>Sukkah</i> 16a.)</i> and seven [handbreadths]<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">by seven handbreadths</i> in area for the purpose of a sukkah, and afterwards hollow it out till it reached ten [handbreadths],<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">the minimum height of a sukkah, as explained in Chapter 3, Halachah 1.</i> it is kosher, since its <i>s'chach</i> was placed for the purpose of shade.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">and then, the original structure was merely expanded.</i>
We may not use bundles of straw, bundles of wood or bundles of reeds as <i>s'chach</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Although, according to the Torah's requirements, these would be considered proper <i>s'chach</i>...</i> This decree [was instituted]<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">by the Sages (<i>Sukkah</i> 12a)...</i> lest one place those bundles on one's roof to dry out, and then change one's mind and sit under them with the intent [that they serve as] a sukkah. The person did not place the <i>s'chach</i> there originally for the purpose of shade. Thus, it resembles a sukkah that came about on its own accord.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">which is unacceptable, as explained in the previous halachah.</i><br>If one<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">placed bundles of these substances on a roof with the intent that they would be used as <i>s'chach</i> and...</i> untied [the bundles], they are acceptable [for use as <i>s'chach</i>].<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 629:17) explains that a different rule applies if the bundles were originally placed on the roof to dry out. For them to be acceptable as <i>s'chach</i>, untying them alone is insufficient, and one must also shift the position of their contents.</i> A bundle is considered to be no fewer than twenty five units.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Thus, any lesser amount of these substances are acceptable as <i>s'chach</i> even though they are tied together.<br>The Rambam bases his statements on the Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Sukkah</i> 1:6). Though the Ra'avad maintains that a bundle may be composed of even a smaller number of units, the Rambam's opinion is accepted as halachah by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 629:15.</i>
Small bundles<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> questions the reason for the addition of the adjective, noting that if the bundles contain fewer than twenty five units, they are permitted, as stated in the previous halachah. Others mention that this refers to bundles of small branches.</i> that were tied together [to be sold] by number<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">to be untied immediately thereafter (<i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>Orach Chayim</i> 629:16).</i> may be used as <i>s'chach</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 13b relates that bundles of this nature were frequently used in Sura, and that the Sages permitted them to be used as <i>s'chach</i>.</i><br>Similarly, if one cuts off the top of a date palm and the branches are bound to it, it may be used as <i>s'chach</i>, because elements that are bound naturally are not considered to be bundles.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Ba'al Hamaor</i> questions whether this principle applies only with regard to <i>s'chach</i>, where it is logical to assume that a more lenient position would be taken, since the entire question revolves around a Rabbinic decree, or whether it also applies in all cases where Torah law itself requires a bundle.</i> Furthermore, even if one tied the tops of the branches from one side, and they thus appear to be a single bundle with one of its two ends bound naturally and the other bound as a result of human activity, it may be used as <i>s'chach</i>. A single tree which is bound up is not considered to be a bundle, but rather a single piece of wood, since [the branches] are bound together naturally.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">However, if one were to add even one branch and then tie them together, it would be considered to be a bundle (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 629:15).</i><br>Similarly, any knot which is not strong enough to hold when carried is not considered a knot [and the resulting bundle may be used as <i>s'chach</i>].<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 13b mentions this principle when explaining why bundles of willows whose upper tie was loosened could be used as <i>s'chach</i>.</i>
A person who constructs his sukkah under a tree is considered as though he built it within his home.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">and the sukkah is unacceptable. This applies only when the shade the tree produces exceeds the open area. However, if there is more open space under the tree than shade, the sukkah may be kosher.<br>The determination of whether such a sukkah is kosher depends on a number of principles, based on the interpretation of <i>Sukkah</i> 9b and 11a. The Rambam's interpretation of that passage, and thus the ground rules he establishes, differ from those accepted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 626:1-2).<br>In the following halachah, the Rambam deals with the resolution of the question when no effort has been made to correct the problem of the non-kosher <i>s'chach</i>. As will be explained, there his interpretation is contested by other authorities. In this halachah, the Rambam describes the rulings which govern the situation when an effort has been made to rectify the situation by detaching the branches from their source of nurture. These are based on the Mishnah, <i>Sukkah</i> 11a, and are also accepted by other Rabbinic authorities. (See the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>Orach Chayim</i> 626:2.)</i> <br>If one draped the leaves and branches of trees [over the sukkah], and then placed <i>s'chach</i> over them, and only afterwards detached them,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in Halachot 1 and 2, branches are fit to be used for <i>s'chach</i> only after they have been detached from their source of nurture.</i> [the following rules apply:]<br>If the amount of [kosher] <i>s'chach</i> exceeded [the branches], it is kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Though at present, the branches would be acceptable as <i>s'chach</i>, as explained in the commentary on Halachah 9, the Torah requires that when <i>s'chach</i> is originally put in place, it must be kosher. Otherwise, it is unacceptable even though steps were taken to correct the disqualifying factors. This is based on the principle that one must make a sukkah and not use one which is already made.<br>Nevertheless, since the prohibition against using these branches as <i>s'chach</i> does not relate to their essential nature, their presence may be nullified when there is a majority of kosher <i>s'chach</i>. This conforms to the principle of ביטול ברב.</i> If the amount of <i>s'chach</i> which originally was kosher did not exceed [the branches],<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">the presence of the branches remains halachically significant. Hence,...</i> one must move them after detaching them, so that they will have been put in place for the purpose of a <i>sukkah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Moving the branches negates their previous placement, and afterwards they are considered to be kosher <i>s'chach</i>, which was put in place for the purpose of creating a sukkah.</i>
If one mixed a substance which may be used for <i>s'chach</i> with a substance that may not be used for <i>s'chach</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">This may refer to a substance like metal, which is unfit for use as <i>s'chach</i> because it does not grow in the earth, or branches of a tree which have not been detached from their source of nurture.</i> and used<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">the mixture of...</i> the two as <i>s'chach</i>, even though the quantity of kosher <i>s'chach</i> exceeds that of the substance which was not acceptable as <i>s'chach</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The presence of the substance which is not acceptable as <i>s'chach</i> is not nullified according to the principles of ברב ביטול, because it exists as a separate entity which can be distinguished from the kosher <i>s'chach</i>.<br>Rav Avraham, the Rambam's son, notes the apparent contradiction between this decision and Halachah 3, which states:[When one uses] branches from a fig tree which contain figs,...We see if the waste is more than the food; then we may use them as <i>s'chach</i>.That halachah also mentions kosher <i>s'chach</i> - the branches - and substances which are not acceptable as <i>s'chach</i> - the figs. However, if there is a greater quantity of branches, the presence of the figs is nullified. In contrast, in this halachah, that concept is not applied.<br>Rav Avraham distinguishes between the two. In Halachah 3, the person does not intend to use the fruit for the purpose of shade; he merely wants to save the effort of removing it from the branches. Therefore, their presence may be nullified. In contrast, in this halachah the substances which are not acceptable as <i>s'chach</i> are being employed for the purpose of shade itself. Hence, their presence cannot be nullified.</i> [the mixture] is not acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned above, this decision depends on the Rambam's interpretation of <i>Sukkah</i> 9b. That passage reads:A person who constructs his sukkah under a tree is considered as though he built it within his home...<br>Ravva said: "The above applies only to a tree whose shade is greater than its open space. However, if its open space is greater than its shade, it is kosher.<br>What difference does it make if its open space is greater than its shade, the substance not acceptable as <i>s'chach</i> will be combined with the kosher <i>s'chach</i> [and therefore, the sukkah will not be acceptable]?<br>Rav Pappa said: בשחבטן.The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i>, the Ra'avad, and Rabbenu Manoach explain that the Rambam renders בשחבטן as "when he separated them." Thus, when the two substances were combined, the Rambam's opinion would be that the sukkah is not kosher, as explained in this clause of the halachah. When they are separate, the sukkah may be kosher according to the stipulations mentioned in the following clause of this halachah and the halachot to come.<br>(The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> explains that the Rambam renders בשחבטן as "when he severed them." However, that difference in interpretation does not result in a difference in halachah.)<br>Rashi and others interpret בשחבטן as "when he lowered them (and mixed them together with the kosher <i>s'chach</i>)." Thus, according to this opinion, the sukkah is acceptable when the kosher and non-kosher <i>s'chach</i> are mixed together. Thus, this view is diametrically opposed to the Rambam's, who maintains that such a mixture is of no avail.<br>As noted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 626:1), there are two ways of understanding this interpretation of the passage. However, since both of them are not acceptable to the Rambam, their explanation will not be included here.</i><br>If one covered the sukkah with the two substances and kept them separate,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">When there is a majority of kosher <i>s'chach</i>, it is judged to be an independent entity. When there is a sufficient amount of kosher <i>s'chach</i>, the sukkah is kosher unless the substance that is not acceptable as <i>s'chach</i> is placed in a manner which can disqualify the entire sukkah, as is explained in this and the following three halachot.</i> [the following rules apply:] If there are more than three handbreadths of the substance which is not acceptable as <i>s'chach</i> in one place,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The principle of <i>l'vud</i>, by which this substance could be considered to be a continuation of the kosher <i>s'chach</i>, cannot apply. However, if there is less than three handbreadths of the substance that is not acceptable as <i>s'chach</i> in one place, even though there are a number of such patches among the <i>s'chach</i>, the sukkah is kosher, as stated in Halachah 16. Furthermore, one may eat and sleep under the non-kosher <i>s'chach</i> (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 632:1).</i> whether<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">the substance which is not acceptable as <i>s'chach</i> is placed...</i> in the middle of the sukkah or at its side, it is not acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in the following halachot, this is relevant only with regard to a sukkah which possesses the minimum area. The reason the sukkah is not acceptable is not that it is disqualified by the non-kosher <i>s'chach</i>, but that it does not have the minimum amount of kosher <i>s'chach</i> (<i>Sukkah</i> 17b).</i>
Where does the above apply? In a small sukkah.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">See the commentary on both the previous and following halachot.</i> However, in a large sukkah,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">which possesses a minimal amount of kosher <i>s'chach</i>, more lenient rules apply...</i> where there is a substance that is unacceptable as <i>s'chach</i> in the middle, it disqualifies the sukkah if there are four handbreadths of it.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">across the entire span of the sukkah, dividing the sukkah in half, it is considered to be significant (Rashi, <i>Sukkah</i> 17a), and hence the sukkah is disqualified.<br>The Ramah (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 632:2) emphasizes that even where the non-kosher <i>s'chach</i> is of this size, if the sukkah possesses seven cubits by seven cubits of kosher <i>s'chach</i> in one place, that portion may be considered to be a kosher sukkah and used during the holiday.</i> [If there is] less than that,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">the division is not as noticeable. Hence,</i> the sukkah is kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 632:1) states that one may eat and sleep throughout the entire sukkah. However, the Ra'avad and Rabbenu Nissim do not accept this leniency, and the <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> (632:3) advises following this stringency.</i><br>Where the substance that is unacceptable as <i>s'chach</i> is at the side, it disqualifies the sukkah if there are four cubits of it.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Four cubits or more exceeds the measure of leniency provided by the Torah.</i> [If there is] less than that, the sukkah is kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">on the basis of the principle of דופן עקומה explained below.</i><br>For example,<br>a) [the roof of] a house which was opened in the center and <i>s'chach</i> placed over the opening<br>b)<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">This and the following examples are given by the Mishnah, <i>Sukkah</i> 1:10.</i> a courtyard surrounded by an <i>exedra</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">note the drawing accompanying Chapter 4, Halachah 8.</i> which was covered with <i>s'chach</i><br>c)<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 17a explains that one might not necessarily have been able to deduce this example from the previous one, because in that instance the walls of the house were constructed for it. In contrast, the walls of the <i>excedrah</i> were not constructed for its sake, but for the houses on either side.</i> a large sukkah over which was placed a substance that was not acceptable as <i>s'chach</i> near the sides of its walls.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 17a explains that one might not necessarily have been able to deduce this example from the previous ones, because in those instances only kosher <i>s'chach</i> was placed over the opening of those structures. In this instance, a substance that is not acceptable as <i>s'chach</i> was placed on the roof intentionally.</i><br>[In all these cases,] if there are four cubits [or more] from the edge of the kosher <i>s'chach</i> until the wall, it is not acceptable. If there is less than that amount, we view it as though the wall has been made crooked<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., the substance that is not acceptable as s'chach is considered part of the wall - Rabbenu Nissim states that this law applies only when the walls reach all the way to the <i>s'chach</i>. Otherwise, it is impossible to consider the non-kosher <i>s'chach</i> to be an extension of the walls. The <i>Tur</i> and the <i>Taz</i> quote more lenient positions and allow such a sukkah, even though the walls do not reach the <i>s'chach</i>.</i> - i.e., the substance that is not acceptable as <i>s'chach</i> is considered part of the wall and it is kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Though the sukkah as a whole is kosher, one may not eat or sleep under the non-kosher <i>s'chach</i> if it is four handbreadths or more in size (<i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, ibid.). The rationale behind this decision is that the non-kosher <i>s'chach</i> is considered to be part of the wall and not part of the <i>s'chach</i>.</i> This concept is a halachah received by Moses on Mount Sinai.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The latter term refers to a law that was transferred from generation to generation reaching back to Mount Sinai. Nevertheless, there is no mention of - or even direct allusion to - it in the written Torah. See <i>Eduyot</i> 8:7.</i>
What is a small sukkah?<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">referred to in the previous two halachot and in Halachah 20</i> Any [sukkah] whose area is no more than seven handbreadths by seven handbreadths.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">As explained above, if there are three handbreadths or more of non-kosher <i>s'chach</i> in one place, the principle of <i>l'vud</i> cannot apply. Hence, the sukkah is not kosher, because it lacks the minimum amount of <i>s'chach</i> (<i>Sukkah</i> 17a). However, if there is less than that amount of non-kosher <i>s'chach</i>, the non-kosher <i>s'chach</i> is included as part of the sukkah and is counted as part of its minimum size (<i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, ibid.).</i><br>[What is meant by] a large one? Any [sukkah] [whose area is large enough] that seven handbreadths by seven handbreadths of kosher <i>s'chach</i> will remain besides the <i>s'chach</i> which is not acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is of sufficient size, it will be disqualified only when it is divided in half by a significant portion of non-kosher <i>s'chach</i>, or when it is too far, i.e., more than four cubits, removed from the walls.</i>
If one used as <i>s'chach</i> substances that were acceptable as <i>s'chach</i> and substances that were not acceptable as <i>s'chach</i>, and placed them alongside each other, leaving no place with non-kosher <i>s'chach</i> more than three handbreadths in area,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">so that even in a small sukkah, all the conditions mentioned in the above halachot are met, there is a further requirement before the sukkah is considered kosher.</i> [the following rules apply:]<br>If the total of the kosher <i>s'chach</i> exceeds that of the non-kosher <i>s'chach</i>, it is kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">This concept can be derived from the Mishnah (<i>Sukkah</i> 1:8): "If someone places iron staves or the frames of a bed as the roof of his sukkah, it is kosher, provided there is an equivalent amount of empty space."<br>In his commentary on that Mishnah, the Rambam emphasizes that the word "equivalent" should be interpreted loosely, since there must be more kosher <i>s'chach</i> than the non-kosher <i>s'chach</i>.</i> If there was an exactly equal amount of both substances, it is not acceptable<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">This decision has raised questions from all authorities. Though the Rambam's decision is based on <i>Sukkah</i> 15a, <i>Eruvin</i> 15b states that a divider whose open portion is as great as its closed portion is considered to be a divider. Indeed, the Rambam himself quotes that decision in <i>Hilchot Shabbat</i> 16:16. If so, why does he not follow the same principle in this instance as well?<br>The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> explains that the Rambam's decision is based on the fact that there is no way that there will not be some tiny open spaces within the kosher <i>s'chach</i> of a sukkah. Accordingly, even if the kosher <i>s'chach</i> is equal in area to the non-kosher <i>s'chach</i>, the tiny spaces between the kosher <i>s'chach</i> will tip the balance in favor of the non-kosher <i>s'chach</i>.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 631:8 quotes the Rambam's decision as halachah, but adds as a reason (a quote from Rabbenu Tam's text of <i>Sukkah</i> 15b): "for it is impossible to be exact." This implies that were it possible to know that the kosher <i>s'chach</i> is exactly equal to the non-kosher <i>s'chach</i>, it would be acceptable. See also commentary to Halachah 19.</i> even though there is not a single place which has three handbreadths [of non-kosher <i>s'chach</i>].<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">in a small sukkah, or four handbreadths in a large sukkah.</i> [This decision is rendered] because non-kosher <i>s'chach</i> is considered to be open space.
If one spread a cloth above [the <i>s'chach</i>]<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">for added shade (<i>Sukkah 1:3)</i></i> or spread one below it to catch [the leaves] which fall,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">the bracketed additions are based on the Rambam's commentary on that Mishnah.</i> it is unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The cloth nullifies the presence of the kosher s'chach above or below it.<br>(<i>Tosafot</i> explains that as long as the sukkah has sufficient <i>s'chach</i> without the cloth, the cloth will not necessarily nullify the presence of the kosher <i>s'chach</i>. However, the majority of the halachic authorities and the <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 629:19 do not accept this view.)<br>However, once the cloth is lifted away, the kosher <i>s'chach</i> is acceptable without any further activity. (See Ramah, <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 626:3.) Thus, a roof can be constructed to protect a sukkah from rain, provided it is removed while the sukkah is being used.</i> If one spread it [under the <i>s'chach</i>] as a decoration, it is kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">for a decoration is considered to be an extension of the article it adorns.</i> Similarly, if one covered the sukkah with <i>s'chach</i> as required by law and adorned it<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbenu Manoach emphasizes how the <i>s'chach</i> should be set in place before the decorations. Otherwise, they could be considered as intervening substances.</i> with various types of fruit, delicacies, and articles<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 10a describes the decoration of a sukkah with tapestries, nuts, pomegranates, grapes, and flasks of wine, oil, and flour.</i> which hang from either the walls or the <i>s'chach</i> as a decoration, it is kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">and one is permitted to eat and sleep under the decorations.</i>
Sukkah decorations do not reduce its height,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., if the inner space of the sukkah is more than twenty cubits high and the sukkah is therefore not acceptable (Chapter 4, Halachah 1), the presence of the decorations is not considered significant to reduce that space to the legal requirements.<br>Alternatively, if the sukkah is very low and the decorations reduce its inner space to less than ten handbreadths, it is still kosher. (See Halachah 2 with regard to branches.) They are considered to be part of the <i>s'chach</i>, and the space they take up is not taken into account (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, Rabbenu Manoach).</i> but they do reduce its width.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Bach</i> (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 639) explains that since we are forbidden to use these adornments throughout the holiday, they are considered to be a permanent part of the sukkah. Thus, the sukkah must have a full seven by seven handbreadths of open space besides them.</i><br>If the sukkah decorations are four handbreadths or more<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">as mentioned frequently above, four handbreadths constitutes a significant space</i> removed from the roof,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">they are considered to be non-kosher <i>s'chach</i>, and if they are of the size mentioned in Halachot 13 and 14...</i> it is unacceptable, because it is as though a person who sits there is not sitting under the <i>s'chach</i>, but rather under the decorations, which are foods and utensils that are not acceptable as <i>s'chach</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 5, Halachot 1 and 2.<br>The <i>Taz</i> (627:5) cautions against hanging any decorations more than four handbreadths away from the <i>s'chach</i> regardless of how small they are.</i>
The [following rules apply when the] <i>s'chach</i> has open spaces through which the sky can be seen: If the area of the open spaces is equivalent to<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">and of course, if it is more than</i> that of the space covered by <i>s'chach</i>, it is not acceptable, because<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Although the sukkah should be acceptable as long as the covered portion is equal to the open portion, in this situation...</i> the portion exposed to the sun will be greater than the shaded portion.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 22b explains that light spreads out. Accordingly, even though on the roof the portion covered by <i>s'chach</i> is equal to the uncovered portion, on the ground the area exposed to the sun will be greater than the shaded area. See also the Rambam's commentary on the Mishnah, <i>Sukkah</i> 1:1.</i> Whenever the portion exposed to the sun is greater than the shaded portion, it is not considered as <i>s'chach</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in Halachah 9, a sukkah must be constructed for the purpose of shade. If the portion exposed to the sun is greater than the shaded portion, it obviously does not serve that purpose.</i><br>If the <i>s'chach</i> exceeds the open space, it is kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">On the surface, the Rambam's choice of phraseology is slightly inexact, because everything depends on the amount of shade on the ground.</i>
When does the above apply? When there is no one open space<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">To disqualify the sukkah, the empty space must extend across the entire span of the sukkah or create a place large enough for a person to stand in (<i>Tosafot, Sukkah</i> 17a; Ramah, <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 632:2).<br>Furthermore, if the sukkah area is more than seven handbreadths by seven handbreadths in addition to the open space, the remaining covered area of the sukkah is kosher.</i> of three handbreadths.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Any space less than three handbreadths can be considered to be part of the <i>s'chach</i> on the basis of the principle of <i>l'vud</i>.<br>Though the sukkah as a whole is kosher, it is forbidden to eat or sleep under the open portion (<i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>Orach Chayim</i> 632:2).</i> However, if there is an open space of three handbreadths<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">whether in the center - i.e., an empty space cutting the sukkah in two</i> - whether in the center or at the side<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., an empty space that runs from one corner of the sukkah to the other. Rashi, on <i>Sukkah</i> 17a, explains that the concept of דופן עקומה, by which non-kosher <i>s'chach</i> is considered an extension of the wall, does not apply in this instance. Since it is merely empty space, it cannot be considered to be an extension of the wall.<br>It must be emphasized that we are speaking about a sukkah with only three walls.
