If the sukkah has four walls, but there is an empty space between the <i>s'chach</i> and the fourth wall, the sukkah as a whole is kosher.</i> - it is unacceptable until one reduces [the space] to less than three.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">it is unacceptable until one reduces [the space] to less than three.</i><br> If one used substances that were not acceptable as <i>s'chach</i><sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">e.g., pillows and blankets - to reduce the space, it is kosher - Non-kosher <i>s'chach</i> and open space are two different halachic categories. They have different measures with which they each disqualify a sukkah, and combinations of the two are not considered to be a single entity. Thus, if there is an open space three and a half handbreadths wide, one may correct the sukkah by putting one handbreadth of non-kosher <i>s'chach</i> in its place. (See the <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 632:3.)</i> - e.g., pillows and blankets - to reduce the space, it is kosher if the sukkah is large.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The terms "large" and "small" are explained in Halachot 13-15.</i> If it is a small sukkah, it is not acceptable<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">because the sukkah has less than seven handbreadths by seven handbreadths of kosher <i>s'chach</i>.</i> unless [the space] was reduced with a substance that is acceptable as <i>s'chach</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">unless [the space] was reduced with a substance that is acceptable as s'chach.</i><br>When the shaded portion of most of the sukkah exceeds the portion exposed to the sun, although in the lesser part of the sukkah the portion exposed to the sun exceeds the shaded portion,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., there are two portions of the sukkah, one which is mostly shaded and one which is mostly exposed to the sun.</i> because<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">when the sukkah is taken...</i> as a whole the shaded portion exceeds the portion exposed to the sun, it is kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">and one may eat and sleep in the part of the sukkah where the portion exposed to the sun exceeds the shaded portion (<i>Magen Avraham</i> 631:1). The Ramah quotes a more stringent opinion which forbids using the portion with more sun if it is seven handbreadths by seven handbreadths in area.</i>
The proper way<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> (631:5) uses the expression לכתחילה - i.e., one should set out to construct a sukkah in this manner.</i> is that <i>s'chach</i> should be thin, so that the large stars can be seen through it.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">From the Jerusalem Talmud, <i>Sukkah</i> 2:3, the source for this statement, it appears that the expression "large stars" means stars large enough to be seen during the day.</i> However, even though it is thick<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">like [the roof of] a house - and stars cannot be seen through it - The latter expression is quoted from the Mishnah, <i>Sukkah</i> 2:2.</i> - like [the roof of] a house - and stars cannot be seen through it, it is kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> (631:5) uses the expression בדיעבד (after the fact), implying that it is not desirable to construct a sukkah in this manner. Nevertheless, the <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> (631:5) quotes many Ashkenazic authorities who tend toward making the <i>s'chach</i> thick.<br>The above applies only with regard to placing the <i>s'chach</i>. However, afterwards, even when the <i>s'chach</i> is thick, a person should have no compunctions about using such a sukkah.<br>Though thick <i>s'chach</i> is kosher, the <i>Magen Avraham</i> 631:2 notes that if it is so thick that rain will not enter the sukkah, the sukkah is unacceptable, because then it resembles a house and not a temporary dwelling made for shade.</i><br>If the <i>s'chach</i> is uneven<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 22a questions: "What is the meaning of the word מדובללת used by the Mishnah?" Shmuel (whom the halachah follows) replies: "One reed going upward and one reed going downward."</i> - i.e., some of it high and some of it low<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">it is kosher - Even though there will gaps in the <i>s'chach</i> and during certain times of the day, when the sun shines at an angle, the portion of the sukkah exposed to the sun will exceed the shaded portion (Ramah, <i>Orach Chayim</i> 631:5).</i> - it is kosher, provided there is less than three handbreadths between the upper and lower [portions of the <i>s'chach</i>].<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">As explained previously, any distance less than three handbreadths is not significant because of the principle of <i>l'vud</i>.</i> If the upper portion [of the <i>s'chach</i>] is a handbreadth<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">by a handbreadth, the minimum size of a roof. (See Chapter 4, Halachah 7.)</i> or more wide, even though it is more than three handbreadths above [the lower portion], we consider it to be descending and touching the edge of the lower portion.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">This principle, referred to by <i>Sukkah</i> 22a as חבוט רמי - literally, "cast it down" - is one of the leniencies followed as a halachah received from Moses on Mount Sinai. It allows this uneven roof to be considered to be a single straight entity. The same principle is also applied in <i>Hilchot Shabbat</i> 15:25 and <i>Hilchot Tum'at Meit</i> 16:5.</i> [This applies] provided it is aligned opposite the edge of the lower portion.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., that the upper portion could be lowered into the space left open by the lower portion. However, if it would not fit exactly in between the spaces, this principle does not apply (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>). The <i>Lechem Mishneh</i>, noting the Rambam's decision in <i>Hilchot Shabbat</i>, explains that even if the lower space is wider than the upper portion, as long as they do not overlap, it is acceptable.<br>The <i>Merchevat Hamishneh</i> explains that the law differs here from <i>Hilchot Shabbat</i>, because there is a principle that two halachot received from Moses on Mount Sinai cannot be used simultaneously to have a sukkah considered to be kosher.<br>In the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 631:5), Rav Yosef Karo quotes the opinion he stated in the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>. However, <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> (631:7) and others follow the view of the <i>Lechem Mishneh</i>.</i>
When a person constructs one sukkah on top of another sukkah, the lower one is unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 9b explains that the verse which commands us to dwell in sukkot (Leviticus 23:42  spells the word - סכת - omitting the letter ו, which is usually used. On that basis, the Sages explained that a single structure may be used for one sukkah, but not for two. Of the two, the lower sukkah is disqualified because...</i> It is considered as though it had been constructed in a house.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">since the floor of the upper sukkah is its roof, it may not be considered to be <i>s'chach</i>, because it was not placed there for purposes of shade alone, nor can the lower sukkah be acceptable because of the <i>s'chach</i> of the upper sukkah, for the floor intervenes between them.</i> [However,] the upper one is kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">There is no reason that the presence of the lower sukkah should disqualify it.</i><br>When do we say that the lower one is unacceptable? When the inner space of the upper sukkah is ten handbreadths or more [high]<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">the minimum height of a sukkah, as explained in Chapter 4, Halachah 1.</i> and the roof of the lower sukkah is strong enough to hold the pillows and covers of the upper sukkah,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Otherwise, the upper sukkah is not fit to dwell in. Though a sukkah is only a temporary dwelling, it must be fit to be used for all the purposes for which one uses one's home, as explained in Chapter 6, Halachah 5.</i> even if that is done with difficulty.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., even if the floor of the upper sukkah shakes when pillows and covers are placed on it (Rambam's commentary on the Mishnah, <i>Sukkah</i> 1:2).</i><br>However, if the inner space of the upper sukkah is less than ten handbreadths [high], or the roof of the lower sukkah is not strong enough to hold the pillows and covers of the upper sukkah, even with difficulty,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">and thus, is unfit to be used.</i> even<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The use of this word is questionable. It implies that both sukkot, the upper and the lower one, are kosher. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> states that if the upper sukkah is not fit to dwell in, it is not kosher, and therefore recommends striking the word "even" from the text. Though the <i>Lechem Mishneh</i> attempts to justify the use of the term, his arguments seem forced. However, it must be noted that most manuscripts and printings of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> as well as the Rambam's and most other texts of the source for this halachah - the Mishnah, <i>Sukkah</i> 1:2 - include this word.</i> the lower sukkah is kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">for the reason that since its roof is of no functional purpose, it is not considered an intervening substance between the sukkah and the upper <i>s'chach</i>.</i> This applies provided the height of both together does not exceed twenty cubits,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">the maximum height of the sukkah, as stated in Chapter 4, Halachah 1.</i> since [use of] the lower sukkah is permitted because of the <i>s'chach</i> of the upper one.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">In the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> - and also in the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 628:1) - Rav Yosef Karo explains that this applies only when the <i>s'chach</i> of the lower sukkah is not substantial enough to allow the use of the sukkah. However, if the <i>s'chach</i> of the lower sukkah alone produces enough shade to permit the use of the sukkah, it is kosher even though the <i>s'chach</i> of the upper sukkah is more than 20 cubits high.<br>He continues to explain that even though the presence of non-kosher <i>s'chach</i> nullifies the kosher <i>s'chach</i> below it, that applies only when the substance used is by nature not acceptable for use as <i>s'chach</i>. In this instance, the <i>s'chach</i> of the upper sukkah is fit to be used; the only difficulty is its position: its placement above the maximum height of the sukkah.<br>(Though this concept is found in <i>Tosafot, Sukkah</i> 9b, no allusion to it appears in the Rambam's statements. Indeed, the simple meaning of the Rambam's words implies the very opposite. Furthermore, in his commentary on the Mishnah [<i>Sukkah</i> 1:1], the Rambam explains that the reason <i>s'chach</i> placed higher than 20 cubits is unacceptable is that it makes the sukkah into a permanent dwelling. Thus, according to the Rambam, such <i>s'chach</i> can be compared to the boards of a house, which though originally kosher for use as <i>s'chach</i>, are disqualified because they became part of a permanent structure.)</i>
A bed [with a canopy placed] inside a sukkah: If [the canopy] is more than ten handbreadths high,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">the minimum height for a dwelling to be considered to be an independent structure (<i>Sukkah</i> 20b).<br>In this and the following instances mentioned in this halachah, the ten handbreadths are measured from the ground to the canopy (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 627:2-3; <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 627:5).</i> a person who sits under it does not fulfill his obligation,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah, <i>Sukkah</i> 2:1 relates:An incident occurred concerning Tavi, Rabban Gamliel's slave. He would sleep under a [canopied] bed [in the sukkah]. Rabban Gamliel told the Sages: "See my slave, Tavi. He is well-learned and knows that slaves are free of the obligation of [dwelling in] the sukkah. Hence, he sleeps under a bed."</i> because it is considered to be a sukkah<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">with non-kosher <i>s'chach</i></i> within a sukkah.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">even if it has no walls (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 627:1)</i><br>Similarly, a canopy<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">placed over a bed</i> with a roof<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">even as small as a handbreadth: - As mentioned frequently above, a handbreadth is the minimum measure of a roof.</i> - even as small as a handbreadth: If it is ten handbreadths high, one may not sleep<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">or perform any other activity required to be performed in a sukkah. (Perhaps the Rambam mentions sleeping because it is an activity that may be performed only within a sukkah. Even a short nap should not be taken outside the sukkah. In contrast, a snack may be eaten outside a sukkah.)</i> under it in a sukkah.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">for the same reasons mentioned above.</i> By the same token, if one sets up four pillars and spreads a sheet over them, if they are ten [handbreadths high], they are considered to be a sukkah within a sukkah.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Hence, we are forbidden to use the space below it as a sukkah.<br>This halachah depends on the Rambam's interpretation of the Mishnah, <i>Sukkah</i> 1:3. However, <i>Tosafot, Sukkah</i> 10b and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 627:3) interpret the passage differently and forbid spreading a canopy over bedposts which are permanently affixed to the four corners of a bed, even if the canopy is less than ten handbreadths above the ground.</i>
In contrast, should one spread a sheet over two pillars<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot</i> (ibid.) and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (ibid.) differ with this decision as well, and explain that if these pillars are ten handbreadths high and permanently affixed to the bed, it is forbidden to spread a sheet over them.</i> or [use] a canopy that has a roof of less than a handbreadth<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">If the canopy is not permanently affixed to the bed, even <i>Tosafot</i> and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> will permit its use.</i> - no matter how high they are<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., even if they are more than ten handbreadths high.</i> - it is permitted to sleep under them within a sukkah.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 19a relates that Abbaye found Rav Yosef sleeping in a canopied bed in the sukkah. The latter explained that the canopy he was using was permitted because it did not have a roof.</i> They are not considered to be a sukkah within a sukkah, because they do not have a roof.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in Chapter 4, Halachah 7, such structures are not acceptable for use as a sukkah. Hence, they also cannot disqualify a sukkah.</i>
A borrowed sukkah is fit [to be used on the holiday].<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Although, as stated in Chapter 8, Halachah 10, a person cannot fulfill the mitzvah of lulav as prescribed by the Torah with a lulav belonging to a colleague, that concept does not apply with regard to a sukkah.<br><i>Sukkah</i> 27b explains the derivation of this concept as follows: Leviticus 23:42 states: "Every citizen of Israel shall dwell in sukkot." The latter word is written סכת, implying one sukkah. This prompted our Sages to declare: "All Israel are fit to dwell in a single sukkah."<br>In his commentary, Rashi explains that the cost of such a sukkah would not be high enough to require every individual to pay a penny's worth. Hence, we must assume that some people would be using a borrowed sukkah, and, nevertheless, our Sages said that it was kosher.</i> Similarly, a stolen sukkah is also fit [for use].<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">There is a slight imprecision with the Rambam's statements. A stolen object which the thief is obligated to return cannot be used to perform a mitzvah, because it is considered a mitzvah that came about through a sin (מצוה הבאה בעבירה). However, in the instances cited by the Rambam, the sukkah itself is never considered stolen property. Hence, the use of such a sukkah is not forbidden. However, in a number of instances (see below), the sukkah itself is considered to be stolen property and must be returned. Hence, it cannot be used to fulfill the mitzvah.</i><br>What does the latter imply? If a person attacked a colleague, forced him to leave his sukkah, stole it, and dwelled in it, the attacker has fulfilled his obligation, because landed property cannot be stolen.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Gezeilah</i> 8:14 relates that "landed property is never acquired by a thief, but rather remains in the possession of its owner forever."<br>Since the sukkah is built on the land, it is considered part of the property and the above rule applies to it as well. Thus, the sukkah is still considered to be the property of its original owner and the thief is viewed as merely "borrowing" the sukkah from him. As mentioned above, use of a borrowed sukkah is permitted.</i><br>[Similarly,] if he stole wood and made a sukkah from it, he has fulfilled his obligation,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 31a relates that a woman once came to Rav Nachman complaining that the exilarch's servants had stolen wood from her to build his sukkah.<br>Rav Nachman answered that they had, nevertheless, fulfilled their obligation...</i> because the Sages ordained that the owner of the wood is entitled only to the monetary worth of the wood.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">but not the wood itself. Thus, the sukkah itself is the property of the thief. Though he is obligated to pay the owner for his wood, that obligation does not prevent the sukkah from being considered as his.<br><i>Hilchot Gezeilah</i> 1:5 explains this principle by stating:Anyone who steals is obligated to return the stolen property itself... If it was lost or changed, he is obligated to return its monetary value...<br>Even if one steals a beam and builds a mansion [using it], since [the beam] has not been changed, the Torah law would require him to destroy the entire building and return the beam to its owner.<br>Nevertheless, the Sages instituted a decree [to encourage] those who repent; they ordained that all that is necessary is for him to return [the beam's] monetary value, and thus he will not forfeit his building.Rabbenu Manoach asks: Since the mitzvah of sukkah is commanded by the Torah, and Torah law regards these boards as stolen, how can the Sages' decree change their status in this context?<br>He explains that, as implied by the principle הפקר הפקר בית דין, the Torah has granted the Sages the right to determine the status of property. Their decree can alter entirely a person's rights to its title. Thus, in the present case, since Rabbinic law entitles the thief to use of the boards, he is permitted, according to Torah law, to use them as a Sukkah.<br>The <i>Magen Avraham</i> (637:5) writes that if the thief refuses to pay for the wood, it is considered to be the property of its original owner, and the thief does not fulfill his obligation.</i> Even if one stole boards and merely put them in place without attaching them or changing anything about them, he has fulfilled his obligation.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">This is a special leniency granted out of respect for the mitzvah of sukkah (<i>Sukkah</i>, ibid.). Since the thief has not made any changes in the wood itself or built a permanent structure from it, the Rabbinic decree mentioned above would not apply. Thus, during the rest of the year (and even in this case, after the Sukkot festival), the wood would have to be returned. During the festival, the Sages considered it to be the property of the thief and he is allowed to use without any constraints throughout the festival (<i>Hilchot Gezeilah</i>, ibid.).<br>Nevertheless, in his <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> and in his <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 637:3), Rav Yosef Karo gives two examples of a stolen sukkah which one is forbidden to use: a sukkah that had been constructed on a ship or one that had been constructed on a wagon. Since the thief did not build anything, the abovementioned Rabbinic decree does not apply; nor are they affixed to land, and hence the principle that landed property cannot be stolen is not relevant.</i><br>If a person constructs his sukkah in the public domain,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Though the Jewish inhabitants of the city would surely not object to use of public property for this purpose, the gentile inhabitants of the city would. Since they also have a share in this property, building a sukkah there is equivalent to stealing from the public (<i>Magen Avraham</i> 637:3).<br>Nevertheless, since the theft of land is involved...</i> it is acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">for the reasons mentioned above. Note the <i>Magen Avraham</i>, ibid. and the <i>Bi'ur Halachah</i>, who discuss whether or not it is permitted to recite a blessing when using such a sukkah.</i>

Chapter 6

Women, slaves, and minors are freed from [fulfilling the mitzvah of] sukkah.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The same laws that apply to hearing the <i>shofar</i> apply to Sukkah. Hence, women and slaves are free of obligation, as is the case regarding all mitzvot whose fulfillment is associated with a specific time. Minors have no obligations at all according to the Torah. (See the commentary on Chapter 2, Halachah 1.)<br>See also the commentary on Chapter 2, Halachah 2 regarding a woman's right to perform the mitzvah if she desires, and whether permission is granted for her to recite a blessing.</i> A <i>tumtum</i> and an <i>androgynous</i> are obligated because of the doubt [concerning their status].<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">See the commentary on the above halachah for a definition of these terms and their status. Note Halachah 13 regarding their recitation of a blessing.</i> Similarly, a person who is half slave and half free is obligated.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See the commentary on Chapter 2, Halachot 1 and 3.</i><br>A minor who does not require his mother's [presence]<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 28b offers two definitions of this term:a) a child who does not need his mother to help him after he relieves himself;<br>b) a child who does not wake up in the night and call out: "Mommy! Mommy!"Nevertheless, based on <i>Eruvin</i> 82a, the Rambam provided us with a more definite guideline...</i> - i.e., a child of five or six<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">is obligated [to fulfill the mitzvah] of sukkah according to Rabbinic decree - According to Rashi (<i>Berachot</i> 48a) and the Ramban (<i>Milchamot Hashem, Berachot</i> 20b), there is no obligation incumbent on the child himself. The child's father is obligated to educate him. If he has no father, the obligation falls on his mother and on the local Rabbinic court (<i>Terumat Hadeshen</i> 99).<br><i>Tosafot</i> (<i>Berachot</i> 48a) differs and explains that the Sages placed the obligation on the minor himself. From the Rambam's phraseology, it appears that he accepts this view.<br>Support for this premise can be derived from the Rambam's decision in <i>Hilchot Berachot</i> 5:15-16, which states that an adult who ate a small meal can fulfill his obligation by listening to a child reciting the grace after meals (for both are obligated by virtue of Rabbinic decree). Though others object on the grounds that the child himself is not obligated in the mitzvah, the Rambam states that such a practice is acceptable.</i> - is obligated [to fulfill the mitzvah] of sukkah according to Rabbinic decree, to train him in [the performance of] mitzvot.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam mentions the same concept in <i>Hilchot Tzitzit</i> 3:9, <i>Hilchot Berachot</i> 5:1, and <i>Hilchot Lulav</i> 7:19.</i>
The sick and their attendants<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Since the latter's performance of a mitzvah - tending to the sick - does not enable them to perform the mitzvah of dwelling in the sukkah, they are absolved from the obligation. (See <i>Sukkah</i> 25a, 26a.) However, this leniency is granted only during the time the sick person requires their assistance (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 640:7).</i> are freed from [fulfilling the mitzvah] of sukkah. This applies not only to a person who is dangerously ill,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">who is free from the obligation to perform all mitzvot, even those as severe as the Sabbath or Yom Kippur;</i> but also to one with a headache<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">or any other minor illness (Rambam, Commentary on the Mishnah, <i>Sukkah</i> 2:4).<br>This leniency is granted because of one of the basic principles related to the mitzvah of sukkah. Leviticus 23:42 states: "You shall dwell in sukkot (תשבו בסכת)." On that verse, <i>Sukkah</i> 26a comments: תשבו כעין תדורו - i.e., dwelling in the sukkah is comparable to living in one's own home. Thus, a person is not required to dwell in a sukkah under circumstances which would cause him to leave his own home.<br>Since a person who is sick would seek the most comfortable lodgings possible and would not camp outside, he is not obligated to do so to fulfill the mitzvah of sukkah (Rabbenu Manoach).</i> or a sore eye.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 26a relates that Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel was granted permission to sleep outside the sukkah for this purpose.<br>The Torah's prohibitions are suspended not only when a person's life is in danger, but also when a particular limb might be lost. Though this premise is accepted by the Rambam (see <i>Hilchot Shabbat</i> 2:4), it does not appear that he is referring to it here. Rather, it seems that he means a minor ailment comparable to a headache. See also <i>Tosafot</i> (<i>Sukkah</i>, ibid.).</i><br>A person who is uncomfortable [when dwelling in the sukkah] is freed from the obligation [to fulfill the mitzvah] of sukkah.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">based on the principle of תשבו כעין תדורו, as explained above. Just as a person would seek out a comfortable permanent dwelling, he is obligated to dwell only in a sukkah which does not cause him unpleasantness.<br>Rabbenu Manoach states that this leniency does not apply on the first night of Sukkot, on which there is a binding obligation to eat in the sukkah. (See Halachah 6.) The Ramah (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 640:4) quotes this statement as halachah.</i> This applies to the person himself, but not to his attendants.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Some earlier texts of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> stated that leniency was also granted to such a person's attendants, but that is surely a printing error (Rabbenu Manoach).</i><br>Who is "a person who is uncomfortable [when dwelling in the sukkah]"? A person who cannot sleep in the sukkah because of the wind or because of the flies, mites, or the like, or because of the smell.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Tur</i>, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 640:4), and the Ramah emphasize that this law applies only when the discomfort is an unexpected occurrence. However, a sukkah which is constructed in a place where the discomfort will surely come is considered to be unfit to dwell in and is unacceptable for the performance of the mitzvah.</i>
A mourner<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The term אבל refers to a person in the seven days of mourning which follow the burial of a father, mother, wife, brother, sister, son, or daughter. An אונן (a person in mourning for these relatives before their burial) is freed from the obligation of sukkah, because he is absolved of the duty to perform all mitzvot (<i>Hilchot Eivel</i> 4:6).<br>It must be noted that none of the mourning rites should be followed during a festival. If a close relative dies before the festival, the <i>shiv'ah</i> (seven days of mourning) is completed at the festival's commencement, even though all seven days have not passed (<i>Hilchot Eivel</i> 10:3, 8).</i> is obligated [to fulfill the mitzvah] of sukkah.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 25b explains that a mourner (אבל) is obligated to fulfill all the mitzvot. Nevertheless, one might suppose that just as a person who is uncomfortable is freed from the obligation of sukkah, a mourner would also be absolved. Therefore, the Talmud includes a special teaching to emphasize the mourner's obligation, explaining that one is absolved only for discomfort which comes from external factors. In this case, the mourner brings discomfort upon himself. He should compose himself and concentrate his attention on the mitzvah.<br>Nevertheless, <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> (640:13) writes that if dwelling in a sukkah will cause a mourner more discomfort than dwelling in his home, he is allowed to choose the latter alternative.</i><br>A groom,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">because he is involved in the mitzvah of marriage. <i>Sukkah</i> 25b adds that generally, a sukkah is to small to carry out the wedding celebrations in the proper manner. Furthermore, the groom is obligated to rejoice together with his wife, and the sukkah is not an appropriate setting.</i> his attendants,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Hilchot Zechiyah Umatanah</i>, Chapter 7, the Rambam describes the custom of שושבינות (attending a groom).</i> and all the members of the wedding party<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">They are absolved of the mitzvah only while they are involved in the mitzvah of celebrating together with the bride and groom, however, when they leave, they are obligated. Furthermore, there are many authorities who require that the meals served the groom and his attendants be served in a sukkah. (See <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 640:33.)</i> are freed from [fulfilling the mitzvah] of sukkah throughout the seven days of [the wedding] festivities.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">It must be emphasized that the wedding must have taken place during the four days between Yom Kippur and Sukkot. Weddings are not held during the Ten Days of Repentance, nor on Chol Hamo'ed.</i>
Emissaries charged with a mission involving a mitzvah<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">e.g., to study Torah, to greet a Sage under whom one studied Torah, or to redeem captives (Rashi, <i>Sukkah</i> 25a).</i> are freed from [fulfilling the mitzvah] of sukkah<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., they are not required to eat, sleep, or perform any of the activities in a sukkah described in Halachot 5-9.</i> both during the day and at night.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi (<i>Sukkah</i> 25a, 26a) states that this applies even when they are lodging overnight. Since, at night they are also involved with thinking about how to fulfill the mitzvah, they are still considered as occupied with the performance of a mitzvah and are not required to seek out a sukkah. An example of this principle quoted by the Talmud supports this interpretation: When Rav Chisda and Rabbah bar Rav Huna would attend [the lecture delivered] at the home of the exilarch on the Sabbath of the festival, they would sleep along the river banks of Sura. They explained: "We are emissaries involved in a mitzvah" (<i>Sukkah</i> 26a).<br>Though these Sages were visiting a city which served as a center of Jewish life and surely could have found a sukkah to lodge in, they did not see the necessity for doing so. (See also <i>Sukkah</i> 10b.)<br>Nevertheless, <i>Tosafot, Sukkah</i> 10b, interprets the passage differently and explains that leniency is granted these emissaries only when dwelling in the sukkah would in some way prevent them from carrying out the mitzvah which they set out to perform. If it is possible to do both - perform the mitzvah they set out to do and fulfill the mitzvah of sukkah - one must endeavor to do so.<br>The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> explains that the Rambam shares Rashi's view. However, the final halachic position followed by most Ashkenazic authorities is closer to <i>Tosafot</i>'s position: i.e., these emissaries are not obligated to trouble themselves to find a sukkah. However, if there is a sukkah available, they should make use of it. Nevertheless, if sleeping in the sukkah would prevent them from getting proper rest, and thus hold them back from fulfilling their mitzvah in a proper manner, they are not required to sleep in a sukkah (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 640:18; <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 640:38).<br>(Abstractly, it is possible to differentiate between the two positions as follows. According to Rashi, there is no obligation of sukkah incumbent on this person whatsoever. In contrast, <i>Tosafot</i> would maintain that the person is obligated to perform the mitzvah. However, at all times he is bound by another commitment which takes precedence.)</i> People who journey during the day are freed from [fulfilling the mitzvah] of sukkah during the day<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., while they are traveling. <i>Tosafot, Sukkah</i> 26a explains that this concept is derived from the principle: תשבו כעין תדורו - i.e., dwelling in the sukkah is comparable to living in one's own home. Since a person will occasionally leave his home on a journey, he is also entitled to leave his sukkah.</i> and are obligated at night.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">if they lodge in a settled place (<i>Tosafot</i>, ibid.).</i> People who journey during the night are freed from [fulfilling the mitzvah] of sukkah at night<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., while traveling</i> and are obligated during the day.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">if they lodge in a settled area.</i><br>A city's day watchmen are freed from [fulfilling the mitzvah] of sukkah during the day<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">when they are on duty.</i> and are obligated at night.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">when they are replaced by their colleagues. Conversely,...</i> Its night watchmen are freed from [fulfilling the mitzvah] of sukkah at night and are obligated during the day. The watchmen of gardens and orchards<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">who spend their entire day guarding the produce (Rashi, <i>Sukkah</i> 26a)</i> are freed from [fulfilling the mitzvah] during the day and the night, because if the watchman constructs a sukkah, a thief will realize that the watchman has a fixed place and will go to steal from another place.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i>, ibid., explains that based on this principle, the leniency is granted only to watchmen charged with guarding an entire orchard or farm. However, if a watchman is charged with guarding produce which is collected in one place, he is capable of fulfilling the mitzvah and discharging his duty. Hence, he is obligated to construct a sukkah there.</i>
How must the mitzvah of dwelling in the sukkah be fulfilled? A person must eat, drink, and live<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">performing all the activities mentioned in this halachah and Halachot 6-9.</i> in the sukkah throughout all seven days [of the festival],<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">These statements and the Rambam's introduction to these halachot imply that each moment a person dwells in the sukkah throughout the seven days of the festival, he fulfills a mitzvah. (Accordingly, a blessing is recited whenever one makes use of the sukkah [Halachah 12]. See Halachah 7.)</i> both during the day and at night,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 43a derives this concept by drawing an analogy (גזרה שוה) to the seven days of preparation for the dedication of the Sanctuary. Thus, just as the priests were required to stay in the Sanctuary for seven days - day and night - similarly, the mitzvah of sukkah must be observed in the same manner.</i> in the same manner as he dwells in his home throughout the year.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">following the principle of תשבו כעין תדורו mentioned above.</i><br>During these seven days, he must consider his house as a temporary dwelling and the sukkah as his permanent home,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">This statement is a quote from the Mishnah, <i>Sukkah</i> 2:8. In his commentary on that Mishnah, the Rambam defines קבע as "of primary importance and a constant practice."</i> as [Leviticus 23:42] states: "You shall dwell in sukkot for seven days."<br>What does this imply? His attractive utensils and attractive bedding [should be brought] to the sukkah. His drinking utensils - i.e., his cups and crystal pitchers - [should be brought] to the sukkah.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Doing so is a sign of honor and respect for the mitzvah.</i> However, utensils used for food - i.e., pans and plates - [may be left] outside the sukkah.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Obviously, during the meal one's plates must be in the sukkah. The Rambam's intent is that after the meal there is no necessity to keep one's plates in the sukkah, because they are not attractive and are generally stored in pantries outside the home. In contrast, it is customary in many communities not to bring pots into the sukkah, even while eating (<i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 639:5). [Needless to say, plates that must be washed must be removed from the sukkah.]<br>The printed (as opposed to certain manuscripts) text of Rashi (<i>Sukkah</i> 29a) also shares the Rambam's interpretation. <i>Tosafot</i> explains the passage slightly differently, rendering "utensils used for food" as "utensils used in the preparation of food" - i.e., pots and pans.</i> A candelabra<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., the source of light</i> [should be brought] to the sukkah.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">even when it is not burning, since it is attractive.</i> However, if the sukkah is small, it should be left outside the sukkah.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">lest it cause a fire (<i>Tosafot, Sukkah</i>, ibid.). This differs from Rashi's interpretation of <i>Sukkah</i>, ibid., which explains that the Talmud is referring to an earthenware lamp. Thus, we may assume that it should not be brought into the sukkah because it is unattractive. This, appears to justify the statements of the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i>, who writes that the lamp should not be brought into the sukkah when it is not burning. Both opinions are quoted by the later halachic authorities (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 639:3; <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 639:7-8).</i>
We should eat, drink, and sleep in the sukkah through the entire seven days [of the festival], both during the day and at night. It is forbidden to eat a meal<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., a meal of bread or food prepared from the five species of grain</i> outside the sukkah for the entire seven [day period].<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>B'nei Binyamin</i> explains that this expression includes the Sabbath and festivals, thus excluding the opinions which maintain that any meal of bread eaten on the Sabbath or festivals is significant and cannot be considered as a snack. See the commentary of Rabbi Akiva Eiger.</i> However, [there is no prohibition] if one eats a snack<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">even of bread, outside the sukkah.<br><i>Sukkah</i> 26a defines "a snack" as "what the students will eat before they enter the study sessions." In his commentary on the Mishnah, <i>Sukkah</i> 2:4, the Rambam explains it as "a small amount, [eaten] without considering it as a meal, in order to satisfy one's hunger [temporarily], until one can complete eating afterwards."<br>Rabbenu Manoach explains that though there is no prohibition involved, it is not desirable to eat even a snack of bread outside the sukkah.</i> the measure of a <i>k'beitzah</i> or less,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah (ibid.) relates that less than a <i>k'beitzah</i> of bread was brought once to Rabbi Tzadok and he ate it outside the sukkah.</i> or even slightly more.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 639:2) restricts the leniency to a <i>k'beitzah</i> (approximately 56 cubic centimeters according to <i>Shiurei Torah</i> and, in this instance, 41 cubic centimeters according to the <i>Chazon Ish</i>) alone (See <i>Tosafot, Yoma</i> 79a.) The same principles are also applied to cooked foods made from the five species of grain.</i><br>One may not sleep outside the sukkah at all, even a brief nap.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to eating, where there is a difference between a fixed meal and a snack, no such difference exists regarding sleep. At times, even a rest of a few moments can have a major effect on a person. (See <i>Sukkah</i> 26a; Jerusalem Talmud, <i>Sukkah</i> 2:5.)<br>At present, there are many communities where leniency is taken in this regard. In some climates, the cold will cause everyone to consider sleeping in the sukkah as uncomfortable and, hence, they are freed of that obligation. Furthermore, in many communities, there is also a danger involved. In addition, if a husband would sleep in a sukkah, he would deprive his wife of some of the happiness and satisfaction that should accompany a festival. (See Ramah, <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 639:2.)<br>Nevertheless, many authorities explain that these leniencies apply only at night, but during the day one is obligated to sleep in the sukkah.</i> It is permissible to drink water<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">or any other beverage, including wine (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 639:2), outside the sukkah. There is a difference of opinion among the later halachic authorities if this decision also applies when one sits down to a party centering on drinking wine (קובע עצמו לשתות). Some (the <i>Bach</i> and <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 639:12) require that such drinking be carried out in a sukkah. However, others (the Vilna Gaon and the <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 639:13) are more lenient.</i> and eat fruit outside the sukkah.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">for these do not constitute a significant meal.<br><i>Sukkah</i> 27a relates that Rabbi Elazar ben Shamu'a ate dates and grapes outside the sukkah, because "fruit does not require a sukkah."<br>Rabbenu Manoach and some other commentaries maintain that this leniency applies only to fruit. In contrast, meat, fish, cheese, and other substantial foods which form the basis of a meal must be eaten in a sukkah. While many later halachic authorities do not accept this view, some maintain that one should not sit down with company to a meal of such foods outside a sukkah. (See <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 639:11; <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 639:15.)</i> However, a person who follows the stringency of not drinking even water outside the sukkah is worthy of praise.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">To exemplify this principle, the Mishnah (ibid.) quotes the following:Once they brought a cooked dish for Rabban Yochanan ben Zakkai to taste, and two dates and a pitcher of water for Rabban Gamliel. They said: "Take them up to the sukkah."<i>Sukkah</i> 26b explains that often our Sages counselled against accepting extra stringencies upon oneself, lest one take pride in one's piety. However, in this instance they make no such reservations.</i>
Eating in the sukkah on the first night of the festival is an obligation.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 27a draws an analogy between the first night of Sukkot and the first night of Pesach. Just as eating a <i>k'zayit</i> of matzah on the fifteenth of Nisan is a mitzvah, so too, each Jewish male is required to eat in the sukkah on the fifteenth of Tishre.</i> If a person eats merely a <i>k'zayit</i> of bread,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">This is the minimum amount required by the Torah in all mitzvot that involve eating. In modern measurements, the figure corresponds to 28 cubic centimeters according to <i>Shiurei Torah</i>, and 48 cubic centimeters according to the <i>Chazon Ish</i>.</i> he fulfills his obligation. Afterwards, [the matter is left to one's] volition.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The difference in the requirement between the first night and the remaining days has other ramifications. As mentioned in Halachah 2, a person who is uncomfortable in the sukkah is freed from his obligation. However, this leniency applies only on the first night after the mitzvah of eating a <i>k'zayit</i> has been fulfilled (<i>Maggid Mishneh; Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 639:3).</i> If one desires to eat a meal,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">including bread, or according to some authorities, other hearty foods, as mentioned above</i> one must eat it in the sukkah. If one desires, throughout the seven [days of the festival], one may eat only fruit or roasted grain<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">which may be eaten...</i> outside of the sukkah.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Lechem Yehudah</i> explains that the Rambam is speaking only from the perspective of the mitzvah of sukkah. However, one is obligated to eat three meals on the Sabbath as an expression of the mitzvah of honoring the Sabbath and taking pleasure in its celebration. (See <i>Hilchot Shabbat</i> 30:9.) Similarly, one is required to eat festive meals on the first day of Sukkot in honor of the holiday. These meals must be eaten in the sukkah.</i> The same laws apply as those regarding the eating of matzah on Pesach.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's intent is that, as explained in <i>Hilchot Chametz U'matzah</i> 6:1, after the first night of the festival a person is not obligated to eat matzah and may subsist on other foods. Similarly, on Sukkot one may eat foods that do not require consumption within a sukkah.<br>However, according to most authorities, his choice of words is not exact. The mitzvah of sukkah differs from that of matzah. In the latter instance, there is no mitzvah involved in eating matzah after the first night of the holiday. In contrast, every activity performed in the sukkah constitutes a further fulfillment of the mitzvah.<br>Accordingly, after the first night of the festival no special blessing is recited before partaking of matzah. In contrast, as stated in Halachah 12, a blessing is recited every time we make use of the sukkah. See also the Moznaim publication of <i>Hilchot Chametz U'Matzah</i> 6:1.</i>
It is forbidden for a person to sit and eat with his head and the majority of his body inside a sukkah while his table is in his home or outside the sukkah.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah (<i>Sukkah</i> 2:6) records a difference of opinion on this matter between the School of Shammai and the School of Hillel. In this instance, the halachah follows the School of Shammai.</i> It is considered as if he did not eat inside the sukkah.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah (ibid.) continues:An incident occurred when the elders of the School of Shammai and the elders of the School of Hillel went to visit Rabbi Yochanan ben Hachoroni and found him sitting with his head and the majority of his body in the sukkah while his table was in his house...<br>The elders of the School of Shammai told him: "If this has been your practice, you have never fulfilled the mitzvah of sukkah."Rabbenu Nissim questions how the Sages' decree can negate the fulfillment of a mitzvah, and thus explains the last phrase to mean: "You have never fulfilled the mitzvah as desired by our Sages." However, <i>Tosafot, Sukkah</i> 3a explains that the Sages' decree is powerful enough to nullify the person's actions entirely, and it is considered as if he never fulfilled the mitzvah at all.</i> [Rather,] the table must also be inside the sukkah. This was decreed lest one be drawn after one's table. This law applies even in a large sukkah.
Throughout the seven days [of the festival], a person should read in the sukkah.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Since the person must regard his sukkah as his home throughout the festival, in addition to eating and sleeping, he must perform all his other day-to-day functions inside of it. Hence, he should also read in the sukkah.</i> However, when he attempts to comprehend what he reads in depth and appreciate its details, he should do so outside the sukkah,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> explains that any time it is necessary to study a concept in depth, one should do so outside the sukkah...</i> so that his mind will be settled.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">while he studies. The <i>Magen Avraham</i> 639:13 adds a further reason to study at home: the difficulty in bringing one's texts back and forth from the sukkah. The <i>Magen Avraham</i> explains that this matter also depends on the individual person. If he is as comfortable studying in the sukkah as studying at home, he is obligated to do so.</i><br>When a person prays,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">at a time when he is unable to pray in the synagogue (<i>Magen Avraham</i> 639:14)</i> he may pray inside the sukkah or outside the sukkah, as he desires.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">depending on where he will be able to concentrate more.</i>
If rain descends, a person may enter his home.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The descent of rain on Sukkot implies that our actions are not desired by God. The Mishnah (<i>Sukkah</i> 2:8) compares the matter to a servant who came to serve his master drink and the latter poured the pitcher in his face.<br><i>Tzafenat Paneach</i> explains that there is a fundamental difference between this law and the case of a person who is uncomfortable while in the sukkah. In the latter instance, in essence, the person is obligated to fulfill the mitzvah. However, because of his discomfort, his obligation is temporarily waived. In contrast, when rain descends it is impossible to fulfill the mitzvah at all, because the sukkah is not fit to dwell in. Hence, there is no obligation whatsoever.<br>There is a practical difference between these two abstract positions regarding the question whether a person is obligated to eat in the sukkah on the first night of the holiday even when it rains. (See the commentary on Halachah 7.) According to the <i>Tzafenat Paneach</i>, under these circumstances there is no mitzvah which one is required to fulfill. Thus, one may eat at home. In contrast, most authorities do not differentiate between rain and other uncomfortable situations. Thus, since eating in the sukkah on that night is a binding obligation, they require a person to fulfill the mitzvah.<br>There is a further aspect to this concept. The Maharil explains that a person who eats in a sukkah when he is not obligated to do so is a simple person and receives no reward for his "piety." On this basis, in many communities, people make no effort to stay in the sukkah when it rains. In contrast, there are some communities where people will continue to eat in the sukkah despite pouring rain.<br>What is the difference between these two approaches? The first approach follows the perspective that, during the rain, there is no mitzvah to dwell in the sukkah whatsoever. Hence, there is no need to strain oneself to remain there. In contrast, the second perspective does not differentiate between rain and any other uncomfortable circumstances, but maintains that, in essence, dwelling in the sukkah is a mitzvah even under such circumstances. Accordingly, they remain in the sukkah, because while they are involved in fulfilling God's will they do not feel even the slightest trace of discomfort.</i> When is one permitted to leave [the sukkah]?<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The Maharil writes that when a person is forced to leave the sukkah because of rain, he should depart with humility rather than with anger.</i> When enough raindrops descend into the sukkah so that they would spoil a cooked dish<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">We need not wait until the food actually spoils. As soon as enough rain descends to spoil the food, one is free to enter one's home (Ramah, <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 639:5).</i> - even a dish of beans<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., a dish made from bean meal, which spoils quickly (Rambam, Commentary on the Mishnah, <i>Sukkah</i> 2:8).</i> - were they to fall into it.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">were they to fall into it.</i><br>If a person was eating in the sukkah and rain descended, and hence, he entered his home, if the rains stop we do not obligate him to return to his sukkah<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">to finish his meal...</i> {that entire night}<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">This phrase is set off by brackets because it is most likely a printing error. It is not easily understood, nor is it found in the manuscript copies of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> or in the quotation of this halachah in the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 639:6).</i> until he is finished eating.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">However, after he concludes eating he must return to the sukkah.</i><br>If he was sleeping and rain descended,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 639:7) emphasizes that even the slightest amount of rain is sufficient to free one from the obligation of sleeping in the sukkah.</i> and hence, he entered his home, we do not obligate him to return to his sukkah that entire night should the rains cease.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The same law applies if it was raining when he desired to go to sleep, and hence, he never slept in the sukkah at all (<i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 639:39).</i> Rather, he may remain sleeping in his house that entire night<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">even if he wakes up in the middle of the night and it is no longer raining, he is not required to move to the sukkah.</i> until dawn.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> and Rabbenu Manoach note that the Rambam's choice of phraseology implies that the person cannot sleep his normal measure, but must rise at dawn to return to the sukkah. They are not stringent and allow the person to remain sleeping at home until he wakes up. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 639:7) quotes the Rambam's statements verbatim. However, the Ramah includes this leniency.</i>
A person should not take apart his sukkah after he finishes eating on the seventh day [of Sukkot].<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">lest he require it later during the day.</i> However, from the afternoon on, he may take down his utensils and remove them.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Keeping one's utensils in the sukkah is included in the mitzvah of dwelling within it, as mentioned in Halachah 5. Nevertheless, one is allowed to take them home to prepare them for use on Shemini Atzeret as a sign of deference to that holiday. (See <i>Sukkah</i> 4:7.)</i><br>If he has no place to put the utensils,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., if he used his home as a sukkah, and thus must continue to remain there on Shemini Atzeret (see <i>Sukkah</i> 48a)...</i> he should reduce its space<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">by opening up [a portion of s'chach]</i> by at least four handbreadths by four handbreadths.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">to make the sukkah, or at least a significant portion of it, unfit for use. This safeguards against the violation of the command of בל תוסיף (Deuteronomy 13:1 , which forbids adding to the mitzvot of the Torah. These acts will differentiate between his eating in the sukkah during the festival, when he is required to do so in fulfillment of the mitzvah, and eating there afterwards, once the mitzvah has been completed (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 666).<br>The Ramah adds that this requirement applies only when one desires to eat in the sukkah on Shemini Atzeret. Once that holiday passes, one may eat in a sukkah without making any changes.</i><br>If he has to eat later that day,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">even if he has removed his utensils or taken away part of the <i>s'chach</i>...</i> he must eat in the sukkah, because the mitzvah extends throughout the seven days.
Whenever a person enters a sukkah with the intention of sitting down throughout the seven [days of Sukkot], he should recite the following blessing<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Note the commentary on Halachah 7, which explains the difference between this law and the rules governing the blessing recited before eating matzah.</i> before sitting:<br>[Blessed<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam maintains that by sitting or doing any other activity which one would perform in one's home in the sukkah, one fulfills the mitzvah. Hence, the blessing is recited before one performs the mitzvah, as required by <i>Hilchot Berachot</i> 11:2.<br>The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> questions why the blessing is not recited before one enters the sukkah, noting that the interpretation of the command לישב בסוכה is "to dwell in the sukkah," not "to sit in the sukkah." However, the <i>Taz</i> 643:2 explains that merely passing through the sukkah is not part of the mitzvah. Rather, one must perform an activity that reflects "dwelling." Hence, the blessing is not recited until one sits down, because previously it is not obvious that one wants to perform a significant activity in the sukkah.<br>The Ra'avad objects to the Rambam's decision, maintaining that one should recite the blessing only before partaking of a meal in the sukkah. This reflects the decision of the Ashkenazic authorities, who maintain that though the performance of all one's activities in the sukkah is a mitzvah, it is not customary to recite the blessing except when eating. When reciting the blessing on that occasion, one should intend to include all other activities. (See <i>Tur</i> and <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 639:8.)</i> are You...] who has sanctified us with His commandments and commanded us to dwell in the sukkah.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> states that there is no limit to the number of times one recites the blessing each day. Nevertheless, if one left the sukkah for a brief interval or left with the intent of performing an activity which would improve the sukkah, that departure is not considered an interruption and no blessing is required upon one's return.</i><br>On the first night of the festival, one should first recite the blessing on the sukkah, and then the blessing for the occasion.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., the blessing <i>shehecheyanu</i>, which thanks God for enabling us to reach this occasion. This blessing is recited whenever one performs a mitzvah that can be fulfilled only from time to time (<i>Hilchot Berachot</i> 11:9).<br>That halachah and, similarly, <i>Sukkah</i> 46a, imply that, at the outset, one should recite the blessing <i>shehecheyanu</i> upon construction of the sukkah. Though the Rambam makes no mention of that concept here, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 641:1) states that one should have the construction of the sukkah in mind when reciting <i>shehecheyanu</i> at night.</i><br>One should recite all the blessings over a cup of wine.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">as did Rav Kahana (<i>Sukkah</i> 46a).</i> Thus, one should recite <i>kiddush</i><sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">for the festival</i> while standing,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 643:2) states that kiddush may be recited while sitting.</i> recite the blessing <i>leishev basukkah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Though one might presume that <i>shehecheyanu</i> would be recited first, since it is more frequent, precedence is given to <i>leishev basukkah</i>, because it is "the mitzvah of the day" (Rabbenu Manoach).</i> sit, and then recite the blessing <i>shehecheyanu</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">On the second night, it is Ashkenazic custom to recite to the blessing <i>shehecheyanu</i> before the blessing <i>leishev basukkah</i>, since on that occasion the blessing is associated with the celebration of the festival and not the mitzvah of sukkah. (See <i>Bnei Binyamin</i>.)</i><br>This was the custom of my teachers and the Rabbis<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Kapach notes that most of the manuscripts of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> state רבי "the great men of" and not רבני, "the Rabbis of."</i> of Spain:<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad objects and comments that even in Spain such a custom was not followed.</i> to recite <i>kiddush</i> while standing on the first night of the Sukkot festival, as explained.
At present,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">in the Diaspora and in certain places in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> (<i>Hilchot Kiddush HaChodesh</i>, Chapter 9)</i> when we celebrate holidays for two days,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">because originally, the messengers sent by the Sanhedrin could not reach there in time to notify them when the holiday should be celebrated. Hence, even now, when we follow the fixed calendar, we continue to follow this custom. (See <i>Hilchot Kiddush Hachodesh</i>, Chapters 3 and 5.)</i> we dwell in the sukkah for eight days.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">In deference to the possibility that the festival could have begun on the second day and the seven days should be counted from then.<br>The commandment בל תוסיף (Deuteronomy 13:1  forbids adding to the mitzvot of the Torah. Indeed, in Halachot 11 and 14, we find certain safeguards instituted because of this commandment. Nevertheless, dwelling in the sukkah on the eighth day does not constitute a violation of this commandment, because the time when the mitzvah is to be performed has already passed. Placing a fifth passage in one's tefillin during the day violates this commandment, because we are obligated to perform the mitzvah at this time. However, once the seven days of Sukkot pass, there is no mitzvah associated with the sukkah whatsoever (<i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 28b).<br>Nevertheless, many Ashkenazic authorities would carry out additional safeguards regarding this matter. For example, the <i>Tur</i> relates (though he opposes this practice himself) that some follow the custom of eating in the Sukkah only on the day of Shemini Atzeret, but not the night. Many of the Rabbis who would sleep in the Sukkah throughout the festival refrain from doing so on Shemini Atzeret. (See <i>K'tav Sofer</i>, Responsum 120.)</i> On the eighth day, which is the first day of the holiday of Shemini Atzeret,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">As its name implies, Shemini Atzeret is the eighth day of Sukkot. However, it also has certain aspects in which it is considered a holiday in its own right (<i>Sukkah</i> 48a).</i> we dwell in the sukkah, but do not recite the blessing <i>leishev basukkah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">because as the Rambam explains, a blessing is not recited when a mitzvah is performed only because of a doubt.<br><i>Sukkah</i> 47a relates that "Rav Huna bar Bizna and all the great Sages of the generation dwelled in a sukkah on the eighth day - which might have been the seventh day - [of the festival], but did not recite a blessing."</i><br>Similarly, a <i>tumtum</i> and an <i>androgynous</i><sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 2, Halachah 1 for a definition of these terms and these individuals' status.</i> never recite the blessing <i>leishev basukkah</i>, because their obligation [to perform the mitzvah is based] on doubt,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., they are required to perform the mitzvah because of the doubt concerning their status: maybe they are to be considered men.</i> and a blessing is never recited when one is doubtful [of one's obligation].<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">This follows the rationale that when there is a doubt concerning one's obligation in matters required by the Torah itself, one must follow the more stringent view. However, regarding obligations of a Rabbinic nature, one may opt towards leniency. Since the mitzvah is required by the Torah, even a person in doubt must perform it. However, the blessing is a Rabbinic requirement; hence, there is no necessity in reciting it. Furthermore, by reciting the blessing when one is not required, one uses God's name in vain.<br>This perspective is not accepted by all authorities. As explained in the commentary on Chapter 2, Halachah 1, the Ashkenazic practice is to allow women and others who are not obligated in the performance of a mitzvah to recite a blessing when fulfilling it.<br>The above applies only regarding the doubts concerning the obligations of a <i>tumtum</i> and an <i>androgynous</i>. However, on Shemini Atzeret, when there is a doubt concerning the obligation of the entire Jewish people even the Ashkenazic authorities would agree that a blessing should not be recited.</i>
After a person finishes eating on the eighth day [of Sukkot], he should take down his utensils and remove them.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">from the sukkah and bring them home, where he will eat on Simchat Torah.</i><br>When a person has no place to put the utensils,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">and desires to continue eating in the sukkah on Simchat Torah,</i> [the following rules<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">safeguards adopted to differentiate between eating in the sukkah as required, and eating there afterwards.</i> apply]: If it is a small [sukkah], he should bring his candelabra into it;<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in Halachah 5, a candelabra should not be brought into a small sukkah for Sukkot.<br>It must be noted that the Rambam changes the text of <i>Sukkah</i> 48a, which states: "light his lamp there." (See <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, Halachah 5.)</i> if it is a large sukkah, he should bring in his pots, plates, and the like,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">which are generally kept outside the sukkah, as explained in Halachah 5.</i> to bring to mind that it<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">the sukkah</i> is no longer acceptable and that its mitzvah is completed. Since the day is a holiday, one may not reduce its space and nullify it.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">as suggested in Halachah 11.</i>
A person who did not construct a sukkah [before the holiday]<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">whether intentionally or unintentionally - <i>Sukkah</i> 27b explains that not only a person whose sukkah fell down in the middle of the festival should reconstruct it, but even one who never built a sukkah at all is entitled to do so.</i> - whether intentionally or unintentionally - should construct a sukkah on Chol Hamo'ed.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The literal translation of Deuteronomy 16:13 is: "Make the Sukkot festival for seven days." <i>Sukkah</i> 27b interprets this verse to mean that a sukkah may be built during the seven days of the festival.<br>The Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Sukkah</i> 2:7) explains that Rabbi Eleazar wanted to forbid a person who purposely did not build a sukkah from doing so during the festival, as a punishment for his failure to prepare for the festival. However, the Sages did not accept that premise.<br>A sukkah may not be constructed on the first day of the festival, even if the walls are already built and all that is necessary is to spread <i>s'chach</i> over it. However, even though certain types of work are forbidden on Chol Hamo'ed, there is no prohibition in this instance (<i>Bi'ur Halachah</i> 637).</i> One should even construct a sukkah on the final moments of the seventh day, because its mitzvah lasts throughout the seventh day.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 11.</i><br>The wood with which the sukkah was constructed is forbidden [to be used for other purposes]<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">e.g., for use as a toothpick. This prohibition applies even in the event the sukkah falls. Also, in contrast to the decorations, as mentioned in the following halachah, this prohibition cannot be nullified by making a condition that one desires to use them for one's own purposes (Ramah, <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 638:1).</i> on all eight days of the festival.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 9a derives the prohibition against using the wood of the sukkah for other purposes throughout Sukkot from Leviticus 23:34: "The Sukkot festival shall be seven days unto God." The latter term is used also regarding the <i>Chaggigah</i> offering sacrificed on the festival. Thus, its use in this context implies that the entities which enable us to celebrate the Sukkot festival must be consecrated "unto God" like those sacrifices.<br>The reason why the wood of the sukkah may not be used on the eighth day is explained below.</i> This applies to both the wood used for the walls and the wood used for the <i>s'chach</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbenu Asher differs and explains that the essential element of the sukkah is its <i>s'chach</i>. Hence, the prohibition applies only to the <i>s'chach</i> and not to the sukkah walls. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 638:1) quotes the Rambam's opinion. However, the <i>Taz</i> (638:1) explains that the prohibition against using the walls is only Rabbinic in nature.<br>Rav Chayim Soloveitchik explains that though the word sukkah as used by the Torah refers to the <i>s'chach</i> and not to the walls, a different principle is involved in this instance. The Torah teaches us that all the entities which enable us to fulfill the mitzvah of sukkah may not be used for other purposes. Since the sukkah walls also serve that purpose, because a sukkah is not kosher without walls, they are also included in that prohibition.</i> Throughout [these] eight days,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Note the contrast to the etrog in Chapter 7, Halachah 27.</i> no benefit may be derived from it for other purposes.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">However, if the sukkah falls, it may be rebuilt using the same wood. Furthermore, the wood used for <i>s'chach</i> may be used for the walls and vice versa.</i><br>[They are prohibited on the eighth day] because the sukkah is <i>muktzeh</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Muktzeh</i> literally means "set aside." In this context, it refers to objects which may not be used for mundane purposes.</i> the entire seventh day, including the period <i>beyn hash'mashot</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Beyn hash'mashot</i> is the period between sunset and the emergence of three stars.</i> Since it was <i>muktzeh</i> during the period <i>beyn hash'mashot</i>, it is <i>muktzeh</i> on the entire day [that follows].<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Shabbat</i> 25:10 states: "Any utensil which is forbidden to be moved during <i>beyn hash'mashot</i> is forbidden to be moved throughout the entire Sabbath, even after the factor that caused its prohibition has passed."</i>
Similarly, it is forbidden to take from the food and beverages that were hung in the sukkah as decorations<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">even if they fall (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 638:2). As mentioned in the commentary on Halachot 17 and 18, the sukkah decorations are not considered an independent entity, but rather as part of the sukkah itself. Furthermore, employing articles used to decorate a mitzvah for other purposes demonstrates lack of respect for the mitzvah (Rabbenu Nissim, <i>Beitzah</i>). Hence, the same restrictions that apply to the sukkah apply to it.</i> for all eight days [of the festival].<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">as is the sukkah itself.</i> However, if at the time one hung [the decorations],<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbenu Manoach questions what the law would be if the condition were made after the decorations were hung but before the holiday commences.</i> he made the condition: "I will not refrain from using them during the entire period of <i>beyn hash'mashot</i>,"<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Everything depends on the status of the decorations at the time the festival begins. If they are not considered part of the sukkah at that time, the "sanctity of the sukkah" does not affect them throughout the festival (<i>Sukkah</i> 10b).<br>The Ramban and the Ramah differ, and require that the person stipulate that he will not refrain from using the decorations during the entire period of <i>beyn hash'mashot</i> of each of the eight days of the festival. If he makes the condition regarding the first day alone, the decorations become forbidden on the subsequent days of the festival.</i> he is entitled to use them whenever he desires,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to the walls or <i>s'chach</i>, in this instance the condition has an effect. It is possible for a sukkah to exist without decorations, but not without walls (Rabbenu Manoach).</i> because he did not set them aside, nor did the sanctity of the sukkah encompass them, nor are they considered part of it.

Chapter 7

The term "the frond of the date palm" employed by the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Leviticus 23:40.</i> refers to the branches of a date palm<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Tzafenat Paneach</i> questions whether or not the tree from which the lulav is taken must actually produce dates.</i> as they sprout, before their leaves separate<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Thus, if the leaves spread out and were later bound together by human activity, it is not acceptable (Ramah, <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 645:2).</i> and spread out in various directions.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Periodically, the date palm sprouts forth new branches. Initially they are closed, and as time passes they spread out to a fanlike shape. The Torah requires that they be used for the lulav while they are still in their initial state.<br>This obligation is derived from the fact that the Torah spells the word כפת, without a ו, implying that the date branch should appear to be a single entity (<i>Sukkah</i> 32a).<br>There is a homiletic aspect to using the lulav while its leaves are closed. <i>Vayikra Rabbah</i> 30:12 emphasizes how the mitzvah of lulav and etrog expresses the unity and oneness which pervade the Jewish people. Not only is this unity expressed by the combination of the four species into a single mitzvah, it is reflected in each of the species themselves. Thus, the lulav is used while its leaves are together as one, before they separate into distinct entities.</i> Rather, they should appear as a scepter.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Here, too, our Sages have emphasized the homiletic lesson to be derived from this shape. Rosh Hashanah and Yom Kippur are days of judgement. The lulav can be considered as the Jews' scepter of victory, acknowledging that they have prevailed (<i>Medrash Tanchumah, Emor</i>).</i> This is called a lulav.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">This is called a lulav.</i>
The "fruit of the beautiful tree" mentioned in the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Leviticus, ibid.</i> is the etrog.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 35a explains that this term is used to refer to the etrog because of a unique quality possessed by this fruit. The taste of the tree itself resembles the taste of the fruit.
