Alternatively, the word הדר can be interpreted to mean "which dwells," and thus refer to the etrog, which can grow on the tree for an entire year.</i><br>The "boughs of covered trees" mentioned in the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Leviticus, ibid.</i> refer to the [species of] myrtle whose leaves surround its branch;<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">thus, covering the branch</i> i.e., there will be three or more leaves in each ring.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">on the same level.<br>Our translation follows the interpretation of the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i>, who requires that the three leaves be on the same level over the entire length of the myrtle. However, other opinions interpret the Rambam's words to mean that a myrtle may be used if the leaves of merely one ring are on the same level. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> (and similarly, the <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 646:5) maintain that a myrtle is kosher if the leaves grow properly over three handbreadths (its minimum size), or at least the majority of that distance.</i> However, if there are two leaves on one level, with a third leaf slightly higher than them, that is not considered to be "covered."<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">but rather from a different species of tree. Hence, it can never be used in the lulav (<i>Or Sameach</i>).</i> Rather, it is called a wild myrtle.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">since its leaves do not grow in the normal pattern.</i>
The term "willows of the brook" mentioned by the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Leviticus, ibid.</i> does not include just any plant that grows by a brook, but rather a particular species, which is called the "willows of the brook."<br>Its<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">That species is defined as follows:</i> leaf is extended as a brook,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., it comes to a point, rather than being rounded (<i>Sukkah</i> 33b).<br>This characteristic is also quoted as an explanation of the name "willows of the brook" (Rabbenu Asher).</i> its edge is smooth,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">rather than jagged (<i>Sukkah</i> ibid.).</i> and its stem is red. It is called a willow. The majority of this species grow near brooks. Therefore, it is called the "willows of the brook."<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">However, the use of that name is not meant to be exclusive...</i> Even if this species grew in the desert or on a mountain, it would be kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">for the fulfillment of this mitzvah.</i>
There is another species which resembles the willow. However, its leaf is rounded, its edge resembles a saw,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., its edge is very jagged, with large protrusions</i> and its stem is not red.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">but white (<i>Sukkah</i> 34a).</i> This is called a <i>tzaftzefah</i>. It is unfit [to be used for the mitzvah].<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">for it is not considered to be merely a different type of willow, but rather another species entirely.</i><br>There is another type of willow, whose leaf<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">is also extended</i> does not have a smooth edge, but it is not like a saw. Rather, it has tiny juttings, like the edge of a small sickle. It is kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 647:6 mentions that willows are frequently picked by young children who are not learned and may mistake a non-kosher species for a kosher one. Hence, the purchaser should carefully examine the willows before purchasing them.</i><br>All the above definitions were explained according to the oral tradition transmitted by Moses, our teacher.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Indeed, in his introduction to his Commentary on the Mishnah, the Rambam uses the definitions of the four species as examples of how the oral tradition is explained in the Talmud:The explanations which we have received from Moses are not contested at all. Throughout all the ages, from Moses' time until the present, we have not heard of a dispute started by a Sage whether... the expression "a fruit from a beautiful tree" refers to the etrog. Nor have we heard of a dispute that the "covered tree" refers to the myrtle...<br>Concerning these and the like, it has been said: "The entire Torah, [both the mitzvot] in general, and all their particulars were given to Moses on Mount Sinai." Though the tradition was received and there is no dispute about it, one can also derive these definitions through the accepted processes of exegesis.<br>Thus, when the Talmud debates and discusses a concept and offers a suggestion...that perhaps, "the fruit of the beautiful tree" is a pomegranate...[and does not resolve the matter] until they bring proof from the exegesis of the verse..., one should not conclude that the matter was left in doubt until this point of exegesis was discovered.<br>Rather, from the time of Joshua onward, we saw that the etrog was the species taken with the lulav and there is no debate about that. They were merely investigating how they could find support from the Torah for the interpretation that had been transmitted.</i>
These four species are considered to be one mitzvah,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to putting on the head tefillin and the arm tefillin, which are considered to be two mitzvot. Here, taking all four species is considered to be a single mitzvah...</i> and<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">therefore,...</i> each one<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">of the species...</i> is required for its performance.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">and only then is the mitzvah performed. Nevertheless, as explained in Halachah 6, the species need not be bound together; it is sufficient to take them one after the other.<br>This statement is taken from the Mishnah, <i>Menachot</i> 3:6, which includes the four species of the lulav in a long list of mitzvot in which all the particular elements that make up the mitzvah are required for its performance to be acceptable.</i> All of them [together] are called the mitzvah of lulav.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Since the lulav is the tallest of all the species, the entire mitzvah is referred to by this name (<i>Sukkah</i> 37b).</i> One may not diminish them<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">doing so violates the commandment בל תגרע (Deuteronomy 13:1 , which forbids diminishing the Torah's commandments.</i> or add to them.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">doing so violates the commandment תוסיף בל (Deuteronomy 13:1 , which forbids adding to the Torah's commandments.</i> If one of the species cannot be found, a similar species<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">e.g., a <i>tzaftzefah</i> for the willow, or a lemon for the etrog</i> may not be substituted for it.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Rather, the mitzvah cannot be fulfilled at all. Nevertheless, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 651:12) recommends taking the species that are available, as a remembrance of the mitzvah. However, in such circumstances, a blessing should not be recited.</i>
The most desirable way of performing the mitzvah is to bind the lulav, myrtle, and willow together, thus making a single, unified entity from the three of them.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 11b explains that binding the three species together is considered more attractive than taking them each individually. Thus, taking the species in this manner conforms to the general directive requiring us to perform the mitzvot in the most esthetically appealing manner possible.<br><i>Sukkah</i> 34b mentions an exegetic teaching that explains why the etrog is not bound together with the other species.</i><br>Before one takes them to perform the mitzvah, he should recite the blessing<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">for the blessings should always be recited before the performance of the mitzvot.</i> on the mitzvah of taking the lulav,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation follows the commentary of the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, who notes that in <i>Hilchot Berachot</i> 11:15, the Rambam states that if one recites the blessing before taking the lulav in his hand, he should conclude the blessing <i>...v'tzivanu litol lulav</i>. The blessing should be concluded <i>...v'tzivanu al netilat lulav</i> only if one has already taken the lulav in his hands.</i> for all the others are dependent upon it.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in the previous halachah and commentary, since the lulav is the tallest of all the species, its name is used to refer to the entire mitzvah.</i><br>Afterwards, he takes this bound entity in his right hand and the etrog in his left hand.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Since three of the four species are bound together, they are held in the hand which the Torah considers of greater prominence (<i>Sukkah</i> 37b).</i> He must take them as they grow<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 45b derives this concept from Exodus 26:15, which states: "upright beams of acacia wood." Implied is that all mitzvot fulfilled with agricultural products must be performed while they are in an upright position. (See also Halachah 9.)</i> - i.e., their roots below towards the earth, and their heads upward towards the sky.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the etrog hangs from the tree with the <i>pitam</i> downwards, its "upright" position is when the <i>pitam</i> faces upward (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 651:12).</i><br>If a person did not bind them together, but rather took them one by one, he has fulfilled his obligation, provided he possesses all four species.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 11b states that it is a mitzvah to bind the three species together, but one may fulfill the mitzvah even when one has not done so.<br>This law is accepted as halachah (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 651:12). The preference of the Rabbis for binding the species together is so great that if one has not bound them together before the beginning of the festival, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 651:1) recommends binding them together with a loop on the holiday rather than taking each one individually.</i> However, if he has only one species or he is lacking one species, he should not take them until he acquires the remaining species.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">as explained in the previous halachah.</i>
How many does one take of each of them? One lulav,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 34b derives this concept from the fact that Leviticus 23:40 writes כפת without a ו, implying a single entity, as mentioned in the commentary on Halachah 1.</i> one etrog,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">because the above verse mentions "a fruit (singular) from the beautiful tree" (<i>Sukkah</i>, ibid.).</i> two willow branches,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">because the above verse states "willows of the brook," using the plural, and thus two are required (ibid.)</i> and three myrtle branches.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The above verse uses three words ענף עץ עבות, each word implying the addition of another branch (Rashi, <i>Sukkah</i>, ibid.).</i> If one would like to add more myrtle<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Some editions of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> also add "and willow." However, most of the manuscripts and texts of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> omit that phrase. Furthermore, in one of his responsa, the Rambam states that since the Talmud mentions adding only myrtles, it is improper to add any of the other species.</i> branches so that the bundle will be larger, he may. Indeed, it is considered to be an adornment of the mitzvah.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The latter statement can be understood to be an explanation of the former. In contrast to the other species, the myrtle is considered an adornment of the mitzvah. Since the extra myrtle branches are viewed as adornments, they are not considered to be an intervening substance separating the person from the mitzvah. (See the commentary on Halachah 11.) Nor is including them considered to be adding to the mitzvah (and thus, a violation of בל תוסיף). (See also Rabbenu Nissim and the Rashba, Vol. I, Responsum 535.)<br>There are some authorities who allow additional myrtle branches to be included even though they do not meet the requirement of having all three leaves on the same level. However, others do not accept this leniency (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 651:15).</i> However, it is forbidden to add to or reduce the numbers of the other species.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">because they are not considered to be "adornments."</i> If one adds to or reduces their number, it is not acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad objects to this statement, arguing that the adding to the numbers of the other species does not nullify the performance of the mitzvah. Rav Avraham, the Rambam's son, writes that, based on <i>Sanhedrin</i> 88b, the Rambam amended his own manuscript copy of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> to state "it does not nullify it" (<i>Birkat Avraham</i> 31).</i>
What is the required length of each of these species?<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 650:8 states that if the species are smaller than the minimum limits established, they may not be used throughout the festival. Though certain leniencies are granted after the first day, they do not apply regarding this matter.</i> The lulav may not be less than four handbreadths.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah (<i>Sukkah</i> 3:1) describes the length of the lulav as "three handbreadths [and more], so that it can be shaken," implying that, like the other species, it should be three handbreadths in length. However, since all three handbreadths of the lulav must be shaken (see the following two halachot) an additional handbreadth was required for the person to hold the lulav in his hand (Rambam, Commentary on the Mishnah).<br>[Rashi and most other commentaries based on <i>Sukkah</i> 32b consider the handbreadth of the lulav that extends beyond the myrtle and the willow as the addition included "so that it can be shaken." From the latter clauses of this halachah, it appears that the Rambam may have also adopted this interpretation.]<br>A handbreadth is 8 centimeters according to <i>Shiurei Torah</i>, and 9.6 centimeters according to the <i>Chazon Ish</i>. In his commentary on the above Mishnah, the Rambam emphasizes that the measure refers to three full handbreadths, each four fingerbreadths in length. This is necessary to negate the opinion of certain authorities (see the Ra'avad), who state that here the intent is three "small" handbreadths, so that the total length is only 10 fingerbreadths.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 650:1) accepts the Ra'avad's opinion. Nevertheless, the Ramah suggests following the Rambam's view if possible.</i> [Beyond that,] regardless of its length, it is kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Menachot</i> 42a states that a lulav has a minimum length, but no maximum length.</i> Its length is measured only from its <i>shidrah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., what is measured is its center stem from its base until the portion which separates into two twin leaves that cling to each other.</i> and not from the tips of the leaves.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., its full length.</i><br>The myrtle and the willow may not be less than three handbreadths. [Beyond that,] regardless of their length, they are kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The latter principle is derived from the laws of the lulav.</i> Even if each branch has only three fresh leaves on it, they are kosher, provided the leaves are at the top of the branch.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 33a states:If most of [the myrtle's] leaves dried out, but three fresh leaves remain, it is kosher.<br>Rav Chisda said: "Provided they are at the top of each branch."The Rambam maintains that the same principles can also be applied to the willow.<br>[Note the commentary on Halachah 8:5, which mentions certain relevant principles. Indeed, in general, this clause appears to be more closely related to the principles mentioned in the following chapter, where the Rambam mentions the characteristics that disqualify the various species, rather than in this chapter, where he relates the fundamental requirements of each one. Based on <i>Sukkah</i> 34a, which relates that myrtles which are not dry (a factor still common in many Diaspora communities today) are very difficult to find, we can interpret this as an almost parenthetical expression teaching that freshness is not among the fundamental requirements for a myrtle.]</i><br>If one has bound [the other species together with] the lulav, the <i>shidrah</i> of the lulav must extend beyond the myrtle and the willow a handbreadth or more.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam maintains that regardless of the length of the myrtles and the willows, the <i>shidrah</i> of the lulav must extend beyond them an additional handbreadth, so that it can be shaken. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (ibid.:2) does not require adherence to the Rambam's view. Nevertheless, it is accepted without question by <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 650:2.</i><br>The minimum size of an etrog is the size of an egg.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">57.6 cubic centimeters according to <i>Shiurei Torah</i>; in this context, 100 cubic centimeters according to the <i>Chazon Ish</i>.</i> [Beyond that,] regardless of its size, it is kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 36b relates that Rabbi Akiva came to synagogue with an etrog so large he had to sling it over his shoulder. (This is not out of the question, because, as mentioned above, an etrog is capable of remaining on its tree for an entire year and can attain quite a large size.)</i>
Once a person lifts up these four species<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">whether he lifts them up together - as described in Halachah 6.</i> - whether he lifts them up together or one after the other<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">lifting up each of the four species individually. However, a person must have all four species before him when he fulfills the mitzvah.</i> - whether in his right hand or in his left hand<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Although, in Halachah 6, the Rambam suggested holding the lulav together with the myrtle and willows together in his right hand and the etrog in his left, this is the most desirable way of fulfilling the mitzvah. However, even if a person does not lift up the species in this manner...</i> - he has fulfilled his obligation.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbenu Chanan'el does not accept this decision. He interprets <i>Sukkah</i> 42a, "If he lifted it up in an opposite manner, he did not fulfill his obligation," as referring to such a circumstance. Nevertheless, the Rambam (and similarly, most halachic authorities, including the Ramah, <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 651:3) interpret that statement as referring to lifting them up opposite to their natural pattern of growth.</i> [This applies] only when he lifts them up as they grow.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 6 and the commentary on it.</i> However, if he does not lift them up as they grow, he has not fulfilled his obligation.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">For this reason, it is customary in many communities to hold the etrog upside down before reciting the blessing, and then to turn it right side up after the blessing has been recited.</i><br>The fulfillment of the mitzvah as the law [requires is as follows]: One should lift up the three species as they are bound together in one's right hand and the etrog in one's left hand.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">holding the etrog together with the lulav (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 651:11).<br>In the <i>Beit Yosef</i>, Rav Yosef Karo quotes the following story from the Recanti. The latter dreamed that he saw a particular pious individual writing God's name with a space separating the last ה from the first three letters. He could not comprehend the dream at all until the next day, when he saw that individual holding his etrog separate from his lulav (<i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 651:21).</i> Then, one should pass them back and forth, up and down,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The Ari suggests that one should face the east and pass the lulav back and forth in the following order; first to the south; then to the north, then east, up, down, and to the west (<i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 651:20). <i>Sukkah</i> 37b relates that shaking the lulav in all directions prevents unfavorable winds.<br>When shaking the lulav downward, one should not turn it upside down, for this is opposite its natural pattern of growth. (See Ramah, <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 651:9.)</i> and shake the lulav three times in each direction.
What does the above entail? One passes the lulav forward<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">once in each direction</i> and shakes the top of the lulav three times,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">while the lulav is extended outward in that particular direction.</i> brings it back<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">to one's chest</i> and shakes the top of the lulav three times.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">while holding the lulav close to oneself.<br>The Ramah (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 651:9) explains that passing the lulav back and forth is itself considered to be shaking it. Hence, rather than shake the lulav three times while it is extended, one must shake it as one extends it and brings it back, and repeat that process a total of three times in each direction.</i> One follows this same pattern when lifting it up and down.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Thus, one will have shaken the lulav a total of 36 times.</i><br>At what point [in prayer]<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbenu Manoach states that the lulav was also passed back and forth and shaken at the time the blessing is recited. This is our custom at present.</i> does one pass the lulav back and forth? During the reading of the <i>Hallel</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">which is recited in its entirety on each day of the Sukkot festival.</i> at the first and final recitation of the verse [Psalms 118:1, 118:29]: <i>Hodu<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot, Sukkah</i> 37b explains the derivation of this practice as follows: 1 Chronicles 16:33 states: "Then all the trees of the forest will rejoice." The following verse (ibid. 34) states "<i>Hodu Lado-nai</i>...," and the subsequent verse: "Let them say <i>Hoshi'eynu</i>...."<br>The rejoicing of the trees - the shaking of the lulav - is thus associated with the verse "<i>Hodu</i>..." and the verse "<i>Ana Ado-nai hoshi'ah na</i>."</i> Lado-nai ki tov...</i> and at the verse [Psalms 118:25]: <i>Ana Ado-nai hoshi'ah na</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">It is customary to repeat this verse when reciting the <i>Hallel</i>. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 651:8) relates that the lulav is passed back and forth both times the verse is recited.</i><br>It is acceptable to take the lulav throughout the entire day.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">However, one should not delay the performance of the mitzvah unnecessarily. Indeed, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 652:2) forbids eating before fulfilling the mitzvah.</i> However, it is not taken at night.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Megillah</i> 20b derives this concept from Leviticus 23:40: "On the first <i>day</i> take...." We may infer: "the lulav is to be taken by day, and not by night."</i>
If one wraps a cord of silver or gold around [the three species] as they are bound together,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah (<i>Sukkah</i> 3:8) relates that the inhabitants of Jerusalem would wrap their lulavim together with golden cords.</i> or wraps a [decorative] cloth around them and takes them, one fulfills his obligation.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Though we are commanded to take the lulav, that does not necessarily imply actually holding the lulav in one's hands, because...</i> Taking the lulav through another medium is still considered to be taking it,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., holding another substance in which the lulav is contained is still considered to be holding the lulav...</i> provided [that medium] is one which gives honor and beauty [to the mitzvah, because]: "any entity which makes a substance more attractive is not considered to be a separation."<br>However,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">between one's hands and the lulav. The substance used to bind the lulav is not considered to be an independent entity, but rather an extension of the lulav. This principle is also mentioned in the commentary on Halachah 7 of this chapter and Halachot 5:17-18. The Ramah (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 651:8) states that it is customary to remove rings or other intervening substances that cover even small portions of one's hands before taking the lulav.</i> if one placed these species in a vase or a pot<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 42a explains that this is unbecoming to the mitzvah.</i> and took them, one has not fulfilled one's obligation.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 651:31 states that this applies even if the container in which one placed the species is made of silver or gold.</i>
If one binds the lulav together with the myrtle and the willow and separates between the lulav and the myrtle with a cloth or the like,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., a substance which does not contribute to the lulav's attractiveness</i> it is considered to be a separation.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">between the various species. Taking the lulav in this manner is not acceptable. The Ramah (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 651:1) notes that the myrtle branches are frequently bound together with a cord. This must be removed before using them to fulfill the mitzvah.</i> If one separates between them with myrtle leaves,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., if the myrtle leaves fall off and collect between the lulav and the myrtle branches,</i> it is not considered to be a separation, because an entity does not separate between its own kind.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This principle applies in many different contexts. (See also <i>Hilchot P'sulei Hamukdashin</i> 1:21 and <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh Hakorbanot</i> 19:5.)</i><br>One may bind the together with a string, a cord, or with any substance one desires,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., using substances other than the three species used in the lulav</i> since binding it together is not a required element of the mitzvah.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">the substance used to bind it is not considered to be significant. Therefore, using a different substance is not considered as adding a new entity to the mitzvah. However, if the binding were required, as one opinion (<i>Sukkah</i> 6b) maintains, it would be forbidden to use a different substance.</i>
The mitzvah of taking the lulav in every place,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., even outside Jerusalem</i> during every age<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., whether or not the Temple is standing</i> - even on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">applies only on the first day of the festival, as [Leviticus 23:40] states: "And on the first day, you shall take..." - See Halachah 16. See also the Rambam's Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Sukkah</i> 4:2).</i> - applies only on the first day of the festival, as [Leviticus 23:40] states: "And on the first day, you shall take..."<br>In the holy place alone,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Sukkah</i> 3:10), the Rambam states that the term מקדש refers to Jerusalem in its totality, not only the Temple Mount. Thus, the Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Sukkah</i> 3:11) states:"And you shall rejoice before God, your Lord, seven days" - in Jerusalem.(See also the commentary on Halachah 2:8.)</i> it is to be taken on each of the seven days of the festival, as [the above verse] continues: "and you shall rejoice before God, your Lord, [seven days]."<br>When the Sabbath falls during the [later] days, [the lulav] is not taken on the Sabbath.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">neither in Jerusalem (where taking the lulav is a mitzvah according to the Torah) nor outside the holy city (where taking the lulav is a Rabbinic decree, as stated in Halachah 15).</i> This is a decree<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">instituted by the Rabbis (<i>Sukkah</i> 43a)</i> lest one carry it four cubits in the public domain, as decreed regarding the shofar.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 2:6.</i>
Why was this decree not put in effect on the first day of the festival? Because [taking the lulav on that day] is a mitzvah from the Torah even outside of Jerusalem. Thus, the laws applying to it are not the same as those applying to the remaining days,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Taking the lulav on the first day was obviously a matter of great importance. Hence, the Sages did not feel that the fear that perhaps a person might carry the lulav in the public domain was sufficient reason to nullify the mitzvah. In contrast, on the subsequent days the mitzvah is not considered to be so severe a matter. Hence, the mitzvah could be nullified in Jerusalem as well.<br>The Rabbis question why the Sages differentiated between the lulav and the shofar, and (as explained in Halachah 2:6), nullified the mitzvah of hearing the shofar when Rosh Hashanah fell on the Sabbath. As explained in our commentary on that halachah, in Rabbenu Nissim's commentary on the tractate of <i>Sukkah</i>, he states that this decree was instituted only because in most Jewish communities, the people were not aware of the date the court had established for Rosh Hashanah. (Note the details of the explanation there.)</i> because on the subsequent days of the festival a person is obligated to take the lulav only in the holy place.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Thus, while the Temple was standing, the lulav would not be taken outside Jerusalem during the subsequent days of the festival.</i>
When the Temple was destroyed, [the Sages] ordained that the lulav be taken everywhere for the entire seven days of the festival, as a remembrance of the Temple.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 41a explains the source for the establishment of remembrances for the Temple practices:[Jeremiah 30:17] states: "'I will restore health to you. I will heal you of your wounds," says God. 'Because they called you an outcast. Zion, for whom no one cares.'<br>The verse states "for whom no one cares," implying that a show of our care is required.On this basis, Rabbi Yochanan ben Zakkai established a number of practices in remembrance of the Temple practices.</i><br>On each day,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">but only once a day (Ramah, <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 651:5).</i> one recites the blessing on it:<br><i>[Baruch Attah Ado-nai...] asher kid'shanu b'mitzvotav v'tzivanu al netilat lulav.</i><br>because<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in the commentary on Halachah 6, the Rambam explains in <i>Hilchot Berachot</i> 11:15 that it is preferable to conclude the blessing, <i>litol lulav</i>. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> maintains that the Rambam's present statement is only a reference to his previous one, and not a reversal of his opinion. Nevertheless, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 651:5) and all later authorities recommend reciting <i>al netilat lulav</i>.</i> it a mitzvah ordained by the Rabbis.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Hilchot Berachot</i> 11:3, the Rambam writes that a blessing should be recited before the performance of a Rabbinic commandment. It is appropriate to say <i>v'tzivanu</i> (and He commanded us) because the commandment to follow the Sages includes the observance of all their enactments.</i><br>This enactment, like the other enactments instituted by Rabbi Yochanan ben Zakkai when the Temple was destroyed [is only temporary]. When the Temple is rebuilt, these matters will return to their original status.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Tosefta, Rosh Hashanah</i> 2:7, states this concept explicitly. It is also obvious from <i>Beitzah</i> 5b, which explains that "when in the near future, the Temple will be rebuilt," difficulties may arise from following Rabbi Yochanan ben Zakkai's decree.</i>
While the Temple was standing,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">and the day when a new month began was established through the testimony of witnesses who saw the new moon.</i> the lulav would be taken [in the holy place<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., the city of Jerusalem</i> even] when the first day of Sukkot fell on the Sabbath.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">as stated above in Halachah 13.</i> The same applies in other places<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">throughout <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> and the Diaspora</i> where they were certain that this day<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">the fifteenth of Tishre</i> was celebrated as a holiday in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in <i>Hilchot Kiddush Hachodesh</i>, Chapter 3, the Sanhedrin would send messengers to all Jewish communities to inform them when each new month had begun. The communities which received this information before the Sukkot festival celebrated the holidays for only one day. They would be allowed to take the lulav when the first day of Sukkot fell on the Sabbath.<br><i>Sukkot</i> 41b relates:When the first day of the festival fell on the Sabbath, everyone would bring their lulav to the synagogue [on Friday]. On the following day, everyone would recognize his lulav and take it.This was the practice outside Jerusalem. <i>Sukkot</i> 42b describes the practice in the Temple.They would bring their lulavim to the Temple Mount [on Friday]. The attendants would take them from them and place them on benches. The elders would place their lulavim in chambers. They would teach them to say: "Whoever receives my lulav may consider it as a gift."<br>In the morning, they would arise and come [to the Temple]. The attendants would throw [the lulavim] to the people. They would grab them from one another and even come to violence. When the court saw that the situation was becoming dangerous, they ordained that each person should take [the lulav] in his own home.</i> However, the places which were distantly removed from Jerusalem<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">and were not informed of the day on which Rosh Hashanah had fallen, would celebrate two days. Therefore, they...</i> would not take the lulav on this day because of the doubt<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">whether or not the day was, in fact, Sukkot</i> involved.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., had they known for sure that the holiday began on this day, they would have taken the lulav. However, since they were not sure of that fact, the Sages did not want to risk the possible violation of the Sabbath laws.</i>
When the Temple was destroyed,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">even though the new moon was still sanctified according to the testimony of witnesses. (That practice continued several hundred years after the destruction of the Temple - <i>Hilchot Kiddush Hachodesh</i> 5:3.)</i> the Sages forbade even the inhabitants of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> who had sanctified the new month<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">and thus knew that they were certainly obligated to perform the mitzvah</i> to take the lulav on the Sabbath on the first day of Sukkot.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">for the reasons mentioned in Halachah 13.</i><br>[This was instituted] because of the inhabitants of the distant settlements, who were not aware of when the new month had been declared. Thus, a uniform guideline was established, rather than having some take the lulav on the Sabbath and some not.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The Sages did not want confusion to arise because of a difference in local custom. Accordingly, they were willing to decree that many Jews forego the performance of a mitzvah from the Torah, in order to establish uniformity throughout the Jewish people.</i> [The guiding principle was] that the obligation [of taking the lulav] on the first day applies in all places,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">and the people in the distant communities could not take the lulav when the first day fell on the Sabbath because of the doubt involved</i> and there is no longer a Temple to use as a point of distinction.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., while the Temple was standing, the difference in practice between the people living in places where the date of the month was known and those where it was not known could be explained because everyone knew that the lulav was taken on the first day in the Temple. However, when the Temple was destroyed, there was no point of distinction, and the Sages established a totally uniform practice.<br>The <i>Lechem Mishneh</i> questions why Sages did not institute the celebration of the second day of each festival in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> as well. If their desire for uniformity of observance was so great, why did they not establish a single practice in this regard as well?<br>He explains that the Sages were more reluctant to exercise their authority when they had to establish a new practice (קום ועשה) - celebrating an additional day as a festival - than when all that was necessary was to have the people refrain from the performance of a mitzvah (שב ועל תעשה), as in the case of the lulav.<br>Another concept can be derived from this halachah. The Hebrew word translated as Temple - מקדש - is used by the Rambam to refer to the entire city of Jerusalem. Thus, we can infer from the statement "there is no longer a מקדש" that this distinction is conferred on the holy city only while the Temple is standing.</i>
At present, when everyone follows a fixed calendar,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 5 of <i>Hilchot Kiddush Hachodesh</i>.</i> the matter remains as it was, and the lulav is not taken on the Sabbath in the outlying territories or in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">or in Jerusalem</i> even on the first day [of the festival].<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">when the obligation to do so is from the Torah itself.</i> [This applies] even though everyone knows the actual day of the month.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned regarding the celebration of the second day of a festival (<i>Beitzah</i> 4b; <i>Hilchot Kiddush Hachodesh</i> 5:5), according to Torah law this practice should not be followed. Nevertheless, as a mark of respect for established custom, the practice is continued.</i><br>As explained,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">in Halachah 13,</i> the reason for the prohibition of taking the lulav on the Sabbath is a decree lest one carry it four cubits in the public domain.
Whoever is obligated to fulfill [the mitzvot of] shofar and sukkah is obligated to take the lulav. Whoever is not obligated regarding shofar and sukkah is not obligated to take the lulav.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., the lulav, like the shofar and the sukkah, is a positive commandment whose observance is restricted to a specific time. Accordingly, women and slaves are under no obligation. (See Halachot 2:1 and 6:1.)</i><br>A child who knows how to shake [the lulav]<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">according to the instructions of our Sages (Rabbenu Manoach). (See Halachot 9 and 10.)</i> is obligated<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See the note on Halachah 6:1.</i> regarding the lulav by Rabbinic law, in order to train him in the performance of mitzvot.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Note the specific instructions in Halachah 8:10 regarding a child's performance of the mitzvah on the first day of the festival.</i>
It is a halachah conveyed by Moses from Mount Sinai<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., a commandment that has the status of Torah law even though there is no explicit commandment to that effect in the Torah.</i> that<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">in addition to the willow of the lulav - There is another totally different mitzvah performed with the willow.</i> - in addition to the willow of the lulav - another willow branch was taken in the Temple.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">as described in the following halachot.<br>Rashi and <i>Tosafot</i>, (<i>Sukkah</i> 43b) explain that the mitzvah of the willow branch was only performed by the priests for only they were allowed to approach the Temple altar where the branches were arranged. However, from halachah 22, it appears that the Rambam does not share this opinion.<br><i>Sukkah</i> 44a, b also mentions that outside the Temple there was a custom of established in the time of the prophets requiring the taking of the willow by all Jews. (See also <i>Hilchot Berachot</i> 11:16.)</i> A person does not fulfill his obligation with the willow branch in the lulav.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">even if after using for the lulav, he detaches it and takes it by itself (<i>Sukkah</i> 44b, <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 664:6).</i><br>The minimum requirement [to fulfill this mitzvah] is one branch with one leaf.
The Willow Branches Placed Around The Temple Altar<br>How was this mitzvah performed?<br>On each of the seven days [of the festival], branches of willows were brought<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">from Motza, a small town slightly west of Jerusalem (<i>Sukkah</i> 45a).</i> and stood upright near the altar<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">From this fact, Rashi and <i>Tosafot</i> conclude that the willows were taken by priests only because Israelites were not allowed to approach the Temple altar.</i> with their tops bent over the altar.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">These willow branches were eleven cubits high and were placed on the base of the altar.<br>Afterwards, they would be taken by the people, as stated in the following halachah. Rabbenu Manoach maintains that the priests would take the willow branches and give them to the people because Israelites were not allowed to approach the altar, as above. However, he notes that Rav Yitzchak ibn Giat's description of the mitzvah could be interpreted to mean that the Israelites themselves were permitted to approach the altar on this occasion.</i> When they would bring them and arrange them [near the altar,] a series of [shofar blasts]<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">teki'ah, teru'ah, and teki'ah - were sounded - as an expression of happiness (<i>Tosafot, Sukkah</i>, ibid.). The shofar was also sounded in connection with the communal sacrifices and other rites carried out in the Temple.</i> - <i>teki'ah, teru'ah,</i> and <i>teki'ah</i> - were sounded.<br>When the Sabbath fell in the midst of the festival, the willows would not be arranged [near the altar]<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">on the Sabbath, since the mitzvah of lulav was negated on such an occasion (Halachah 13). Indeed, in his Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Sukkah</i> 4:3), the Rambam writes that the reason the mitzvah of taking the willow on the Sabbath was negated was so that people would not extend the leniency and take the lulav as well.</i> unless the seventh day fell on the Sabbath.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The seventh day of Sukkot falls on the Sabbath only when Rosh Hashanah is celebrated on Sunday. Since the Sages attempted to prevent the latter occurrence (<i>Hilchot Kiddush Hachodesh</i> 7:7) - indeed, it is impossible according to today's fixed calendar - it was rare that the seventh day of Sukkot would fall on the Sabbath.</i> [On such an occasion,] the willows were arranged [near the altar],<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">and the willows were taken as during the week. Though the mitzvah of lulav would not be performed on such an occasion, an exception was made regarding the willows, in order...</i> to publicize the fact that [taking] them is a mitzvah.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Since the willows were placed near the altar by the priests, and the performance of the mitzvah was under the supervision of the court, there was no need to worry about people carrying willows in the public domain (<i>Sukkah</i> 43b).<br>In a related context, <i>Sukkah</i> 43b relates that the followers of Boethus, who did not respect the Oral Law, once tried to prevent the people from following the mitzvah of the willow branches on the Sabbath. Indeed, precisely because the source for the mitzvah is the oral tradition alone, the Sages made a point of allowing it to be observed on the Sabbath at least under such circumstances (Rabbenu Manoach).<br>The actual performance of the mitzvah on the Sabbath is described in the following halachah.</i>
How would they fulfill [this mitzvah on the Sabbath]?<br>They would bring [the branches] to the Temple on the Sabbath eve and place them in golden containers, so their leaves would not dry out.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Some commentaries explain that the golden vessels were used as an expression of respect for the mitzvah. However, the <i>Ma'aseh Rokeach</i> explains that, in comparison to containers made of other metals, golden ones are more beneficial in preserving the willows' freshness.</i> On the following morning, they would be placed next to the altar and the people would take them in the same manner as they did each day.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Note the commentary on the previous halachah.</i><br>Since the willow is not explicitly mentioned in the Torah,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh's</i> text of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> reads: "it is not explicitly [an obligation] from the Torah." Accordingly, he and the other commentators debated whether the Rambam considers a halachah conveyed by Moses from Mt. Sinai as a Torah obligation or not. In <i>Hilchot Tum'at Meit</i> 2:10, the Rambam specifically states that a halachah conveyed by tradition has the power of Torah law.</i> it is not taken on each of the seven days of the festival as a remembrance of the Temple.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">as is the lulav (Halachah 15).</i> Rather, at present it is taken only on the seventh day.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Hoshanah Rabbah.</i><br>What is done?<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">to fulfill the mitzvah at present?</i> One takes one<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">willow</i> branch or many branches<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">At present, it is customary to take five.</i> in addition to the willow of the lulav, and hits the ground<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Zohar</i> mentions this practice</i> or a utensil with them two or three times.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">According to Kabbalah, the custom is to hit the ground five times.</i> No blessing is recited, because this practice is a custom instituted by the prophets.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Sukkah</i> 4:3), the Rambam writes: "The willow is a halachah conveyed by Moses....It was a custom of the prophets to take it without a blessing."<br>However, in <i>Hilchot Berachot</i> 11:15, he writes:Every practice which is a custom - even if it a custom of the prophets (e.g., taking the willow on the seventh day of the festival)... - [does not require] the recitation of a blessing.It is possible to reconcile the two statements as follows: A blessing was never recited upon taking the willow in the Temple (though it could have been), because of the custom of the prophets mentioned in his Commentary on the Mishnah. There was never any reason to recite a blessing over taking the willow outside the Temple because it was only a custom.</i>
On each day of the festival,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">in the Temple</i> they<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">There is a question if this practice was carried out only by the priests, or by Israelites as well. (See the commentary on Halachah 21.)</i> would walk around the altar once, carrying their lulavim<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 43b mentions an opinion that states that the people would walk around the altar carrying the willow branch, but concludes as the Rambam does. (See also <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 664:4.)</i> in their hands, reciting: "Please, God, save us. Please, God, grant us success"<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Many manuscripts of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> state "Please, God, save us" twice, repeating that verse as is our custom in the recitation of <i>Hallel</i>.</i> [Psalms 118:25]. On the seventh day, they would walk around the altar seven times.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Yalkut Shimoni</i> notes that this recalls the miracle of the conquest of Jericho.</i><br>It has become universally accepted Jewish custom to place the ark in the center of the synagogue<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">From <i>Hilchot Tefillah</i> 11:3, it appears that in addition to the main ark of the synagogue, there was a small movable ark that was positioned in the center of the synagogue. The present custom is to hold the Torah scrolls on the reader's platform in the center of the synagogue. This is also mentioned in the <i>Yalkut Shimoni</i>: "The <i>chazan</i> stands as an angel of God, holding the Torah scroll in his arm."</i> and walk around it each day,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">once, and seven times on <i>Hoshanah Rabbah</i>.</i> as they walked around the altar in remembrance of the Temple [service].
The following custom was observed in Jerusalem:<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">as an expression of the dearness with which the people regarded the mitzvah:</i> A person would leave his house in the morning [carrying] his lulav in his hand.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 652:1) states that "the eager" fulfill the mitzvah of lulav early in the morning. Afterwards, the lulav would be carried - while open, without a carrying case - throughout the day.</i> He would enter the synagogue with it in his hand; pray while it was in his hand;<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Even though one should not ordinarily hold any objects in one's hands while praying, lest one's concentration be distracted, since holding the lulav is a mitzvah dear to a Jew's heart, it will not become a distraction.</i> go to visit the sick and comfort the mourners with it in his hand.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">for there is no difficulty in carrying the lulav while fulfilling those mitzvot.</i> When he entered the House of Study, he would send it home with his son or servant.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">We fear that a person's involvement in his studies will prevent him from showing proper attention to the lulav, and perhaps he will drop it (Rashi, <i>Sukkah</i>, ibid.).</i>
During the time the lulav was taken on the Sabbath,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachot 13 and 14. At present, it is forbidden to carry the lulav on the Sabbath (Ramah, <i>Orach Chayim</i> 558:2).</i> a woman was allowed to receive the lulav from her son or her husband<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Though she was not required to fulfill the mitzvah herself, no prohibition was instituted against her carrying the lulav.</i> and return it to the water on the Sabbath.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">This was not considered a violation of the Sabbath laws which prevent causing any agricultural growth. However, it was forbidden to add to the water or change it on the Sabbath because of the trouble involved.</i> On the festival, a person might add to the water.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">but not change it.</i> On Chol Hamo'ed,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">though there are certain restrictions against work</i> one might change the water.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Indeed, it is proper to do so to keep the lulav fresh (Rabbenu Manoach).</i>
It is forbidden to smell the myrtle in the lulav.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in Chapter 6, Halachah 15, with regard to the <i>s'chach</i> of a sukkah: after it has been set aside for use as a mitzvah, it may not be used for mundane purposes throughout the holiday.</i> Since it is useful only for smelling<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">in contrast to the etrog, mentioned in the second clause.</i> and it has been set aside for the performance of the mitzvah, it is forbidden to smell it.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The prohibition applies even on the Sabbath, when the lulav is not taken (<i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 653:2).</i> However, it is permitted to smell an etrog, because setting it aside for the mitzvah [prohibits it from being] eaten.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">An etrog is primarily used for eating. Thus, its being set aside for use for the mitzvah causes that function to be prohibited. However, smelling it is a secondary function that is not included in that prohibition.<br>Nevertheless, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 653:1) recommends refraining from smelling the etrog, because of the doubt regarding the proper blessing to recite. The <i>Mishnah Berurah</i> (653:3) states that with the exception of the time when the lulav is being taken, one may smell the etrog throughout the holiday.</i>
It is forbidden to eat the etrog<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">even if it has become unacceptable for use in performing the mitzvah (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 665:1).</i> throughout the seventh day [of the festival];<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah (<i>Sukkah</i> 4:6) relates that despite this prohibition, after the mitzvah was fulfilled on the seventh day of Sukkot the children would eat their etrogim.</i> since it was set aside for a portion of the day, it is set aside for the entire day. However, on the eighth day it is permitted to be eaten.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast, the <i>s'chach</i> of the sukkah might not be used for a mundane purpose on the eighth day either (Chapter 6, Halachah 15). Rabbenu Manoach explains the difference, relating that - should one desire to eat - the sukkah must also be used <i>beyn hash'mashot</i> - the period between sunset and the appearance of three stars - while the lulav would not be taken during that time.</i><br>At present, when we celebrate [the festivals for] two days<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">because of the previously established custom, even though there is no question regarding the dates of the holidays because of the fixed calendar we use, as explained in the commentary on Halachah 18.</i> - even though the etrog is not taken on the eighth day<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> notes the contrast between the mitzvot of lulav and of the sukkah, which, as mentioned in Chapter 6, Halachah 13, must be fulfilled on the eighth day of the festival. He differentiates between the two, noting that it is a mitzvah from the Torah to dwell in the sukkah for all seven days of the holiday. Therefore, because of the doubt, that mitzvah was also observed on the eighth day. In contrast, outside of Jerusalem there was never a mitzvah from the Torah to take the lulav for seven days. Hence, that mitzvah need not be observed on the eighth day.<br>The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> adds a further point. It is forbidden to carry the lulav for no purpose on the eighth day, the holiday of Shemini Atzeret. No such prohibition applies regarding the use of the sukkah.</i> - the etrog is forbidden on the eighth day,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">However, it is permitted on the ninth day, Simchat Torah (<i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, ibid.).<br>In contrast, at present, the use of the <i>s'chach</i> is forbidden on the ninth day.</i> since it was forbidden on the eighth day<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> advises amending the text to read "on the seventh day." In either case, the intent is the same - because it was forbidden by law on the seventh day, that prohibition was observed on the eighth day as well, because of the doubt involved.</i> during the time [the festivals] were celebrated for two days because of the doubt whether [the eighth day] was, in fact, the seventh.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., during the time when the calendar was established according to the testimony of witnesses, and word of the sanctification of the new moon could not reach the people in the outlying communities in time for the celebration of the holiday, as mentioned in the commentary on Halachah 16.</i><br>When a person sets aside seven etrogim, [one for each] of the seven days [of the festival], each one of them can be used for the mitzvah on its day and eaten on the morrow.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">more precisely, even on the night after it was used, for the etrog was set aside only for that day alone.</i>

Chapter 8

[These are the rules governing] the four species: the lulav, the myrtle, the willow, and the etrog. If one of them was:<br>a) dried out,<br>b)<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The etrog is described as פרי עץ הדר (the fruit of the beautiful tree). <i>Sukkah</i> 31a explains that an analogy is established among the various species, and all of them must be "beautiful." Fruit or branches that are dried out do not fit the latter description.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 645:5, 646:7) defines "drying out" as losing all its green and fading to a whitish color. The Ramah mentions an even more lenient opinion.</i> taken by force<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">גזל - armed robbery or the like</i> or stolen,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">גנבה - petty theft and the like. Though in other areas there are differences between these two categories of theft, in this context the same laws apply.<br>In his Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Sukkah</i> 3:1), the Rambam explains that any of the species which are stolen may not be used for the mitzvah, because a sin may not serve as the medium with which a mitzvah will be performed (מצוה הבאה בעבירה). (See also <i>Hilchot Chametz U'matzah</i> 6:7; <i>Hilchot Issurei Mizbe'ach</i> 5:7, 5:9.)<br><i>Sukkah</i> 29b-30a mentions this reason, but also a second explanation: because, as stated in Halachah 10, on the first day of the festival a person must own the four species he uses for the mitzvah. (See the commentary on Halachah 9 for a further discussion of this matter.)</i> even after [the owner had] despaired of its recovery,<br>c)<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">According to the first reason, the owner's despair over the recovery of his article has no effect on the thief's potential to use it for a mitzvah. Even according to the second opinion, the despair over recovering the article is not sufficient to allow the thief to use it, as is obvious from <i>Hilchot Gezeilah</i> 2:1, where the Rambam writes:A stolen article whose form has not changed...even though its owner has despaired of its recovery...must be returned to its owner.Thus, the article is not considered to belong to the thief, and he may not use it to fulfill the mitzvah. However, if the thief performed a deed which changed the appearance of the lulav or any of the other species, he is considered to have acquired it and may fulfill the mitzvah with it. Nevertheless, he should not recite a blessing before performing the mitzvah with such a lulav (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 649:1).</i> came from an <i>ashera</i><sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">a tree which is worshiped as a deity. It is also forbidden to be used for the mitzvah, on the basis of the principle that a sin may not serve as the medium with which a mitzvah will be performed (מצוה הבאה בעבירה) (Rambam, Commentary on the Mishnah, ibid.).</i> that has been worshiped, even though the worship of the <i>ashera</i> has already been nullified,<br>d)<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The commentaries on <i>Sukkah</i> 31b explain that this refers to an <i>ashera</i> that entered a Jew's possession before it was nullified, or an <i>ashera</i> that was worshiped by a Jew. In these instances, the nullification of the <i>ashera</i> will not cause it to be permitted for use; rather, it must be totally destroyed. Hence, it is considered to have no size at all, and, therefore, may not be used for the mitzvah (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>). (See also the Commentary on Chapter 1, Halachah 3.)<br>Alternatively, since the <i>ashera</i> itself was worshiped, it may no longer be used for a mitzvah. A parallel can be found in <i>Hilchot Tzitzit</i> 1:11, which relates that the wool of a sheep that was worshiped may not be used for tzitzit, although that wool does not become prohibited.</i> or it came from an apostate city<br>it<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">All the property of an apostate city must be destroyed. Therefore, any of the four species that come from such a city is considered to have no size at all, and, thus, is unacceptable for use in the mitzvah. (See also the commentary on Chapter 1, Halachah 3.)</i> is not acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">it is not acceptable.</i><br> If one of them belonged to an idolater:<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">but was not worshiped itself (Rabbenu Manoach); i.e., it grew in a garden of the temple of an idol. However, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 649:3) explains that it applies even when the tree itself was worshiped.</i> at the outset, it should not be taken.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Because of its connection with idol worship, such an article is considered to be disgusting, and it is improper to fulfill the mitzvah with it (Rabbenu Manoach). However...</i> If it was taken, the person has fulfilled his obligation.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">provided the tree had belonged to a gentile and the latter had nullified its connection with idol worship before it came into the Jew's possession. Alternatively, on any day of the festival but the first, one may take it even before its connection with idol worship was nullified. Since the possibility exists that it can be nullified, it is not considered to be a nonexistent entity. (See also the commentary on Chapter 1, Halachah 3.)</i><br>If [one of the species] was wilting, but had not dried out entirely, it is kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 31a explains that as long as the species have some moisture left to them, they are not disqualified for use.</i> In extreme situations or in a time of danger, a dried out lulav is kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 32b relates that the inhabitants of the large cities would bequeath their lulavim to their descendants as part of their estate. Obviously, the lulavim would have dried out during this time.</i> However, [this does not apply] to the other species.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">for only the lulav was mentioned in that passage.<br>The Ra'avad differs with the Rambam and maintains that even a dried out lulav is not acceptable. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 645:5) follows the Ra'avad's view.</i>
An etrog<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">These laws apply only to an etrog, because the agricultural prohibitions mentioned in this halachah apply only to fruit and not to mere branches (Rabbenu Manoach).</i> of <i>orlah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">For the first three years of a tree's growth, one is forbidden to benefit from its produce (Leviticus 19:23).<br>The Torah's prohibition against <i>orlah</i> applies only in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>. In the Diaspora, there is also a prohibition against <i>orlah</i>, which was received as a halachah from Moses from Mount Sinai. However, much greater leniency is involved, and if there is a doubt whether a fruit is <i>orlah</i> or not, it may be eaten (<i>Hilchot Ma'achalot Asurot</i> 10:10). The same rules apply regarding its use for this mitzvah.</i> of impure <i>terumah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Terumah</i> refers to the portion of produce which must be separated and given to a priest (Numbers 18:12). If the <i>terumah</i> becomes ritually impure, it is no longer permitted to be eaten and must be destroyed (<i>Hilchot Terumah</i> 12:1).<br>In his Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Sukkah</i> 3:8), the Rambam writes that etrogim of <i>orlah</i> and impure <i>terumah</i> are not acceptable because "they must be destroyed by burning. Therefore, they are unacceptable, because God requires 'a fruit,' and these are not fit to be eaten at all.”</i> and of <i>tevel<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">produce from <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> from which the agricultural requirements - <i>Terumah, Ma'aser Rishon</i> (the first tithe), and <i>Ma'aser Sheni</i> (the second tithe) - have not been separated. <i>Tevel</i> is also unfit to be eaten, and thus, it may not be used for the mitzvah.</i></i> is unacceptable. [An etrog] of <i>d'mai<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Produce concerning which there is doubt whether or not the tithes have been separated (<i>Hilchot Ma'aser</i>, Chapter 9).</i></i> is permitted, for it is possible for a person to declare all of his property as ownerless.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary on the Mishnah (ibid.), the Rambam states "he can consecrate all his property to the Temple." In practice, there is no difference between the two.</i> Thus, he will be a poor man who is permitted to eat <i>d'mai</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Ma'aser</i> 10:11. Since there is a possibility of his being allowed to eat the etrog, he may use it for the mitzvah (Rambam, Commentary on the Mishnah, ibid.).</i><br>An etrog of pure <i>terumah</i> and of <i>ma'aser sheni</i> in Jerusalem<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">In the first, second, fourth, and fifth years of the seven-year agricultural cycle, the second tithe had to be taken to Jerusalem and eaten there in a state of ritual purity (Deuteronomy 14:22-27).<br>The Rambam maintains that one can use an etrog of <i>ma'aser sheni</i> for the mitzvah only while in Jerusalem, since that is the only place that it is permitted to be eaten (Commentary on the Mishnah, ibid.). However, Rabbenu Nissim and others differ and allow such an etrog to be used for the mitzvah in other places as well. They maintain that though we are not allowed to benefit from <i>ma'aser sheni</i> outside Jerusalem, the fulfillment of mitzvot is not an act of personal benefit.</i> should not be taken, lest one cause it to become susceptible to contracting ritual impurity.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">which was undesirable. From Leviticus 11:34, our Sages learned that produce does not become subject to contracting ritual impurity until it comes into contact with water. Since the lulav was generally placed in water (see Chapter 7, Halachah 25), when it was taken together with the etrog it would probably make the etrog wet, and thus cause it to become subject to contracting ritual impurity.</i> However, if it was taken, it is kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">for there is no inherent difficulty with such an etrog.</i>
A lulav whose tip<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">According to the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> and Rabbenu Manoach, this refers to the center leaf that extends outward from the <i>shidrah</i>, and not the <i>shidrah</i> itself. Rabbenu Asher and the Ra'avad explain that this refers to the majority of the lulav's leaves, and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 645:6) quotes their opinion. However, the Ramah quotes the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i>'s statements.</i> becomes cut off is unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">because such a lulav is not "beautiful" (הדר: Jerusalem Talmud, <i>Sukkah</i> 3:1). All the other factors mentioned in this halachah disqualify a lulav for the same reason.</i> Should it become split to the extent that the two sides of the split become severed and appear to be two, it is unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 31b, 32a states: A lulav which is split is kosher; [but] if it is like a fork, it is not. The commentaries explain that the Talmud refers to a shape like a tuning fork, where the two ends are distant from each other. The commentaries explain that such a separation can disqualify a lulav even if the majority of leaves are not split in this manner, and hence, the principles mentioned in the following halachah would not apply.</i><br>If it is bent forward<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., the <i>shidrah</i> would be bent toward a person facing it</i> so that its <i>shidrah</i> appears like a hunchback, it is unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> (ibid.) states: If it is bent like a scythe, it is unacceptable. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> emphasizes that the bend must be severe (as described by the examples given by the Talmud and the Rambam). However, a slight curve will not disqualify a lulav.</i> If it is bent backwards,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">the shidrah bending away from a person facing it</i> it is kosher, since that is its natural pattern of growth.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">and this can be considered as a "beautiful" lulav (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 645:19). The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 645:9) differentiates between when the <i>shidrah</i> bends gradually and when it is bent over to the extent that its tip points downward. In the latter case, even if it is bent backwards, the lulav is unacceptable.</i> If it is bent toward either side, it is unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> (ibid.) questions whether such a lulav is kosher or not and leaves the matter unresolved. Hence, we follow the more stringent view (Rav Yitzchak Alfasi).</i><br>If its leaves have separated one from the other, but they have not begun to hang downward like the top of a date palm,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., the leaves are still firm and pointed upward (Rambam, Commentary on the Mishnah, <i>Sukkah</i> 3:1).</i> it is kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">However, the most proper way of performing the mitzvah is to use a lulav whose leaves are not separated at all (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).</i> However, if its leaves have burst open and they hang down from the <i>shidrah</i> as does the top of the date palm, it is unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">even if they are bound together against the lulav. This applies only if the majority of the lulav's leaves have opened up in this fashion (Ramah, <i>Orach Chayim</i> 645:2).</i>
The natural pattern of growth of the leaves of the lulav is that two grow in pairs, connected at their back. The back of each pair of connected leaves is called the <i>tiyomet</i>. If the <i>tiyomet</i> is split, it unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> explains that the Rambam maintains that the lulav is not acceptable only if the majority of the leaves are split. However, others (Rashi, <i>Tosafot</i>) explain that this law refers to the middle leaf alone. If the majority of that leaf is split, the lulav is unacceptable. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 645:3) quotes the Rambam's opinion, while the Ramah favors that of Rashi and <i>Tosafot</i>.</i> Should a lulav's leaves grow individually from the beginning of its formation without having a <i>tiyomet</i>, it is unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., there is no difference if the lulav's lack of a <i>tiyomet</i> is a natural phenomenon or is brought about through human activity; in all cases it is unacceptable.</i><br>When a lulav's leaves do not grow on top of the other like all lulavim, but rather one below the other, [the following rules apply:] If the top [of the lower leaf] reaches the base of the one above it so that the entire <i>shidrah</i> of the lulav is covered with leaves, it is kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">This is the <i>tzinei har habarzel</i> mentioned in the Mishnah (<i>Sukkah</i> 3:1). <i>Sukkah</i> 32b relates that such lulavim grow at the entrance to Gehinnom.</i> If the top [of the lower leaf] does not reach the base of the one above it, it is unacceptable.
A myrtle branch whose top is cut off is acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Since the myrtle branch is covered by its leaves, the fact that its top is cut off is not noticeable (Rabbenu Manoach).<br>Though the Mishnah (<i>Sukkah</i> 3:2) states that such a myrtle is unacceptable, the Talmud (<i>Sukkah</i> 34b) states that the halachah follows Rabbi Tarfon, whose opinion is quoted by the Rambam in this halachah.<br>In his commentary on this halachah, the Ra'avad states:For a number of years, the spirit of prophecy has been present in our chamber of study, and we have determined that [such a myrtle] is not acceptable.The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 646:10) quotes the Rambam's opinion. However, the Ramah writes that it is proper to adhere to the Ra'avad's view if possible. However, he quotes Rabbenu Nissim, who maintains that the top of a myrtle is only considered to be "cut off" when the top of the branch is broken. The leaves' falling off is not considered of significance.</i> Even though most of its leaves have fallen off, it is kosher, provided three leaves remain in one row.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> explains that this refers to an Egyptian myrtle, which has seven leaves in each row. Rabbenu Manoach explains that this clause refers to the entire myrtle branch, stating that even though most of its leaves have fallen off, as long as it has one full row of leaves covering the branch (at its top, as in Chapter 7, Halachah 8), it is kosher.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 646:4) quotes the <i>Maggid Mishneh's</i> statements as halachah. In contrast, Rabbenu Manoach's interpretation is not accepted. Halachah 5 (ibid.) states that the majority of the length of the myrtle branch must be covered with leaves.
