However, if that condition is met, the myrtle is kosher even if the leaves at its top have fallen off.</i><br>When there are more berries than leaves, [the following rules apply:] If they are green, it is kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">for their color is the same as the leaves of the myrtle.</i> If they are red or black,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">since the berries are a different color from that of the leaves...</i> it is not acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">since this is not considered to be "beautiful" (Rashi, <i>Sukkah</i> 33b).</i> If one reduced their number,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">so that there would be more leaves than berries,</i> it is acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">it is acceptable.</i><br> We may not reduce their number<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., the number of black or red berries</i> on the holiday itself,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., on the first day of the festival, when the restrictions against work apply</i> because [by doing so, one] makes [the myrtle] fit for use.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">and this may not be done on a holiday. (See <i>Hilchot Sh'vitat Yom Tov</i> 4:8.) However...</i> If one transgressed and removed them or removed them one by one to eat them,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">which is permitted. <i>Sukkah</i> 33b states that permission is granted, provided only that one has another myrtle to use for the mitzvah. Otherwise, it is forbidden to eat the berries, because through one's activity one will definitely make the myrtle fit for use. Hence, even though one's act was motivated by another intention as well, it is forbidden. (See <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 646:13.)<br>The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> quotes a slightly different version of the text, substituting "or if they were removed by another person" for the phrase "removed them one by one." Authentic Yemenite manuscripts of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> follow the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i>'s text.</i> it is kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">despite the fact that it was made fit for use on the holiday.</i>
A willow branch whose top is cut off is kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam maintains that the laws pertaining to the willow parallel those governing a myrtle. However, even many of the Sages who accept the Rambam's opinion regarding the myrtle disagree with regard to the willow. They explain that such a myrtle is acceptable because its leaves cover the branch's severed top. This concept does not apply with regard to a willow. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 647:2) also states that such a willow is unacceptable.</i> If its leaves have burst open,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., they are hanging down limply from the branch (<i>Maggid Mishneh)</i></i> it is not acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">for this is not beautiful. Rabbenu Aharon Halevi interprets the Hebrew נפרצו as "split." The Shulchan Aruch HaRav 647:6 quotes both of these opinions as halachah.</i>
If an etrog is perforated<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">even though none of the etrog's substance is missing</i> from side to side<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">This is the literal translation of the term מפולש. Nevertheless, Rabbenu Asher interprets that term differently, explaining that the etrog is unacceptable if the hole reaches the etrog's seed chamber. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 648:3) quotes both opinions. Regarding <i>halachah l'ma'aseh</i>, the authorities recommend heeding Rabbenu Asher's opinion. However, they state that when it is difficult to find a kosher etrog, the Rambam's opinion may be relied upon.</i> - no matter how small the hole is<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., even if it was perforated with a thin needle</i> - it is not acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This and the other factors mentioned in this halachah disqualify an etrog for use because it is not "beautiful."<br>The Ra'avad objects to this statement and maintains that some of the etrog's substance must also be lacking for it to be deemed unacceptable. The difference between these two opinions depends on a difference in the text of the Mishnah (<i>Sukkah</i> 3:6). The Rambam's text reads:If [an etrog] was perforated, peeled, cracked, or lacking even the slightest amount of its substance, it is unacceptable.In contrast, the Ra'avad's text read:If [an etrog] was perforated, peeled, cracked: when it lacks even the slightest amount of its substance, it is unacceptable.The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (ibid. 2) follows the Rambam's view. The Ramah writes that when it is difficult to find another kosher etrog, one may rely on the Ra'avad's view.</i> When [the hole] does not go from side to side, if it is the size of an <i>isar</i><sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">a silver coin from the Talmudic period, four barley corns in size (Rambam, Commentary on the Mishnah, <i>Sukkah</i> 3:6).</i> or more,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">e.g., the hole was made by plunging an awl into the etrog (Rashi, <i>Sukkah</i> 36a).</i> [the etrog] is not acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Here, too, the Ra'avad differs and maintains that as long as none of the etrog's substance is lacking, it is kosher.</i> If [a hole was made in an etrog which caused] even the slightest amount [of the etrog] to be missing, [the etrog] is not acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad differs and maintains that an <i>isar</i> of the etrog's substance must be missing. In this and in the previous case, the same decisions of the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> and the Ramah mentioned above apply.</i><br>If its <i>pitam</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., the small protrusion from which its flower grows - The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> differentiates between the stem of the <i>pitam</i> and its tip from which its flower grew, maintaining that only the removal of the former disqualifies the etrog for use. The Ramah (ibid. 7) quotes this opinion as halachah. However, he suggests trying to use an etrog whose <i>pitam</i> is entirely complete.</i> - i.e., the small protrusion from which its flower grows - was removed, it is not acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">However, there is no difficulty in using an etrog that grows without a <i>pitam</i>, as many etrogim do (Rabbenu Manoach; Ramah, ibid.).</i> [Similarly,] if the stem from which it hangs from the tree is removed from the etrog itself and a hole is left, it is not acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">However, if only part of the stem is cut off and the portion attached to the etrog remains, it is kosher (Ramah, ibid. 8).</i><br>If it becomes covered with bumps<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The term חזזית is generally used to refer to human skin ailments - e.g., boils or warts. In this context, it refers to bumps which protrude above the etrog's surface. However, the commentaries disqualify an etrog only if the bumps grow naturally from the etrog itself. However, if they are caused by external factors - e.g., thorns - the etrog is not disqualified.</i> in two or three places,<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad asks why both the numbers two and three are mentioned. In response, the <i>Mishneh Lamelech</i> quotes many examples of the use of similar terminology throughout the Talmud. It must be noted that the Rambam, in his commentary on the Mishnah (ibid.), and the <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> (648:19) mention only two places.</i> it is not acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">because it looks spotty (<i>Sukkah</i> 35b).</i> Even if it is covered with bumps in only one place,<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., as long as there is no place for an additional bump between the two, it is considered to be one place (<i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i>, ibid.).</i> if that place covers the majority of the etrog's surface area,<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Magen Avraham</i> 648:13 also disqualifies an etrog if there is a line of bumps that covers the majority of the etrog's circumference from any point on its surface.<br><b>it is not acceptable. [Similarly,] if a bump grows on even the slightest portion of the pitam -</b> See the Rambam's Commentary on the Mishnah, ibid.<br><b>it is not acceptable -</b> because a blemish on the <i>pitam</i> is very obvious and unattractive.<br><i>Sukkah</i> (ibid.) states: "if a bump grows on the etrog's nose, it is not acceptable." The Rambam interprets "nose" as referring to the <i>pitam</i>. However, Rabbenu Asher and others explain that it refers to the portion of the etrog which begins to narrow as it approaches the <i>pitam</i>. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (ibid. 9) accepts the latter view.<br><b>If its peel is removed without causing [the etrog] to lose any substance and it remains greenish yellow as in its natural state -</b> i.e, only the outer coating of the peel was removed and the thick, white inner peel is not yet revealed. Should this peel be revealed, the etrog is considered as though it has lost some of its substance (Rambam, Commentary on the Mishnah, ibid.).<br><b>[the following rules apply:] If the peel was entirely removed, it is not acceptable. -</b> for the attractiveness of the etrog will begin to decrease without any possibility of its returning to its original state (<i>Levush</i> 586:6)<br><b>If even the slightest portion of the original peel remains -</b> the peel could have regenerated itself if it had remained connected to the tree (<i>Levush</i>, ibid.). Therefore,...<br><b>it is kosher. -</b> <i>Tosafot, Sukkah</i> 36a maintains that at least a portion of the original peel equivalent to a <i>sela</i> (a large silver coin) must remain. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (ibid. 6) follows the Rambam's opinion.</i> it is not acceptable. [Similarly,] if a bump grows on even the slightest portion of the <i>pitam</i>, it is not acceptable.<br>If its peel is removed without causing [the etrog] to lose any substance and it remains greenish yellow as in its natural state, [the following rules apply:] If the peel was entirely removed, it is not acceptable. If even the slightest portion of the original peel remains, it is kosher.
An etrog which is inflated,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., water fell on it after being detached from the tree and it became inflated (Rabbenu Manoach, <i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 648:15).</i> decaying,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., it produces a foul odor because it is infested with worms (Rabbenu Manoach).<br>Based on <i>Sukkah</i> 36b, the <i>Ba'al Halachot Gedolot</i> writes that "inflated" and "decaying" etrogim are disqualified only when the blemish is externally visible. However, if the blemish is only internal, they are kosher. (See <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (ibid. 4) and commentaries.)</i> pickled,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (ibid. 15) defines "pickled" as "placed in vinegar." However, the <i>Magen Avraham</i> (648:22) maintains that "pickled" should be interpreted according to its definition in the <i>Shulchan Aruch, Yoreh De'ah</i> (105:1-2). Thus, if an etrog has been soaked in water or in any other liquid for more than 24 hours straight, it is considered to be "pickled" and disqualified for use. One should take care regarding this matter when soaking etrogim to preserve their freshness.</i> cooked,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">thoroughly on a fire or in boiling water (Rabbenu Manoach).</i> black, white, spotted,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., a mixture of any two or more colors. When the different color is concentrated in only one place, the etrog is not disqualified unless that color covers more than half the etrog's surface area. However, if there is more than one spot of different colors, it is disqualified regardless of their size (<i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, ibid. 16).</i> or green like a leek is unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The Hebrew word ירוק refers to two different colors: dark green and yellow. Initially, the etrog, like most citrus fruit, is dark green. As it matures, it turns yellowish.</i><br>If it was grown in a mold and shaped into the form of another creation, it is unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">It is not considered "beautiful" because its natural form was changed (Rabbenu Manoach).</i> If its natural form was preserved, even though it was shaped in different layers, it is kosher. Two etrogim that grow joined together,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">This is kosher because the etrogim grew naturally in this way. In this instance, when performing the mitzvah, a person should hold both etrogim in his hands. Though the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (ibid. 20) accepts this decision, there are other authorities who differ. Hence, it is preferable to use another etrog (<i>Mishnah Berurah</i> 648:63).</i> and an unripe etrog are kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 31b mentions etrogim which are small and green as being not fully ripe. However, since the Rambam mentioned those factors previously, it appears that here the intent is different and refers to a fruit that is not yet ready to be eaten, even though it is as large as an egg and has begun to turn yellow. (See also Sukkah 36a.)</i><br>In places where the etrogim grow naturally with a slight black tinge, it is kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi (<i>Sukkah</i> 36a) states that if such an etrog is brought to a place where etrogim of a normal color grow, it may not be used.</i> However, if [the etrogim] are very black<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., like a Kushite - they are unacceptable everywhere - even in Africa, where etrogim of this color grow naturally (Rashi, <i>Sukkah</i> 34b).</i> - i.e., like a Kushite - they are unacceptable everywhere.
All the species which we categorized as unacceptable because of the blemishes we described<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 36b relates that Rabbi Chanina would eat from an etrog and then use it for the mitzvah. Though an etrog which is lacking even the slightest amount of its substance may not be used (Halachah 7), he still fulfilled his obligation with it. From this incident, our Sages concluded that, on the later days, even such an etrog is kosher.<br>According to the Rambam's opinion, the same applies to all other physical blemishes on the species. Since there is no greater blemish than an etrog which is bitten into, none of the requirements for "beauty" must be observed on the later days.<br>Nevertheless, <i>Tosafot, Sukkah</i> 29b and Rabbenu Asher maintain that this leniency applies only to etrogim which are lacking in substance. All the species which were disqualified because they are not "beautiful" may not be used on the later days as well. Though the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> 649:5 quotes the Rambam, the <i>Magen Avraham</i> (649:17) and the <i>Taz</i> (649:9) quote the other view.</i> or because they were stolen or taken by force<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Pri Megadim</i> explains that even according to the Rambam, a blessing should not be recited when performing the mitzvah in this fashion.</i> are [disqualified for use] only on the first day of the festival.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in the commentary on Halachah 1, there are two explanations why a stolen lulav may not be used on the first day:<br>a) a sin may not serve as the medium with which a mitzvah will be performed (מצוה הבאה בעבירה);<br>b) as stated in Halachah 10, on the first day of the festival a person must own the four species he uses for the mitzvah.<br>The commentaries explain that the Rambam follows the latter rationale. However, those who follow the first opinion (the Ra'avad, the Ramban, Rabbenu Asher) forbid the use of stolen species on the later days as well. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (ibid.) quotes the Rambam, while the Ramah follows the other view.<br>[Though the above explanation is frequently used, it is difficult to accept. First, in his Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Sukkah</i> 3:1), the Rambam mentions the reason of מצוה הבאה בעבירה. Also, in <i>Hilchot Chametz U'matzah</i> 6:7 and in <i>Hilchot Issurei Mizbe'ach</i> 5:7 and 5:9, he forbids the use of stolen articles for the performance of a commandment. In addition, he has not yet mentioned the halachah requiring one to own the lulav used on the first day.<br>The <i>Taz</i> (649:15) clarifies the matter slightly further by explaining that since taking the lulav on the second day is only a Rabbinic commandment, using a stolen lulav is allowed even though it is a מצוה הבאה בעבירה.]</i> On the second day of the festival<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The literal translation of the Rambam's words are "from the second holiday," implying that these leniencies are granted even in the Diaspora, where the second day is celebrated as a holiday. Though all the laws of the holiday apply on the second day as well as the first, this applies only to the laws of the holiday itself. In contrast, in other contexts, since we follow a fixed calendar and know that the holidays fall on their appropriate dates, the restrictions applying to the first day do not apply on the subsequent days (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).<br>Rabbenu Asher differs and maintains that all the restrictions that apply to the lulav on the first day must also be observed on the second day as well. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 649:5) writes that all the species disqualified on the first day may be taken on the second day, but a blessing should not be recited.</i> and on the other days,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Chol Hamo'ed</i>, when all opinions agree that...</i> they are all kosher.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">they are all kosher.</i><br> Those which are disqualified because of the association with idol worship<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">mentioned in Halachah 1</i> or because the etrog is forbidden to be eaten<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">as mentioned in Halachah 2</i> are unacceptable both on the first day and on the later days.
On the first day of the festival,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">It appears from the Rambam's statements that even in Jerusalem, where Torah law requires that the mitzvah be fulfilled for all seven days of the festival, the obligation to own the lulav applies only on the first day.</i> a person cannot fulfill his obligation by using a lulav that belongs to a colleague and was borrowed from him,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the commentary on the previous halachah, the Biblical source for the mitzvah of taking the four species, Leviticus 23:40, states: "On the first day, take for yourself the fruit of a beautiful tree..." The phrase "for yourself" implies that a person must own the species he uses for the mitzvah (<i>Sukkah</i> 41b).<br>As mentioned in Chapter 5, Halachah 25, a borrowed sukkah may be used on the holiday. Though Deuteronomy 16:13 states "Celebrate the Sukkot holiday for yourself for seven days." In this context, only a stolen sukkah is disqualified and a borrowed sukkah is permitted (<i>Sukkah</i> 27b).<br>Since in both cases, the source for the exclusion is the same phrase, one might ask why the laws pertaining to each are different. Many explain that since, as explained in the commentary to that halachah, the Torah includes a special verse to teach us that a borrowed sukkah is permitted, the scope of the exclusion implied by "for yourself" is limited.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch HaRav</i> 637:3 explains that since the person owning the sukkah grants his colleague the use of it, the borrowed sukkah can be considered "as his own." Since a person has to treat a sukkah as his permanent dwelling for the seven days of the holiday, it follows that the sukkah was lent for that purpose. Thus, while a person is using it, he may consider it "as his own," i.e., just like his own dwelling. See <i>Likkutei Sichot</i>, Vol. 19.</i> unless the latter gives it to him as a present.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Though there are certain restrictions against the transfer of property on a holiday, it is permitted to give a colleague a present (Rambam, Commentary on the Mishnah, <i>Sukkah</i> 3:11).</i><br>If [the owner of the lulav] gives it to him as a present on the condition<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The use of the Hebrew term על מנת frees one from certain obligations in phrasing the terms of the conditional agreement. (See <i>Hilchot Zechiyah Umatanah</i> 3:8.)</i> that he return it, he may fulfill his obligation with it and return it,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">There are authorities who require that the owner specifically state that he is giving the lulav to his colleague on the condition that the latter return it. However, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 658:5) states that even if the owner gives a colleague a lulav without making such a statement, it is assumed that the lulav was given subject to this condition.</i> because a present given on condition that it be returned is considered a present.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Zechiyah Umatanah</i> 3:9.</i> If he does not return it, he does not fulfill his obligation, because it is as though it were stolen.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., by failing to fulfill the condition under which the present was given, the recipient nullifies the legal transfer of ownership. Thus, although he was in physical possession of the article, it did not belong to him. Hence, it is considered to be stolen and, thus, not eligible to be used for the mitzvah (<i>Sukkah</i>, ibid.).</i><br>[On the first day,]<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">before all the adults who desire to use it have fulfilled the mitzvah (<i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, ibid. 6)</i> a lulav should not be given to a minor,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The definition of the term "minor" in this context has been the subject of debate among the Rabbis. From the Rambam's phraseology, it appears that he includes all minors in this category. However, Rabbenu Nissim writes that a child of six or seven can transfer property and, hence, his return of the lulav is valid.<br>Support for Rabbenu Nissim's position is brought from the Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Sukkah</i> 3:10), which relates that Rav Nachman bar Ya'akov gave his etrog as a present to his son and told him: "When you take possession of it and fulfill the mitzvah, return it to me." Nevertheless, the passage does not serve as conclusive proof, because the possibility exists that his son had already reached majority. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (ibid. 6) follows the Rambam's view.</i> since, according to Torah law, a minor can acquire articles<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">which are given to him by others who consciously desire that the minor acquire the property, in contrast to ownerless articles, which he cannot acquire according to Torah law (<i>Maggid Mishneh, Hilchot Zechiyah Umatanah</i> 4:7).</i> but cannot transfer them to others.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The transfer of the ownership of an article requires a conscious decision. Torah law maintains that a child lacks intellectual maturity (דעת), and thus is incapable of making such a decision.</i> Thus, [the minor's] return of the article is not considered to be a return [from a legal perspective].<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., although he has physically returned the article, the child still remains the legal owner.<br>The <i>Machaneh Ephraim</i> (<i>Hilchot Meshichah</i> 2) notes that according to Rabbinic law, a child can transfer property that he owns to another. Thus, the Rambam's statements seem to imply that any of the four species that are acquired only according to Rabbinic law may not be used for this mitzvah.<br>A very practical point can be derived from this concept. According to Torah law, property that is purchased changes ownership only after it has been paid for. The completion of a transaction when the property are taken by the purchaser (<i>meshichah</i>) is a Rabbinic institution. Accordingly, if a person purchased a lulav and etrog on the condition that he pay for it after the holiday - even though the seller willingly consented - the purchaser has only acquired the lulav and etrog according to Rabbinic law. Thus, based on this halachah, he would not be able to use it for the mitzvah.</i><br>The above applies to the lulav and to each of the other species of the four taken with it.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">The verse quoted above includes all the four species, and not only the lulav. Therefore...</i> If one of them was borrowed, the person does not fulfill his obligation on the first day of the festival.
When partners purchase a lulav or etrog together, neither is<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">considered to be the owner of the species in its entirety. Therefore, neither is...</i> able to fulfill his obligation with it on the first [day of the festival]<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Batra</i> 137b relates that the requirement of owning a lulav includes not only having a share in it, but rather owning it entirely.</i> unless his colleague gives him his portion as a present.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The principles mentioned in the previous halachah regarding giving a lulav as a present also apply to giving a share of it as a present.<br>The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> mentions a very frequent application of this concept: a lulav and etrog purchased by a synagogue for the use of all of its members. He quotes the Rashba, who explains that since the etrog was purchased with the intent that it be used by each member of that community, implicit in their agreement is that, on the first day, it will belong to each individual entirely at the time he uses it to fulfill the mitzvah.<br>The Ramah (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 648:7) quotes this statement as halachah. However, the <i>Magen Avraham</i> 648:10 raises questions concerning it and suggests that each member of the community should grant his colleague his share as a present.</i><br>Should brothers purchase etrogim from the funds [of their father's] estate, which they have not divided yet:<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This is the case mentioned in <i>Bava Batra</i> (ibid.), from which the above principle is derived. In such an instance, the estate is considered to be the mutual property of all the brothers concerned.</i> If one of them takes an etrog with the intent of fulfilling his obligation, [the following rules apply:] If he could eat it without the other brothers objecting,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">it is considered to be his own. Hence,...</i> he has fulfilled his obligation. If they would object,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">and require that an equal division of the property be made before it was used, he cannot be considered to be the full owner of the etrog. Hence,...</i> he does not fulfill his obligation until they give him their share [in the etrog] as a present.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The above principles apply only when the funds of the estate were used to purchase a number of etrogim. Since many fruits of the same species were purchased with the money from the estate, it is possible that the other brothers will not object to one brother's taking an etrog for his own use. However, if the money of the estate was used to buy a number of different species of fruit - e.g. ….</i><br>If one brother bought an etrog and another a quince, or together they bought an etrog, a pomegranate, and a quince from the funds [of their father's] estate, which they have not divided yet,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">we assume that the brothers would desire to have the property formally divided before using it. Hence, they are all considered to be mutual owners of the fruit. Thus,...</i> one cannot fulfill one's obligation with the etrog until the others give him their share [in it] as a present, even though they would not object to his [use of it].
Even though it is a mitzvah to rejoice on all the festivals,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">as Deuteronomy 16:14 teaches: "And you shall rejoice on your festivals." Though that verse is mentioned with regard to Sukkot, Deuteronomy 16:11 states with regard to Shavuot: "and you shall rejoice before God," and <i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 4b explains that an analogy is established to include Pesach as well.</i> there was an additional celebration in the Temple on the festival of Sukkot,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's words present a question: The Mishnah (<i>Sukkah</i> 5:1) refers to this celebration as <i>Simchat Beit Hasho'evah</i>, connecting it with the drawing of water for the water libation. (See <i>Hilchot T'midim Umusafim</i> 10:6-10.) Indeed, the Talmud (<i>Sukkah</i> 50b; see also the Rambam's commentary on the above-mentioned Mishnah) emphasizes that connection, quoting Isaiah 12:3: "And you shall draw water with happiness." However, here, the Rambam makes no mention of that water at all!<br>We are forced to say that the Rambam views the verse from Isaiah as a mere <i>asmachtah</i> (an allusion from the Bible with which our Sages connected a verse to an independent concept) and that the celebration came about because of the unique nature of the Sukkot festival. Though this celebration was associated with the water libation, the latter is not the source for the practice. Indeed, the choice of the name <i>Simchat Beit HaSho'evah</i> (the celebration of the house of drawing the water), and not <i>Simchat Hasho'evah</i> (the celebration of the drawing of the water) allows for such an interpretation. (<i>Likkutei Sichot</i>, Vol. XVII).</i> as [Leviticus 23:40] commands: "And you shall rejoice before God, your Lord,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., in the Temple</i> for seven days."<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Although this verse is used to derive the requirement of taking the lulav in Jerusalem for each of the seven days of the festival (Chapter 7, Halachah 13), its simple meaning remains.<br>Nevertheless, this charge is not considered to be an independent commandment, but rather an extension (and an intensification) of the mitzvah of celebrating on the festivals. Thus, in <i>Sefer Hamitzvot</i> (Positive Commandment 54), the Rambam describes that mitzvah and explains that it includes "to celebrate with musical instruments and to dance in the Temple....This is <i>Simchat Beit Hasho'evah</i>."<br>This explanation resolves another question: <i>Mo'ed Kattan</i> 8b teaches that one celebration should not be interposed upon another. For this reason, weddings are not held during the festivals so that the wedding celebrations should not clash with those of the festival.<br>Thus, were one to consider the celebration of <i>Simchat Beit Hasho'evah</i> as associated with the water offering, one might ask why the Sages instituted such a celebration which might appear to overshadow the celebration of the festival itself. However, the above explanation resolves this difficulty as well, for as stated above, the <i>Simchat Beit Hasho'evah</i> celebration is an extension of the festival celebrations and not an independent matter. (See <i>Likkutei Sichot</i>, ibid.)</i><br>What was done? On the eve of the first day of the festival,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Two reasons are given why the courtyard was not set up during the festival itself:<br>a) It involved construction, which is forbidden on Chol Hamo'ed (<i>Knesset Hagedolah</i>)<br>b) Preparing the courtyard before the festival would allow the celebrations to begin immediately after the departure of the festival (<i>Kinat Eliyahu</i>).</i> they would set up a place in the Temple<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">in the open courtyard before the entrance to the Temple courtyard proper. This was called <i>Ezrat Nashim</i> - the women's courtyard - because in contrast to the Temple courtyard, women were allowed to enter the <i>Ezrat Nashim</i> even when they were not offering sacrifices. The Rambam describes the <i>Ezrat Nashim</i> in <i>Hilchot Beit Habechirah</i> 5:7.</i> where women [could watch] from above, and men from below, so they would not intermingle with each other.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Originally, the men and the women would stand in separate sections on the same level. However, the Sages feared that, particularly during a time of celebration, such closeness might lead to frivolous interaction between the sexes, and decided to have a balcony constructed for the women (<i>Sukkah</i> 51b).</i><br>The celebration would begin on the night after the first day of the festival.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Since, as explained in the following halachah, the celebration was not held on the first night of the festival.</i> Similarly, on each day of Chol Hamo'ed, after offering the daily afternoon sacrifice, they would begin to celebrate for the rest of the day and throughout the night.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 53a quotes Rabbi Yehoshua ben Chananyah as saying: "While we were celebrating at <i>Simchat Beit Hasho'evah</i>, our eyes saw no sleep."</i>
What was the nature of this celebration?<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah (<i>Sukkah</i> 5:1-2) states:Whoever has not seen <i>Simchat Beit Hasho'evah</i> has never seen rejoicing in his life!...<br>There were golden candelabras....There was not a courtyard in Jerusalem that was not illuminated with the light of <i>Beit Hasho'evah</i>.<br>The pious and men of stature would dance before them with torches of fire in their hands and recite songs of praise. The Levites would play the harps, lutes, cymbals, and all other types of instruments on the steps...leading down from the Israelites' courtyard.</i> The flute would be sounded<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Indeed, from the Mishnah (<i>Sukkah</i> 5:1), which states: "The flute, for five or six days; this was the flute of <i>Beit Hasho'evah</i>," it appears that this was the major element of the celebration.</i> and songs played on the harp, lute, and cymbals. [In addition,] each person would play on the instrument which he knew. Those who could sing, would sing.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See the quote from the Mishnah above.</i> They would dance and clap their hands, letting loose and whistling, each individual in the manner which he knew. Words of song and praise were recited.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Note the following halachah and commentary.</i><br>This celebration does not supersede either the Sabbath or the festival [prohibitions].<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Since, as explained above, the celebration involved musical instruments, it would not be held on the first night of the festival (or on the Sabbath), when playing such instruments is forbidden (<i>Hilchot Shabbat</i> 23:4).<br>The celebration is an extension of a Torah commandment, while the above prohibition is only Rabbinic in nature. Furthermore, it was held in the Temple, where Rabbinic prohibitions of this nature were usually suspended (אין שבות במקדש, <i>Pesachim</i> 65a). Nevertheless, since there is no specific Torah obligation to celebrate in this manner, the celebrations were suspended because of the Rabbinic prohibition (<i>Likkutei Sichot</i>, ibid.).</i>
It is a great mitzvah to maximize this celebration.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">for the experience of genuine Torah happiness is a fundamental and necessary element of our service of God, as explained in the following halachah.</i> The common people and anyone who desired would not perform [in these celebrations];<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Though they would attend, as mentioned in the final clause of the halachah</i> only the greatest of Israel's wise men: the <i>Rashei Yeshivot</i>, the members of the high court, the pious, the elders, and the men of stature.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">for it was only their celebration that could inspire the people with spiritual feeling.</i> They were those who would dance, clap their hands, sing, and rejoice in the Temple on the days of the festival of Sukkot.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkah</i> 53a relates how Hillel the Elder would rejoice at <i>Simchat Beit Hasho'evah</i>. Other Sages would also participate. For example, Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel would juggle eight torches of fire and bow down so low he could kiss the ground. The Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Sukkah</i> 5:3) relates how Rabbi Shimon ben Yehotzedek would perform unique feats of jumping.</i> However, the entire people<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">the men and the women - would come to see and hear - deriving joy from this passive participation.</i> - the men and the women - would come to see and hear.
The happiness with which a person should rejoice in the fulfillment of the mitzvot and the love of God who commanded them is a great service.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">By mentioning this concept at the conclusion - but as part - of the these halachot, the Rambam emphasizes how the celebration of the festivals is not just an isolated experience, but rather is intended to influence the totality of our service of God. The rejoicing of the festival of Sukkot enables us to appreciate true happiness in all aspects of our Torah service.</i> Whoever holds himself back from this rejoicing is worthy of retribution, as [Deuteronomy 28:47] states: "...because you did not serve God, your Lord, with happiness and a glad<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">This verse comes after the full description of the curses and retribution which God will visit upon the Jewish people for their sins. From the Rambam's statements, it appears that it is the lack of happiness in the service of God, and not the sins themselves, which brought about this punishment.<br>The <i>Ari</i>, Rabbi Yitzchak Luria, explains that even though the punishment comes for the sins, had the Jewish people served God with true joy and happiness, that happiness would have caused Him to overlook those transgressions, regardless of how serious they were. In contrast, a lack of happiness in the performance of the mitzvot demonstrates a deficiency in the person's awareness of the connection to God established thereby.</i> heart."<br>Whoever holds himself proud, giving himself honor, and acts haughtily in such situations is a sinner and a fool.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">for he is concerned with himself, rather than with God.</i> Concerning this, Solomon warned [Proverbs 28:10]: "Do not seek glory before the King."<br>[In<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., God, in whose presence we are at every moment.</i> contrast,] anyone who lowers himself and thinks lightly of his person in these situations<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">transcending his self-consciousness and committing himself to God totally, without any restrictions</i> is [truly] a great person, worthy of honor,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">his ability to open himself up to God allows him to serve as a medium for the expression of His greatness, and thus, his own personal prestige rises, because, as our Sages commented: "A king's servant is like a king."</i> who serves God out of love.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Teshuvah</i>, Chapter 10.</i> Thus, David, King of Israel,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The mention of David's position further emphasizes the concept explained. Alternatively, a king has a connection to each of his subjects. Thus, mentioning the example of the king of Israel emphasizes how each Jew has a potential to achieve this rung of service.</i> declared [II Samuel 6:22]: "I will hold myself even more lightly esteemed than this and be humble in my eyes," because there is no greatness or honor other than celebrating before God, as [II Samuel 6:16] states: "King David was dancing wildly and whistling before God."<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">When Michal, Saul's daughter, witnessed David's recklessness and total lack of inhibitions, she reproved him for conduct unbefitting a king. David answered her sharply, explaining that it is precisely this ability to give oneself totally over to Godliness which characterizes a Jewish monarch and makes him fit to lead the people in the service of God.<br>Though the Rambam stresses how an approach of humility and happiness befits people of honor - as is obvious from the nature of the Sages' celebration of <i>Simchat Beit Hasho'evah</i> - these ideas also apply to every Jew. When an individual is conscious of God's constant presence, he will naturally be infused with these two emotions. He will feel his own smallness in God's presence, yet he will also feel real joy at the knowledge that God is with him at every moment, and that through his service of Torah and mitzvot, he can develop a greater connection with Him.<br>May our service of God with joy hasten the time when "crowned with eternal joy" (Isaiah 35:10 , we will be able to "rejoice before God, your Lord, for seven days," in the Messianic Temple. May we merit it, speedily, in our days.</i>
